The Red Page
Verses by Fritz Hart
The latest volumes of “The Wisdom of the
East” series (Murray; 2s.) includes The
Way of Contentment, translated from the
Japanese of Ivaibara Ekken, a 27th century
philosopher, who advises his disciples on
many tilings concerning the soul and the
body. In the latter department: “If you
have a fefeling of giddiness during the night
move your big to^s; by thus doing, you will
bring on a fit of yawning, during which the
giddiness will pass off.” Another volume is
Ancient Egyptian Legends, which contains a
cheerful tale entitled “The Beer of Heli-
opolis,” which this critic regrets that he has
never tasted.
“In a moment his arms were around her,”
we are told beneath the frontispiece of In
Facing Silk, by Gerald It. Baldwin (N.S.W.
Bookstall). But the artist is more re-
strained. He shows us that only one arm
was around her, and that that arm had gone
only half-way. And at the end, “His only
answer was to draw her to him and again
kiss her on the lips.” In between, there are
200 pages of racing, intrigue and girl. What
more does the Australian reader want for
his Bookstall shilling?
THE HILLS.
Crouched against the sky,
The hills like mammoths lie
Asleep! Spent is the rage
Of ilieir forgotten age.
JSiow rest they deep
In an eternal sleep!
The curving line
Of monstrous spine—
'The aspect grim
Of mighty limb,
Are grass-clad now ;
And leafy bough,
A nd golden bloom
Of burning broom.
The sleeping monsters dress
In robes of loveliness.
Their strength alone remains;
And we, from peaceful plains,
Look up with wonder; half-afraid
To see them stir in sleep—and swayed
By awe of what past ages knew,
Ere time her monstrous offspring slew!
AT MIDNIGHT.
Vague, trembling and uncertain, on the flow
Of midnight stream the poplar-shadows fa"-
A mystic thrill of silence ( musical —
Though noiseless as the stars that would
forego
Their place in Heaven, and, mirrored, shine
below)
Fills full and holds the heart of Love in
thrall: ,
Obliterating fears that did appal,
Till moonlit midnight caused their over-
throw.
Grave, tender, loving eyes of perfect faith.
More exquisitely deep than summer night
By faery stream;- you gazed in mine, and
made
It crystal-clear—spite what the preacher
saith —
All is not vain: and that our earthly plight
Is Heaven-blest, so we walk unafraid!
Open, your window and gaze at file morn,
A nd —listen !
A lark sings of jewels—night's tear-drops
forlorn —
That glisten !
Open pour door and step into the day,
See—under
The lee of the hedgerow — webs, silver and
gray;
And wonder!
Open your heart to my love; and to spring's
Endeavor ;
Full-throated and clear is our song, and it
rings
For ever!
From “Appassionata,” by Fritz Hart.
MORNING SONG.
Welcome a new Australian poet—a minor
bard but melodious. In Appassionato-, Songs
of Youth and Love (Lothian, Melb.; 3s. 6d.),
Fritz Hart collects some 80 little songs and
sonnets, slight, pleasing and admirably
wrought. He sings for the joy of singing,
but he is technically well equipped. His
theme is young love: —
Since you have loved me, Love, so well —
Why not a little better? >
Why not have kept the spirit-truth,
As you have kept the letter?...,
0, crimson heart of Ijove for which I seek
By night and day beneath the world's gray
skies —
0, budding mouth, and brimming, fevent
eyes —
Deep-bosomed Love —when may I lay my
cheek
For heart-ease on thine own? that I may
speak
The words incapable of ecstasies:
But whose high crystal-summit thou’lt sur-
mise,
As men through mountain mist divine the
peak ....
A month ago —a week — nay, less —a day —
And Love danced in those eyes that now
portray
The calculating mind of one who knows
And weighs the merits of a judged rose, —
Nor perfume, glow, nor hushed beauty
seeks,
But some faint flaw —then critically speaks!
A day ago! How fete the hours now sped
Since Love was living—Love that now is
dead!
All very young, but very delightfully
young. But Fritz Hart is in love not only
with Love, but with verse. He decks his
stanzas with fastidious taste in word and
phrase. Likely enough he will not go far,
enough for us that he has so buoyantly set
forth.
The origin of Lite
How did life begin? Why did it begin?
Or did it begin? Benjamin Moore, professor
of Bio-Chemistry at Liverpool University, in
The Origin and Nature of Life (Home Uni-
versity Library ; Is. 3d. in Australasia), re-
writes Genesis for the modern scientific stu-
dent. He begins far back, with electrons and
atoms, goes thence to chemical evolution in
the suns, inquires about the building materials
for living matter on the earth, treats of
the evolution of colloids—that bridge between
the inorganic and the organic—speculates
how life arose on earth, and considers the
living organism at work.
There is no breach of continuity between
the inorganic and the organic, or any need
for the postulation of an abrupt act of crea-
tion of the organic from the inorganic at
some definite past moment in cosmic history
which has never been repeated.
Exit Genesis.
But the evolution of life from , inorganic
materials is only one more stage more mys-
terious than, the evolution of any one form
of matter from another. The riddle of the
universe lies much deeper than life. The
riddle of the universe is the universe: why
there is matter and energy at all, and gen-
erally why things happen, and why
there happened to be things that could
happen, and what would have hag-
pened if there hadn’t. The scientist
stands now on the verge of the discovery that
all the elements are derived from one form
of stuff only, which may not be matter itself,
but when tenanted, or dossed in. or haunted,
by something called energy, becomes con-
verted into forms of matter leading up to the
elements first unearthed by the scientist. So,
in investigating the genesis of life we must
go back to the birth of electrons and atoms.
The good old doctrines of the conservation of
matter, and of the conservation of energy,
which assert that neither matter nor energy
can be created or destroyed, must now be
hurled overboard. We are now told that
each chemical element represents a moVe or
less stable position in the charging of matter
With energy. Instead of matter and energy
being fixed in amount and always conserved,
they are mutually convertible into each
other. Then is there any place in the uni-
verse where energy is forming matter out of
the alleged ether? Yes, in the hottest suns
and in gaseous nebula?. Here, in all proba-
bility, matter is to-day being created, which
may ultimately lead up to the John Smith of
millions of centuries ahead. And the study
of radio-activity has disclosed intermediate
stages between the chemical atom and the
unformed pre-matter, or luminiferous ether
—whatever that is. The alpha and beta,
particles of radio-activity form the initial
stages in the synthesis of matter which
reaches/up ultimately to you
These primeval beginnings of the transmu-
tations of matter and energy, with the for-
mation of the proto-elements, are illustrated
by the present stages of the developments of
w-rMc, pcj seen bv the astronomer, in the
constellations of all ages which surround
this world of ours.
Jt
Despite the high cost of living and labor
troubles, Nature is still at her old job
The stellar evidence, of the synthesis of
matter and the creation of worlds leaves the
position entirely untenable that life came by
a single creative unrelated act upon this
earth, or on any other earth from which it
was carried to ours. Life is at present orig-
inating in countless other worlds as daily it
is upon our own ; and, given a
certain state in evolution when matter has
reached a certain complexity in structure,
and becomes tenanted by certain types of
energy, life must come, and having come must
evolve into higher and higher forms.
Coming a long stage further, all life on
the earth depends upon two simple factors:
(1) the presence of a mere trace of carbon-
dioxide in the air, and (2) the natural power
of the carbon atom contained in the carbon-
dioxide of entering into energy relations
with fellow carbon atoms. But an energy-
transformer is needed, and this is found in
the green plant-cell and its green-coloring
matter, chlorophyll. And the energy which
is by this machine converted into chemical
energy of organic compounds is simply sun-
light. But, of course, life had an immense
amount of experimentation to go through
before it managed to produce so complex a
thing as a green plant-cell. The colloids fill
the gap. All living structures consist of
colloids. And the investigation of colloids,
begun only 50 years ago, has been prosecuted
with vigor only during the last decade. This
is the most promising of all the modern
spheres of research into the problem of life.
The author devotes to colloids a brilliant
chapter.
J*
If a mental picture be conjured up of a
world in which there is as yet no life, but
where conditions are suitable for life to
appear, it is evident that a spontaneous pro-
duction of such a thing as even a bacterium
or other unicellular organism would by no
means solve the problem : the new-born cell
would have no organic pabulum, and must
perish. The production of anything so com-
plex as chlorophyll at such a stage is un-
thinkable to anyone acquainted with the
I subtle continuity of all nature. In such a
.World inorganic colloids must first develops,
and in time one of these must begin to
evolve, not a living cell, not anything so coni"
plex as a micro-coccus or a bacillus, not even
a complex protein, carbohydrate, or fat, but
some quite simple form of organic molecule,
holding a higher store of chemical energy
than the simple bodies from which it was
formed. To carry out such a function, the
inorganic colloid must possess the property
of transforming sunlight, or some other form
of radiant energy, into chemical energy.
Later, such simple organic compounds, by
the agency of the same or some other colloid,
and with a supply of external energy, would
begin to condense and form more complex
organic molecules, and finally complexes of
inorganic and organic matter would come
into existence as crystallo-colloids. In this
way, without any hiatus life would be led up
to and inaugurated.
You see how simple the whole matter is 1
But Moore states that the search for such an
inorganic transformer is not without a hope-
ful outlook. Selenium, formaldehyde and
other substances are suggested. But how
did inorganic matter —the indispensable foun-
dation upon which organic life had to be
built —emerge? Yet as soon as the tempera-
ture of the earth allowed, sufficient complexity
of chemical structure for life to be borne on
the earth, then life appeared. The sponta-
neous production of life lies far below the
level of bacteria ; it lies at a level possessing
a lower unit than the living cell. There we
must, perforce, leave the problem.
It was no fortuitous combination of
chances, and no cosmic dust, which brought
life to the womb of our ancient mother earth
in the far distant Palaeozoic ages, but a well-
regulated orderly development, which comes
to every mother earth in the Universe in the
maturity of her creation when the conditions
arrive within the suitable limits. Given the
presence of matter and energy forms under
the proper conditions, life must come inevi-
tably.... The beginning of life was not a
fortuitous event occurring millions of years
ago. and never again repeated, but one which
in its primordial stages keeps on repeating
itself all the time and in our generation. So
that, if all intelligent creatures were by some
holocaust destroyed, up out of the depths in
I process of millions of years intelligent beings
would once more emerge.
So that if the defeat of the Labor party,
or the invasion of the atmosphere by some
gas poisonous to all life, or some other horrible
world-catastrophe wiped us all out, in a few r
millions of years inorganic colloids would
becomes stable, bacilli would arrive, the
pterodactyl and the aeroplane would event-
uate, and new Napoleons, O’Dowds, O’Mal-
leys, grocers, Kaisers and “Bellerives” would
ravage the new virgin earth. The prospect
is comforting.
Novels
Oliver Onions has written a sequel to his
fine psychological study, In Accordance with
the Evidence. This story, it may be re-
membered. told of the murder of a “rotter”
of a London clerk by his fellow clerk, his
rival in love. The Dehit Account (Bell, per
Dyinock’s Library) is an equally sincere
piece of psychology. And, though a sequel,
the book is in itself complete. Taking up
the story four years after the carefully
planned murder, which the coroner, in
accordance with the evidence, brought in as
a case of suicide for sufficient reasons,
Onions tells of the marriage of the capable
murderer with the unsuspecting girl, of his
rapid rise to wealth, of the confession he
made to another girl who loved him, and
of the growing necessity he felt of the need,
for his soul’s sake, of a complete confession
to his wife. The character drawing is vivid
and realistic, and there are scenes, such as
that of the night walk the murderer took
with the girl who suspected, that are packed
with the utmost pathos and irony. The
deyelopniebt of the man’s instinct to confess
is done with remarkable sureness of touch.
Oliver Onions shows a continual upward
development in his patient art. Ultimately
he must be taken notice of; but, so far, the
reading public has not discovered him.
&
WO% is the curious title of a good sea-
yarn by Maurice Drake (Methuen), the
author of The Salving of a Derelict and
Wrack. It is a scientific mystery- and
adventure-story. The title is the chemical
formula for wolframite or tungsten dioxide,
a deposit of which is discovered in the shape
of mud in Holland. A small company is
engaged in secretly shipping the valuable
stuff, worth £240 a ton, to England, under
the pretence that it is ballast; for the pub-
lication of the news of such immense de-
posits would ruin the market. A' rival
German company appears; and there is plot
and counter-plot, adventure and love-making.
The heroine is a suffragette, delightfully
drawn. When she finds that even a suf-
fragette can be caught by love, she exclaims:
“This love —this awful love ! There seems
no escape. Haven’t I seen the hateful thing
everywhere? In the laboratory, watched
through the microscope the rushing together
of tiniest live atoms —shameless little beasts!
Haven’t 1 1 seen how all the scheme of life
was tangled no with it, till even I was almost
afraid. Afraid, I tell you ! Blown pollen in
the moving grasses—bees in the flowers—
cell seeking cell everywhere—all telling the
same thing....l wouldn’t love; I swore I
wouldn’t. There is so much to be done for
women, and they themselves won’t •do it.
They’ll never do it. They can’t.* They swear
to devote themselves to the cause, and—and
then a man lays hands on them, and where
are their vows? A man holds them and
kisses them, and all’s over.... Oh, the
shame of it! Here am I, crying on a man’s
shoulder like any housemaid.... And I
wouldn’t be anywhere else foif worlds!
That’s- the worst of it....A1l I can spare
the cause is a fit of crying and some regrets.
Oh, I’m a Judas!.... But I’ll have my 30
pieces of silver. Kiss me on the nose again.
You dear.”
Here is Jeffery Farnol again, with The
Amateur Gentleman (Low. Marsden, per
George Robertson, Sydney), “in which
(Chapter One) Barnabas knocks down his
father, though as dutifully as may be,” and
“in which (Chapter Five) the historian sees
fit to introduce a lady of quality ; and, fur-
ther, relates how Barnabas tore a wonderful
bottle-green coat,” and (Chapter Six) “ 0 f
the bewitchment of black eyelashes; and of
a fateful lace handkerchief,” and much more
including this of the lady of quality : ’
“Motionless stood Barnabas with eyes
that wandered from the small polished rid-
ing-boot with its delicately spurred heel, to
follow’ the gracious line that swelled volup-
tuously from knee to rounded hip, that sank
in sweetly to a slender waist, yet rose again
to the rounded beauty of her bosom.”
Adventures,.fights, horses, persons of qual-
ity, clothes, deliberately quaint “vulgar”
characters, and girl—-all in the time of Beau
Nash: 599 pages....and what more, does
the library subscriber want? Farnol is well
set on the broad highway of popularity.
This paper has been entertaining
children unaware. “Patricia Murchison,”
the pen-name" of the author of the
charming place-poem, “Ivarela Hill,”
which came only second to “Mathin-
na,” in the recent competition, is just 16
years old. At the age of 12 she wrote a
poem which, after keeping for two years, she
sent to The Bulletin. The Editor paid
her 8s. Gd. for it, and published it last year.
It is quite a nice poem. Altogether this
paper, of course quite ignorant of the youth
of its correspondent, has blithely accepted
and paid for nine of her verses, and in the
issue that contained “Ivarela Hill” two other
verses by her appeared. She affects a variety
of pen-names ; but has decided henceforth to
be true to “Patricia Murchison.” The Aus-
tralian youngster has already been offered a
position in journalism in Melbourne.
The Round Table for March (Aust.
Agent, Macmillan, 109 Ilussell-street, Melb..
2s. 6d.), contains the most succinct state-
ment of British policy and sea-power yet
enunciated. This admirable quarterly is
-worth buying, and especially worth filing, by
all who desire an unbiassed setting-forth of
Imperial problems. For the scientific,
Science Progress (Murray, 5s. quarterly)
affords varied technical up-to-date pabulum.
But for the literary there is no other maga-
zine in the English-speaking world which
has the virility, the daring, and the literary
standard of the monthlv English Review
(Is.).
THE OLD RED ROAD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There are myriad lights on the great white
road,
And a voice that bids me stay.
And the hoofs ring on where the white
stones glance,
And the wheels sing on where the white
lights dance
When the face of the world grows gray;
But my heart beats back to the old, red road
A hundred miles away.
Oh, my feet are set in the great icliite road,
And my face is turned that way;
But when white lamps burn, and the roar
has ceased.
In mg dreams I turn to the twilit East,
And I tread on the rough, red clay.
Oh, the dusk falls soft on the old. red road
A hundred miles away.
Oh, my feet are set in the great white road,
And I march while it is day;
Yet T backward look, when the march is slow,
For the road I took in the long-ago
Is behind. And I may not stay;
But my wilful heart mourns the old, red road
A hundred miles away.
There are myriad lights on the great white
road.
And a voice that calls me — “Btay!”
But the inland breeze whispers down to me
With the sound of trees and a melody
That the breeze and the grasses play,
When the dusk comes down on the old, red
road
A hundred miles away.
PATRICIA MURCHISON.

The Proprietors of Kriischen Salts, of Manchester,

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Mimosa”: John Cooke, the meat exporter, in an
interview recently in Maoriland, stated that Queens-
land beef had deteriorated, and he cited in proof the
steady fall in the average weight of carcases. He
attributes this to the failure of Queensland cattle-
men to pay the price the Argentine estancieros are
offering for the best-bred bulls. Cooke is not the
only person who begins to have a poor opinion of
our cattle. Writer has just received a letter from
the leading shorthorn breeder in S’Africa. Inter alia
he states: “We import only the best Cruickshank
shorthorns we can get in Great Britain... .To be
candid, Australia is about the last place I would
think of importing a shorthorn from. That country
never yet imported an outstanding shorthorn; if so,
there is no record of it. 1 have seen a large number
of photos, of winning shorthorns at Australian shows,
including those of , and though they are the
best Australia has, I would not breed from them.”
Not pleasant comment; but the steady slump in the
weight of the carcases put through the works seems
to prove that it is just.
* * * *
“E.R.”: Australia’s Director of Fisheries has
been opening some enticing prospects to trawlers.
He has recently been exploring the Great Australian
Bight, and in his last trip caught fish with a trawl
at the rate of 3041 b. an hour. Reckoning the value
at 4d. per lb., and working, say, 16 hours a day, this
would mean £BO. The expenses per day would be
£lO or £l2. Two days’ steaming from Adelaide
would bring one to the fishing grounds, which is
marvellous in consideration of the fact that trawlers
from Hull and Grimsby (Eng.) go close on 2000
miles to the fishing-grounds, and the fishing-grounds
in the Bight are, mile for mile, as rich as those in
the North Sea.
• • • *
“Quarter Single”: The Prahran (Melb.) garbage
destructor, which has superseded the frowsy and fear-
some corporation tip, would have served Ruskin as
a text for a new “Ethics of the Dust.” It devours
daily 29 tons of mixed garbage, and returns to the
local council nearly £lOOO a year, amassed by the
sale of paving and building substances, of which
its dust and ashes form the raw material. The old
tip was a pestilence; the destructor is a mere brick
chimney in the midst of lawns. The firemen who
shovel the rubbish into the furnaces make queer
discoveries. Empty bottles are their perquisites as
a matter of right; but they find also stray articles
of jewellery, spoons, forks and cutlery, an occa-
sional coin, and it is on record that a dead monkey
and portion of a human leg were among the treasure
trove. The leg was believed to be that of a woman,
portion of whose anatomy had been sent to a doctor
for clinical purposes. Had it not been discovered,
it might have been worked up into asphalt, or formed
part of the rough-cast fronting a suburban villa resi-
dence. The monkey was boiled away to dust.
* * * *
“Gouger”: I’ll tell you what “blind-stabbing” is.
First you find your creek, then carry picks, shovels,
streaming boxes, hammer, drills, gelignite, and the
few other accessories of the tin scratcher’s business.
Step into the cold, muddy water, until it reaches just
over your belt, then bend down and scrape and
root at the rocks under water with the pick. Pre-
sently you strike a stone, weighing half a ton or so.
All efforts to dislodge it with the pick being un-
availing, you get the hammer and drill and proceed
to bore a hole in it. That accomplished, sooner or
later (generally later), you proceed to put in the
gelignite, then the fuse, and light it. After that
you run for the nearest tree, where you remain until
the conclusion of the submarine volcano. Back in the
water, you take the shovel and proceed to scrape
up all the dirt you have dislodged. An amateur will
mostly bring up a spoonful at a time. Then you
clean the miserable little heap of resurrected muck,
and get from 2jlb. to 41b. of tin out of it for the
day’s work. Tin is worth fully Is. Id. per lb. It’s
a great profession.

w ) len the Customs Department proposes to discover
that packages of imported shoddy are being pushed
under its nose as shoddy, and sold to the Australian
public as genuine goods. For instance, packages of
socks or undervests are branded on the outside cover
as .< cottc# and woo l,” while the article inside is
branded “all wool.” There’s a line of men’s socks
branded thus, and being sold to the retailer at 6s. od.
a doz _ Allowing warehouse charges, duty, etc., those
articles cost about 3d. a pair in England. Dunno
wba t the Commerce Act has to say about this, but I
think it is something fairly severe. When the
swindle is brought before the Courts in a year o"
ten I’ll let you know.

Ll*ou : I wish the Minister for Home Affairs
would pass a Doorstep Saving bill. Mine is getting
worn out. As a housewife ot two years standing, i
testily that in that period I have spent two
weeks at the door telling various assorted bores that
J don t want to buy their idiotic wares, or sample
them, or enter into any hire-purchase contract with
anybody that I don t gainble to the extent ot a six-
penny lottery ticket, and that 1 m not in a mood to
help build churches, or dogs dormitories. Personally
1 am badly handicapped by the 1 otter having turned
me out of His mould without enough aggressiveness
to give any caller the hump or cold feet. The door-
step destroyers may be classified — (a) Commerce,
(b) Charity. Against the brass front and ' brass
throat of class a I hold out as long as I can. Then,
if they won’t believe I am penniless and can’t buy (it
never occurs to them I don’t want to buy, no matter
how 1 protest), I take down the old worn purse I
keep behind the door for their benefit, and show them
my all, two half-pennies and a penny, or two pennies
and a half, as the case may be. Then, if they wont
sell me the grand piano or whatever it is for that,
they have to get. Occasionally they sell me other
goods to the value of (they say) Is. or Is. 6d. for
the stock of coppers. And then I have to replenish
the purse. Class b is harder to deal with. It is
always horribly long-winded, greasily pious and ser-
vile, and, if female, consistently dowdy. An obstinate
Salv’armist drove me to invent a method of dealing
with Charity. (Recipe for same appended for benefit
of suffering sisters.) She (the Army lass) was so
moved I thought she’d weep all over the new front
doormat, and I could smell the steak burning in the
kitchen. So now I always keep a few leaves of mv
husband’s cheque-book on hand (I have no bank ac-
count of my own), and, if the collector won’t go
quietly, I simply fill in one for a generous amount,
s j gn m y Christian name in full, and give it to the
gra teful cadger with my blessing. It works like a
c harm. The caller departs with gracious brevity, and
; reburrl to work experiencing all the blesssdness of
having given in abundance,

“Kobbi”: It tickles my third rib when I see the
advts. of the seedsmen stating that only this season s
seeds are stocked. To force this alleged fact on to
my dull attention he shows me with smiling pride
that each packet of seeds is marked in glaring red
the date 1918. He protests that all of last years
unsold stock is sent back to headquarters and de
stroyed. And when I plant the wretched seeds
they won’t grow, and then drop in on the seedsman
to point out that each packet contains a plain packet
inside—unmarked, of course—and that to tool the
public only the outside cover need fl b ® n r ™° a V dLtrov er
fresh one substituted, he votes me a bo ™\ a des ‘ rd ' e "
of . peace, and an obvious enemy of Private Enter
prise.

Brisbane, April 8, 1913.

Report of the Directors of
The National Bank of Australasia, Limited.
The Balance-sheet of the Bank as on 31st March last, and the Profit and Loss Account for the Half-
year which then terminated, are now submitted to the shareholders.
The net profit, after deduction of all expenses, including, £8250 16s. for note, incopie and land
Amounted to . . .. .. .. .. •• .. .. .. .. £98,632 13 3
Add balance brought forward from previous half-j'ear . . . . . . . . 14,412 18 3 j
£113,045 11 6
During the Half-year the branches in-Wictoria at Melton, Thoona and Wangerr.p were converted
into agencies, and the agency at Lavers' Hill established as a branch. The agencies at Myrniong
and Lindenow were closed. In South Australia an agency was opened at Prospect.
To the great regret of his colleagues and the management, the Honorable .1. M. Pratt found it
necessary, owing to ill-health, to resign his seat onthe Board, and it is desired to place on record the
high appreciation in which the honorable gentleman’s services as a director of the bank for nearly 22
years are held. His wide experience and sound judgment were at all times of great benefit to the
institution.
The vacancy on the Board thus caused was filled, in accordance with the authority conferred on the
directors by the articles of association,, by the appointment of J. Newman Barker, Esq., well known in
Australian business circles as late chief inspector' of the New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency
Company, Limited, and now an Australian director of that company.
At the Ordinary General Annual Meeting, to be held at the Board room of the Bank, Collins Street,
■ Melbourne, on Wednesday, 7th May, at noon, the shareholders will be called upon to elect two directors
ij n lieu of Hugh Murrav Strachan and .1. Newman Barker, Esquires, who retire by rotation, in accord-
ance with the articles of association, and are eligible for re-election. There are no other candidates.
The shareholders will also be Called upon to elect two auditors for the ensuing year.
By order of the Board of Directors.
E. 11. WREFORD, Chief Manager.
The National Bank of Australasia, Limited, ~
Head Office, Melbourne, 24th April, 1913.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On the Parramatta.
lis June in England, and England’s June
Is richer by one girl-rose in you ;
Yet, somehow, the days sing out of tune
And the sunsets burn with a sober hue
Since you said “Good-bye” to the Parra-
matta.
I saw a face in the street to-day
So like your face that it made me start,
And all of a sudden the noon turned grey,
For it set me thinking of you, Dear Heart.
How strange the power of this soul of ours
That holds one chained in a busy street,
And sets him thinking of songs and flowers —
Long dead, long muted —but still, how
sweet!
I, think of you, not as you loved to dress,
In silks nnd satins and laces rare, -
And jewels—a diamond cross (no less)
Arid a hat that hid too much of your hair.
No. no, I think of you, dear, as you sat,
A laughing girl on a boat’s mid-seat,
With tossed hair under a prisoning hat,
And round, bare arms in the sun and fhe
heat,
By a terraced shore of the Parramatta.
I’d paint you just as you were that day,
If I had the artist-gift that I need,
And men would look at my work and say-
“He dipped his brush in his heart indeed.”
How still it was on that afternoon!
The busy winds were asleep, I think ;
The river iay in a golden swoon.
A blue crane dreaming upon its brink.
For one such hour it was good to live,
And three such hours were our lot sub-
lime ;
It lay with Nature —the power to give
A fitting close to that glorious time.
<
She gave it: flooding the world with gold,
And painting the landscape—reach and
rise—
Till the hour seemed fuller than it could
%' hold
(Of glory poured from the western skies.
What spilt wine reddened the rounded cove?
Why rose the music from feathered
throats?
Whence flowed the purple and gold above?
What ailed the sails of the fishing boats?
The far clouds flamed with a fire intense
Till, mute and breathless, we gazed at
last
Enslaved bv beauty in soul and sense
Yet soon, Dear Heart.... to soon it
passed—
That sunset hour on the Parramatta.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
The Sweater Girl.
The sweater girl is featly drest,
Her curves are full and fair,
The simple hat that suits her, best
Is soft about Jier hair,
The modest ro midness of her breast
A queen had loved to wear.
When Mabel, in her cosy pride
And simple ribbon bright,
Sits out beside the rippling tide
And all the stars are white,
I know that she is warm inside
However cold the night.
No sharpest tooth of biting breeze
May pierce her warm heart through,
She shivers not for all that seas
And winter airs can do,
For soft wool holds her by the knees
*- And wraps her from the dew.
We sit beside a watchful tree,
And none may work us harm ;
I know not how she looks on me,
But both her lips are warm,
And I can feel about me free
The royndness of her arm.
The keen airs blowing down the‘bay
Are very good to taste,
The lighted steamers glide away
With decency and haste—
I never knew until to-day
The wonder of her waist. ' '
The sweater girl is featly drest,
Her curves are fair to see,
With winter’s chilly fingers prest
She goes erect and free,
The modest roundness of her breast
Is melody to me.
N.S.W.
CURSE O' MOSES.
Old Letters.
There are just two dozen of them, and
they’re nothing much to see,
For their writing’s rather scrawly and their
spelling somewhat free,
But there’s still a touch of heartache in
their faded lines for me.
Their diction’s hardly lofty, they are phrased
in language plain,
Though in some there throbs a passion, and
in some there sobs a nain,
But they all were writ in longing, and they
all were penned in vain.
And some are dark with anger, and some
are bright with wit,
For Molly’s mind was Irish, and Irish fan-
cies lit
The page where rests the tear-stain, more
dear because of it.
And, spite of blot or blemish, or aught that
V. may occur,
U hold those crumpled pages more Choice
than rose or mvrrh,
Or pearls of poet’s rapture, because they
came from her.
And though the world holds laughter and
many voices call,
And hope with clear lips smiling stands
beckoning and tall,
One bunch of faded letters is more to me
than all.
N.S.W.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
Per Annum . . . . f 1 G 0

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-
Six Shillings Sterling you can have The Bulletin
posted regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques. Drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements. etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
£ pedal Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Corn spondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse. Short
Tates or Sketches (those dealing with Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded with-
out stamps for return it will be understood that the
copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Personal,
applications for return of MS. cannot receive atten-
tion. The Bulletin desires, further, those sending
MS. to distinctly understand that it is not respon-
sible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash, of
sufficient merit and interest and adapted for direct
reproduction, or any interesting photograph illus-
trative of a current event, will be paid for same on
acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea, but
unfitted for direct reproduction -— i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under ivhich such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS. ■ -

THURSDAY, MAY 8, 1913.
THE TRANSCONTINENTAL RAIL-
WAY AGAIN.
Many a bad cause is kept afloat for a long
while by the dull, plodding reiteration of a
very ordinary lie. There need not be any
evidence behind the lie —in fact, it is often
better that there should be none. The
bogus character of false evidence can be
found out, but the bogus character of no
evidence at all is harder to prove. Thus
the old Egyptian preacher in Kingsley’s
book told Philammon, the monk, to bellow
at Hypatia, the heathen philosopher girl,
and call her bad names, but on no account
to argue with her. In a poor cause it is
always safest to assert, to declare, to lay
down assurances with a thud, to yell, de-
nounce, blither, excommunicate and hit the
table hard. And it is in this s<>rt of fashion
that the case for the crooked and devious
railway across Australia is usually sup-
ported as against the straight line through
the heart of the Continent, though now and
then there is a disastrous attempt to prop
up the case with evidence. So far, the great
effort of the Brisbane and Sydney Tory
papers is to drag the transcontinental rail-
way—which will almost cease to be a trans-
continental railway in such a case —close
up to their own doors. The Adelaide papers
want, in like fashion, to bring it to their
own doors. The unanimous conviction of
these organs that its own door is the right
door raises a natural suspicion of self-
interest. It recalls the Federal capital scrim-
mage, when each Federal member in N.S.W.
was bursting with unbiassed conviction that
the proposed site in his electorate was the
only site that any reasonable man could con-
sider. Just about here The Bulletin en-
deavors to stand in a category by itself. It
seeks to flock separately, so to speak. Its
own front door is in Sydney, and the said
door is quite as clean and respectable as
that of the S. M. Herald or Telegraph or
Evening News. But this paper tries (o re-
gard all Australia as its door, and doesn’t
feel bound to swear that the bit of brown
wood down in the front stomach of its build-
ing is the centre of all things. Because of
this effort to be Australian it advocates the
straight railway across the Australian con-
tinent. On the same principle it couldn’t
bring itself, as the Sydney dailies mostly
did, to swear that Canberra was the ideal
Australian capital, though among the various
sites proposed that one was nearest to its
place'off business and its bit of freehold.
On the transcontinental railway subject
the S. M. Herald broke out the other day
in a vigorous fashion. For instance, it
clamored to this effect: —
It is important to attempt to get this Northern
Territory of ours into its proper perspective. It has
an area of 525,000 square miles, or nearly one-fifth
of the Commonwealth, and one-third larger than
New South Wales. At present it is, to all intents and
purposes, uninhabited. Now, even if no additional
disadvantages present themselves, of which we are
not yet aware, it is going to take many millions of
money even moderately to settle the Northern Ter-
ritory. There; is no use going in for half-measures.
South Australia did that, and failed in consequence.
How terrible was her failure only those in that State
realise. Not only did they place a millstone round
their State Treasurer’s neck, but piled up a debt
that would have comnletelv strangled them financially
had not the Commonwealth come to a timely rescue.
They had their two little lines of railway ending
nowhere in the desert. The line from Port Darwin
to Pine Creek took on the average two passengers
per train, which ran every second day, one day down
and the other up, through 150 miles of anthills and
spinifex. At the other end, from Hergott Springs
to Oodnadatta, the position was, if anything, still
worse, for it did not carry on the average a single
passenger per day. We want no repetition of this
kind of thing.
* * * *
For some reason or another this proposi-
tion that S.A. was a ruined place because
of the Territory, and that the Common-
wealth took over its biggest liability out of
charity, is one of the stock arguments in
favor of the crooked railway, though it isn’t
easy to see where it comes in. Certainly
S.A. took the management of the Territory
when the less patriotic or less enterprising
State of N.S.W. refused to be responsible
for it any longer. In a burst of fresh young
enthusiasm and gilded ambition, it pre-
vented -mbre than one-sixth of Australia
becoming a nigger-infested Crown Colony,
probably controlled from Calcutta, and by
so doing it made White Australia possible.
Certainly it didn’t, during its comparatively
short term of control, do very much more
for the Territory than N.S.W. did during its
much longer occupancy —the occupancy which
ended in the final collapse and surrender by
the Ma State. Yet S.A. built a bit of railway,
while N.S.W. built nothing. Furthermore,
it must be admitted that S.A., during its
comparatively brief administration of the
Territory, had just about as poor luck in
the matter of settlement as N.S.W. is
having to-dav. and has always had, with its
own North-West. And S.A. certainly got
into debt—the Territory’s debt alone, for
which S.A. was responsible, ran to over
£3,000,000. Still, the Crow-Eater’s Province
always paid its way, and even before it got
rid of the Northern “incubus,” as the Tory
press is so fond of calling that great asset,
it wasn’t so deeply involved as either
Queensland or Westralia. So it is an absurd
lie to sav that it was “strangled financially,”
unless it is claimed that these two States
were strangled also. The Territory, until
the Commonwealth took it over, had an an-
nual deficit; but that is the normal condition
of Northern Westralia, of Western Queens-
land, of all N.S.W. west of the Darling, and
very often of whole States. There was
one sorrowful year, not so very long since,
when the only States' which hadn’t a deficit
were Westralia and South Australia (with
its Northern Territory incubus). The Terri-
tory’s railway certainly didn’t pay, and
doesn’t pay even now, but that is the normal
condition of more of the N.S.W. railways
than one cares to remember. Some people
might reckon that when a N.S.W. railway,
located close to the city of Sydney, earns
£10.451 in a year, and spends £10,435 on
working costs, and is liable for £14,116 in
interest, the case is so bad that it is unwise
to say too much about anybody else’s bit of
iron road. And it is doubtless true, as
alleged in the S. M. Herald’s plea for the
crooked railway, that the Territory will cost
a heap of money before it is adequately
settled. All Australia cost a heap of money
in the work of settlement. So, for that mat-
ter. did England and the globe generally. In
fact, many people think it would have been
a lot of money in Adam’s pocket if he had
never been created.
Again, to quote the S. M. Herald, still
clamoring for the line that will take the
shortest possible route to its own ill-painted
office-door: —
In South Australia it was desired by a large sec-
tion of the people to stipulate for the junction of the
Oodnadatta-Pine Creek line right through the dead
heart of the continent. But it was realised that
this was not a matter to be left to the desires of any
particular State, for the interests of all should be
considered, seeing that all would be called upon to
pay. An alternative route crosses into Queensland, in
the neighborhood of the Herbert River, where it can
be tapped by the Townsville-Cloncurry, the Roclc-
hampton-Longreach, and further south by the Bris-
bane-Charleville lines. It will then cross into South
Australia, in the vicinity of Birdsville, where it
could be linked up to the New South Wales system
by a line from Bourke, and possibly by another
from Broken Hill, joining the main South Australian
system somewhere about Hergott Springs. The pre-
sent commission will report upon these two schemes.
The latter, as Mr. Harper pointed out in his evi-
dence last week, is the longer bv nearly 300 miles,
but it has the advantage of bringing Port Darwin
just on 800 miles nearer to Sydney, and 1500 miles
nearer to Brisbane, as well as somewhat nearer to
Melbourne, the three great centres of population from
which troops' would undoubtedly have to be drawn
in case of trouble. Its strategical advantages are
thus great.
After all, it is a question whether, when the
general interests of Australia are concerned,
the alleged “dead heart of the continent” is
anything like as dead as the heart of the
S. M. Herald. It is quite possible that
Harper, above quoted, felt called upon, as
a N.S.W. official, to give a kind of guarded
support to the long coastal line which would
connect Port Darwin with the S. M. Her-
ald’s office at Sydney by a rather’ shorter
route than the transcontinental track would
do. But he didn’t say, as the Herald seems
to think, that the crooked line via the Her-
ald office, would bring’ Melbourne nearer to
Darwin than the straight route via Ade-
laide. In fact, he showed that the route via
Adelaide (2379 miles) would be about 40
miles shorter than the one via the Herald
office (2419 miles). This is surely the kind
of misrepresentation that a pious paper
shouldn’t be guilty of.
Also, the suggestion that it is essential to
connect the “empty North” by the shortest
possible route with Brisbane, Sydney and
Melbourne, because these are “the three
great centres of population from which
troops would undoubtedly have to be drawn
in case of trouble,” for the defence of the
Territory, is another thing that a holy pub-
lication shouldn’t be guilty of, no matter how
urgent the desire to get the great Federal
railway brought to its own city. Adelaide
is a much greater centre of population than
Brisbane—at last census it had 189,646
people against Brisbane’s 139,480. It is
growing faster, its increase inside ten years
being 27,385 against Brisbane’s 20,052.
This growth may not be to Adelaide’s credit,
but if it is a question of “three great centres
of population” from which to draw soldiers
for the defence of the North, then Adelaide
and not Brisbane is the place to be con-
sidered. Furthermore, Adelaide is nearer
to Darwin, the great port of the Territory,
than Brisbane. It is much nearer to the
most adjacent point of the Territory than
Brisbane. And when the Territory is really
in danger Adelaide will have troops to spare
while Brisbane won’t, and Melbourne may
have troops to spare when Sydney has none.
When the ill-guarded North is menaced it
must be by sea, and when the enemy is in
possession of Torres Straits he will be so
near to Townsville, Rockhampton and Bris-
bane that these places will be fully occupied
with their own affairs. Adelaide, Melbourne
and Perth, being the last, capital cities on
the mainland that an Asiatic invader could
reach by sea. might have men to spare, and
they might do good service if there was a
railway by which they could shove their re-
inforcements swiftly into the North ; but the
northern cities would have big troubles of
their own sitting on their chests just then.
In other words, when the North is attacked,
its best chance of assistance, provided there
is a sane railway system to bring that assist-
ance, is from the big centres which are
nearest to it by land and farthest from it by
water. These are Melbourne. Adelaide and
Perth. The last-named, by the way, is
almost as large a city as Brisbane and its
population is growing much faster.
* * * *
A curious argument advanced by the
people who want to break the agreement un-
der which South Australia handed over the
Territory, and to abandon the transconti-
nental idea, and to divert the great Federal
railway, with much extra length and ex-
pense, round by the S. M. Herald’s office
door, is that the proposed deviation would
give the Northern Territory port the quickest
possible access to Townsville, Rockhampton
and Brisbane. Consequently, Harper, of
the N.S.W. Railway Department, appeared
before the Northern Territory Railways and
Ports Commission; and, unless some of the
Sydney papers slandered him grievously,
submitted this table of distances :
By the crooked
line deviating
By the straight through the
Port Darwin to line. Eastern States.
Adelaide . . . . 1896 . . 2172
Melbourne . . . . 2379 . . 2419
Sydney .. . . 2961 .. 2206
Brisbane . . . . 3686 . . 2143
Rockhamnton . . 4082 . . 1895
Townsville . . 4492 . . 1565
A lot of these figures—especially those con-
cerning Rockhampton and Townsville —are,
of course, a silly and impudent jest. They
are based on the theory that the great main
line from North to South may easily be
diverted eastward to hitch on to the local
railways from Rockhampton and Towns-
ville, and also Brisbane ; but that for some
wholly incomprehensible reason, if the great
Federal line goes straight across the con-
tinent from North to South, these local lines
can’t be extended a little further west to
hitch on to it. As a matter of fact, if the
great main line —the line that really matters
—was built to connect the Empty North on
the shortest possible track with the nearest
big city, it would put Rockhampton and
Townsville, and even Brisbane, a very few
miles further away from Darwin than the
deviation would do. The alleged 4082 miles
from Rockhampton to Darwin, supposing the
straight line to be built, are apparently es-
tablished by measuring the distance between
the two places via Gladstone, Brisbane,
Sydney, Melbourne and Adelaide. In the
same way North Shore, Sydney, is 1400
miles from Redfern railway station, Sydney,
if you go round by Brisbane. And anybody’s
front door may be 35.000 miles from his
back door if he persists in going from one
to the other via Timbuctoo.
* * * *
It is up to the people who. clamor for the
line round towards the S. M. Herald office
to remember that, in a matter of this sort,
Townsville. Rockhampton and Brisbane
don’t really matter. Especially Towns-
ville and Rockhampton don’t matter
one deformed swear. Brisbane, as already
mentioned, is smaller than Adelaide, and not
much bigger than Perth—also, it is not
growing so fast as Adelaide or Perth. Rock-
hampton is a pleasant place, and this writer
has very pleasant recollections of it, but in
the matter of population—that is to say, in
the matter of ability to contribute men to
the defence of the Empty North —it isn’t in
the same street with .Adelaide, Perth, New-
castle, Ballarat, Bendigo, Hobart. Parra-
matta, West Maitland, Geelong, Broken Hill,
Kalgoorlie or Launceston. And Townsville
is even smaller than Rockhampton. After
these coastal towns had made some provision
for their own defence they could do far less
' to protect the Territory in the hour of
trouble than obscure inland snots such as
Mount Gambier and Gawler and a
hundred others. They are all very
bad ports, and there is no need for
wild haste to connect the splendid harbor of
the Empty North with three rotten harbors
in the North-East. The connection must
come, of course, but the first essential is to
tie up the Empty North by the .
possible route with some States that havea
shred of population to spare. And the m .
densely peopled of the Federated State*
(1) Victoria and (2) Tasmania mi,®
direct transcontinental line will supply t *
briefest line of communication with th«
States, and that is of more importance
supplying the briefest connection with three
unfortunate dishes of water—to wit R,-n
bane, Rockhampton and Townsville—in '
State that ranks last but one for density
population. J 01
[James Edmond, The Bulletin Office, Sydney ]
THE ALLEGEDLY RUINED AND
DEFENCELESS TRUST.
The latest anti-Referendum argument ap-
pears to be that injurious trusts don’t exist
anywhere. Not only are they unknown in
Australia, but America itself is free from
them. The few that remain in the U.S. are
beneficent concerns, it would seem, for the
reason that the SiießxMan Anti-Trust Act
leaps upon them with lethal violence ami
unerring aim, the moment they start to inis
behave. Ex-Premier C. (1. Wade, 0 f
N.S.W., lately made this astonishing noini
in the S. M. Herald. In support of the
Liberal contention that the trusts in
America, as here, are perfectly innocuous
institutions, he wrote (“The Referendum”
Article vii.) as follows: —
There is a manufacturing case, the “Addyston Pipe
Trust case, in 1899, reported in 175, United States
Reports, 211.” There was an agreement of certain
iron pipe manufacturers to increase the selling price
of the pipes and to abolish competition between the
parties to the agreement. The Court held there was
an invasion of the Act, and granted an injunction.
After quoting a couple of other more or
less analogous cases, in which the Sherman
Act had been effectively employed, he went
on: —
Lastly, “the notorious Standard Oil C 0.,” after
hard fighting and many delays, was compelled to
succumb to the judgment of the Court, which granted
an injunction, and ordered the dissolution of the
trust.
The above, which are typical cases of the success-
ful operation of the anti-trust law, clearly demon-
strate that the machinery is effective,, but advocates
of the present Referendum assert that trusts are still
numerous and active in America, and that no sooner
are they apparently vanqu shed before the Courts
than they devise means for resuscitation and evasion
of the authority of the law.
In the matter of disingenuousuess the drag-
ging-in of the Addyston Pipe Trust case, in
this connection, is the limit, the absolute
brink, the extreme edge. .As an argument
it is about as justifiable as the contention
that Victor Thumper is no cricketer; be-
cause he was once bowled. Here and
there, in the United States, especially dur-
ing the first decade after the passing of
the Sherman Act, a combine, as a result
entirely of its own criminal carelessness,
has been stumped 6r thrown out or what
not. The Addyston Pipe and Steel Co. was
one of the few, the lamentably few, that fell
in in this way. The facts are simple
enough. The Addyston Co. consisted of six
corporations, whose business was the manu-
facture of steel pipes. It held a mon-
opoly in its particular line, which ex-
tended over many States of the Union.
Its intention “to monopolise or at-
tempt to, monopolise inter-State com-
merce” was frankly set out in its signed
and sealed agreement. And it was owing
solely to this ghastly and inexplicable error
that the co. came under- the operation of the
Sherman Act. Had it incorporated in
New Jersey, or Oklahoma, or South Dakota,
or West Virginia, or several other unpatri-
otic States that might be mentioned, and at
the same time time refrained from express-
ing in its agreements any intention to
monopolise inter-State commerce (while
fully meaning to do so, nevertheless) the
anti-Trust legislation of its native land
would have had no power whatever to harm
it. The classic case which points the
above moral is United States v. Knight.
Here an allegedly State Trust had been
instituted under the name-of the American
Sugar Refining Company. The members
of it were actually sugar refiners in a num-
ber of other States. However, the Trust
in its articles of agreement was careful to
announce that its only object was to make
and sell sugar within the borders of the
Trust’s State of origin. On being taken or
propelled to the Supreme Court, the Trusts
counsel took the point that there was nothing
in the articles which disclosed an intention
to monopolise the inter-State trade in sugar-
The Court agreed and ipso facto the Trust
went free. This despite the fact that, at
the time, the American Sugar Refining Co.
had control of considerably over 90 per
cent. of the total output of sugar
throughout the United States. To-day,
after 23 years’ experience of the fanures
of the Sherman Anti-Trust Act, the L.s-
Sugar Combine is one of the completes!
monopolies on earth. The nosition therefore
is, as regards the Sherman Anti-Trust Af -
that if a trust is suicidal enough to delin-
erately insert its head into a legal noose, o
if it relies on the advice of lawyers who are
so incredibly incompetent as to be
of facts which most U.S. schoolboys know
if, in short, it is one of those simple. Arca-
dian trusts that one may catch with one
hat, so to speak—in those unlikely events
will get into trouble. But if it goes to
trifling expense of registering in any one
the many States which will readily we c ,• n
it; and if it does not gratuitously men
in its articles that it is in the game
the object of monopolising all the busi
in its line, not only within its own State,
in other States as well, then neither
Sherman Act nor any other Federal m
sure can touch it. # »
The remark about the dissolution of th
Standard Oil Co. is as much calculated, u
not designed, to deceive, as is the a-
that the mishap which befell the Ad .
Pipe Trust peonle is “typical _ of the
disaster that always smites ,
bines in the United States. The Standard
Oil Co. was convicted under the - bv
Anti-Trust Act. and ordered to dissolv
the Supreme Court, in May, 191 L .. ur j
previously been found guilty m - ■ re ,
(also under the Sherman Act) °
peatedly getting unlawful rehat waS
railways; and on those £ r £unoonOO It
fined the stupendous sum of xb.uuu, d j g .
has never paid the fine, and it dissolved
solved. That is to say, it has no: , lge
in any sense which is of the - * nts idethe
to the public. Oil manufacture A <jn
combine are finding it as hard to


any good for themselves as ever, unless
reputable American* papers such as Life
and the Argonaut lie; and discoverers of
new oil-wells stand to he blackmailed as of
yore. The consensus of opinion among 90
per cent, of Americans is that the Oil Trust
has merely taken the 1931 judgment of the
Supreme Court to mean that if it can’t be
good it must at least be careful. As a
matter of fact, even Mr. Wade refrains from
explicitly committing himself to the state-
ment that this trust has dissolved: he merely
remarks darkly that it has been “ordered
to dissolve,” and passes on to other things.
One may reason that it hasn’t dissolved in
any genuine sense of the term, in much the
same absolutely reliable fashion as one may
deduce that Armageddon, though ordered,
has not yet arrived. To such gigantic pro-
portions has this Trust grown that its
actual dismemberment would be in the
nature of a cataclysm. The syndicate which
is the Standard Oil owns, amongst a num-
ber of other huge banks, the National City
Bank, which is perhaps the biggest and
richest in the world. It has controlling in-
terests in 85,000 miles of railways; in the
whole of the electric lighting, gas and rail-
way systems of New York ; and in the three
greatest insurance companies in the world.
Its wealth runs into unimaginable figures.
Spelling has faintly suggested it in these
words :
It can boom or depress the stock market at will,
and can control the call, as well as the time rates
for money borrowed on stocks. It could bring on a
financial panic within 24 hours that would cause a
thousand bankruptcies, compel an issue of Govern-
ment bonds and paralyse industry in all civilised
nations.
An institution of that magnitude does not
expire in any hole and corner way. Its
death would be a matter of burning interest
wherever people are able to read. The cable
told us that it had been officially ordered to
die two years ago. But no intimation of
the actual swallowing of the hemlock has
ever come to hand. The remains have never
been identified. Is it unreasonable, in the
circumstances, to assume that, in this, as in
a thousand other cases, the Sherman Anti-
Trust Act has proved impotent?
* * * *
Australia, under its existing Constitution,
is as indifferently equipped to deal with its
trusts as America is to control its Beef,
Sugar and Oil Combines. And this pro-
vides one of the soundest reasons for
the present Referendum campaign. There
are the closest analogies between the
plight of the two countries in this
respect. A series of High Court decisions
has indicated to the Commonwealth Parlia-
ment some of the things which it can’t do
in the way of controlling trusts ; a series of
Supreme Court decisions has taught Con-
gress a similar lesson in the United States.
The discovery by the American combines
that the Anti-Trust law will not reach them,
provided they form corporations under the
laws of individual States, and without ex-
pressing In writing the intention to monopo-
lise the commerce of other States, is paral-
leled here by the discovery, on the part of
the Beef Trust in Queensland. Tattersall’s in
Tasmania, etc., that a business concern which
registers in one State can defy the
displeasure of the Federal Parliament
for an indefinite period. The trusts
of America have always relied on gain-
ing immunity from justice by raising the
State Rights cry against their assailants:
the trusts of Australia are doing precisely
the same in this country at the present mo-
ment. The decree in the Standard Oil judg-
ments accomplished, to quote a writer auoted
last week bv Mr. Wade, “everything that it
is possible to acomplish under the Sher-
man Anti-Trust Act.” And when the
achievement is examined it is found to
amount to practically nothing. The decrees
of our Courts have accomplished everything
that is possible under our Anti-Trust legis-
lation. Which is to say, they have done
so little that they might as well have never
been formulated. It is ludicrous, in view
of the glaring facts of the case, for party
politicians to go on arguing that the United
States and Australia are equipped, to scotch
their trusts. A more useful lie is that all
trusts are unadulterated blessings. It is a
better lie, because it is so much harder to
disprove.
[John Bede Dalley, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Candidates.
Owing to the casMron and boiler-metal
fashion of machine politics, there are very
few triangular contests preparing for the
coming Federal election, and not a single
quadrangular one, while such a thing as an
octagon is never heard of nowadays. Under
a more sane political system there would be
a second ballot between the two top or roof
candidates, when half a dozen men (or
women) went to the poll and no one got a
clear majority; and then any joyous person
could butt in and add to the gaiety of
nations without causing the election of his
worst enemy upon a split vote. Nowadays
there is no such joy. Out of the 75 Federal
electorates there are only 10—Cook
(N.S.W.), Macouarie (N.S.W.), Nepean
(N.S.W.), Corio (Vic.), Henty (VicQ,
Bendigo (Vic.), Indi (Vic.), Echuca (Vic.)
—the machine never works so well
in Victoria as in N.S.W. —Adelaide
(S.A.), and Denison (Tasmania) —where a
third candidate has turned up. Three men
—Frazer, of Ivalgoorlie (W.A.), and Glynn
and Archibald (both of S.A.) get in unop-
posed. Glynn was unopposed in 1910 and
in 1903; in fact, his luck is so remarkable
that a lot of his enemies say that, some dav
or other, he will die unopposed. But S.A.
has put up a marvellous record for unop-
posed elections. At the first Federal poll
the whole State was one constituency, and
everybody opposed everybody else. But in
1903 five out of the seven members had a
walk-over; in 1906 four out of seven; in
1910, three out of seven ; in 1913. two out
of seven. Each State has to elect, three
Senators. In Queensland there are six can-
didates; in N.S.W. nine; in Victoria, seven;
in S.A., six; in Westralia, six; and in Tas-
mania, seven. In the Hume (N.S.W.) Labor
hasn’t put up a pledged candidate, but allows
good old Uncle Bill Lyne to carry its ban-
ner. which he will doubtless do with credit
to himself and the banner. In Koo.vong
(Vic.) it allows Miss Vtda Goldstein to
represent it as a friendly sort of Indepen-
dent. George Wise, who is a mighty good

three are journalists, and all the three candi-

for Bendigo (Vic.) are described as “barris-

the Hunter (N.S.W.) are miners. The two
for Wannon (Vic.) are farmers. The two for

consuming dog. The names that strike the'

[James Edmond, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
Again the Forgotten Mono-Rail.
A man who is apparently suspected of being
threatened with traces of information about
his own job—J. Haydon Oardew, presi-
dent of the Sydney University Engineering
Society—delivered an address recently which
contained some valuable points. This, for
instance, is from the Sydney Telegraph
report:—
“Speed is becoming the necessity of civilised coun-
tries, but it is more than a necessity in Australia,
it is essential to our existence.” He advocated the
establishment of an aeroplane service for carrying
mails, AND PROPHESIED THAT THE BRENNAN MONO-
RAIL WOULD BE THE RAILWAY OP THE FUTURE IN
Australia. As the old, slow steam locomotive was
responsible for the enormously overgrown cities of
the seaboard of Australia, so the high-speed mono
railway would break up the congested population of
the cities, and spread them over the more distant
and unsettled parts and create a revolutionary power
for the settlement of the so-called desert lands.
Australia has before it some of the biggest
railway construction jobs in the world. It
has to build its southern railway from east
to west; its northern railway from east to
west ( i.e from North Queensland to
Northern Westralia) ; and the north and south
railway down the middle of the continent.
Also, innumerable branches will be required
to feed these main Federal lines. Then the
Commonwealth has before it the stupendous
responsibility of changing thousands of miles
of existing lines to a uniform gauge. If
it makes a blunder, and does this work ,on
an antiquated pattern, so that the roads
will only be finished in time to start for the
scrap-heap, it may easily throw away
£50,000,000 or £100,000,000 of good Aus-
tralian money; Therefore, seeing .that there
is the strongest' possible reason to suspect
that a new invention is on the market—-
Brennan’s mono-rail, to wit—which will
consign all the existing lines to the scrap-
heap above-mentioned, one would fancy that
the most stodgy of Governments would take
an earnest look at it —or at least tell George
Retd to wake uo and abandon his bag of
confectionery and look at it—before plung-
ing into vast labors according to the old paths.
It is claimed for the mono-rail device that it
can tackle steep grades and narrow curves
that no two-rail locomotive can look at, and
that it can produce, with safety, at least
twice the velocity of the ancient biped con-
traption. These claims rest on actual trials
made in the presence of experts, and if the
trials were unfortunately made on a very
small scale that was merely because no Gov-
ernment had sense enough to put up the
money for a really big attempt. Countries
like England and France, which have their
railway systems practically finished, and are
doing very little new work, can afford to
lie low and wait till someone else proves
or_ disproves the advantages of the mono-
rail. They lose very little by waiting, and
they avoid responsibility. But Australia,
between its new constructions and its neces-
sary reconstructions, is almost starting
afresh, and if it starts on wrong principles
simply for want of a little reasonable in-
vestigation, there will be no gallows high
enough for the men who are responsible,
supposing it were possible to serve them out
poetic justice. Among all the questions now
before the Federal electors there isn’t one
half so important as this one. Yet, curi-
ously enough, this question doesn’t seem to
be before the Federal electors at all. In
fact, it looks as if all the bullock-teams in
Australia would be insufficient to drag
Andrew Fisher and co. to even a casual
and cursory consideration of it.
[James Esmond, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
The Lesson of Hunger Striking.
“0.K.” writes : English suffragettes, claim-
ing equal political rights with men, are apt
to squeal when the rights are not the sort
they want. For instance, they squealed so
shrilly against forcible feeding in prison that
the nourishment is no longer compulsory.
Even their higli-priestess, Pankiiurst, was
allowed to starve herself into a temporary
release from gaol the other week. But a
male conspirator with the suffragettes
(Hugh Franklin), who tried the hunger
strike dodge when he was sent to quod for
nine months for setting fire to a railway car-
riage, had to suffer the alleged pangs of for-
cible feeding three times a week—ll 4 times
in all. He didn’t get equal political conces-
sions with the women. Nor did they squeal
loudly on his account. To have asked for
equality of the sexes in Franklin’s case
would have been to confess themselves privi-
leged offenders against man-made laws. So
they were content to let the male champion
of Woman’s cause be treated as a mere
male. The hunger strike device, being essen-
tially feminine in conception, has brought
the “weaker” sex into violent collision with
suffragette doctrine. Mrs. Pankhurst would
stand on an equal footing with tyrant man
as a law-maker, but not as a law-breaker.
She has already taken advantage in prison
of her downtrodden condition, to do what
no man is permitted to do, and in so doing
she has given the cue for an all-round asser-
tion of sex privilege, which will be equally
embarrassing to her friends and her oppo-
nents. The possibilities of the hunger-strike
policy seem bound to appeal to every intelli-
gent woman who is in gaol for any cause
whatever. Why should the embezzling lady
clerk endure the awful monotony of two
years’ hard labor (that is, when lady clerks
begin to be equally prosecuted with mere
male clerks for the same sort of dis-
honesty, and equally sentenced by the
mere male Judge) if she can get out of gaol,
under surveillance, through refusing to take
the nourishment which only men prisoners
are compelled to absorb if they go on hunger-
strike? Why should a lady arrested on
suspicion of murder ever be satisfactorily
tried if she can hunger-strike with impunity,
because her sex is nrivileged? The tactics
of the suffragettes have reminded the Law
of how much latitude it allows, and will be
likely always to allow to women unless they
deliberately kill the goose-like sentiment
which lays the golden concessions. The
hunger-strike is the test of man’s inhumanity
to Sissie, compared with his inhumanity to
Jim. If a woman were allowed to starve her-
self to death in gaol, the public outcry would
be 10 times more absurdly emotional than
even the successful protest against forcible
feeding. On the other hand, a man might be
permitted to starve himself to death or die
of compulsory feeding, and the nublic would
reckon him well out of the world for his
accursed foolishness. What were brutality
to the woman would be “just what the man
deserved.” Man insists upon granting in-
equality to the other sex; the Law allows
it, and Mrs. Pankhurst, whilst doing her
best to put a stop to it, is evidently deter-
mined to see that she gets it.

The Finest Coastal Marine in the
World.
The Australian coastal steamship trade has
been flourishing fine balance-sheets for
several years now, and some of the latest
inter-State liners are fit to berth alongside
the best oversea vessels that lumber up Cir-
cular Quay. The Canberra, a towering
8000-tonner, came into port the other morn-
ing, and added itself to (he fleets that include
the Indarra (10,000 tons), Warilda (8000
tons), Wandilla (8000 tons), Karoola (7000
tons), and other new and impressive sea
mansions. There would be more occasion
for the usual speeches and champagne if, in-
stead of merely wandering in from the ocean,
these expensive ships slid into the water
from a Sydney or Melbourne slip. There is
no particular reason why they should not be
made on the national premises; the Austra-
lian has everything needful but an effective
duty; but that is sufficient to stop him, and,
while he is waiting for the Fisher Govern-
ment to attend to its job and provide him
with adequate Protection, whole squadrons
of foreign-built steamers are being added to
the mercantile fleets, and the chance of a
prosperous period is fading over the horizon.
The lunatic provision whereby all new ships
exceeding 500 tons, burden are duty free
does not even give local shipbuilding firms
a chance to bid for the shallow draught boats
that hurry up and down between the big
ports and the bar harbors. The naval archi-
tects have discovered, of recent years, how,
to build quite large • boats that will draw
only a few feet and still refrain from stag-
gering unduly. (Occasionally they forget
themselves and give passengers the shock of
their lives ; still, as a rule, they are well be-
haved.) These ingenious sea-balancing
machines steam contemptuously past the
pottering Federal duty, and the local man
has to try and keep going on an occasional
dredge or mud-punt order, and any repair
jobs that happen along. For very shame’s
sake, the Commonwealth should make haste
and give the shipbuilding industry a shove
ahead. From an economic point of view,
an island continent that is not capable
of making its own ocean-going steamers is
several miles behind any community of South
Sea head hunters. No one ever heard of a
head hunter importing canoes from London.
[Ernest O’Ferrall, The Bulletin Office, Syd-
ney.]

“If I were compelled,” said Joseph Cook,
leader of the Opposition in the Australian
Parliament, and chronic applicant for the
Prime Minister’s job (N. 8., no one that this
paper knows of desires to compel Joseph
Cook) —“if I were compelled” (this paper
here resumes Cook’s story after a lengthy
parenthesis) “to write a recipe for the reduc-
tion of the present high cost of living in
Australia over and above the increase com-
mon to the world, I should say, ‘The restora-
tion of honest economical and efficient gov-
ernment throughout Australia.’ ” The plati-
tude is so beautiful that there is an adjourn-
ment just here for applause. But,
after all, what does Joseph Cook mean by
economical government? For instance, a
healthy, upstanding Bulgarian legislator,
sound in wind and limb, and capable of just
as much _ incivility to the Speaker as any
other legislator, can be hired for 12s. a day,
and even that payment only lasts while the
one-House legislature at Sofia is sitting.
How is that for economical government?
And judging by the way the Bulgarian has
lately knocked seven devils and some saucer-
pans out of the Unspeakable Turk, the Gov-
ernment can’t be wholly inefficient.
[James Edmond, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
Apropos of the present anti-Defence clamor :
Again arises the moral —Be Prepared. In the
scrap which shook up south-eastern Europe the
Turks, with more than twice the population of the
Confederates to draw upon, hardly won a single skir-
mish, and the description of the 80,000 antiquated
scrap-iron rifles which the Bulgarians took from
them accounts for a great deal of this misfortune.
On the other hand, among the Confederates the only
Confed. that didn’t score a single really valuable suc-
cess was Montenegro. The Montenegrins certainly took
Skutari, which is a town of about 32,000 population,
and so completely dominated by hills that first-class
modern artillery should bump it out inside three
days, but it appears now that the place was sold to
them in return for their promise of assistance in
making the scoundrel in charge, Essad Pasha, King
of Albania. That leaves Montenegro with a very big
responsibility in return for a very small town, re-
garding which the Austrians have already served
notice to qyit. Even what little effect was produced
on Skutari by the besiegers was apparently, in the
main, the work of some well-trained Servian artil-
lery lent for the occasion. The Montenegrin has
always been regarded as about the best fighter
in Europe, but he is a fighter of the howl-and-rush
variety, like the McGregors and Macphersons of
the old Scottish clans, and though howl-and-rush
tactics, combined with a magnificent capacity for
lurking behind a boulder on a perpendicular hill-
side, have done great things in the defence of the
Black Mountain, they were of little value in attack
against even the crumbling walls of Skutari. On
the other hand, the Turk also used to have the repu-
tation of being about the best fighter going, and in
the days when he swept over Northern Africa, and
annexed Hungary, and pounded at the gates of
Vienna, and helped himself to Southern Russia, and
scared the Pope in Rome, he had some claim to the
honor. But though he was a great pounder and wal-
loper of the mediaeval type, and did tremendous
things in an age when everybody pounded personally
and walloped by hand, he didn’t keep up with the
scientific procession of more recent years. He fell
behind and became a back number, till a time came
when his. fez was imported from Austria. Even
with numbers and strong fortifications in his favor
his old Brown Bess armament gave away the Otto-
man show, and the Servian and the Greek and the
magnificently-trained Bulgarian climbed all over his
turban and his carcase. As already mentioned, the
one good show he put up was against the leaping
Montenegrin who was worse-trained than himself.
Montenegro left the best of its manhood dead outside
Skutari, and had to cry for help to its nearest
ally, and it finally secured the one fortified town
that stands to its credit by entering into large obli-
gations on behalf of the Turkish commander, who
promptly went forth and set up in business as a
fresh new monarch. And now that Montenegro has
been ordered to leave' its scrap of plunder all its
sacrifices are practically in vain. Even Austria
hardly cares to serve notice to quit on Bulgaria and
Greece, but Montenegro, with its gallant and pictur-
esque comic opera army, gets no consideration worth
mentioning.
[James Edmond, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
C. G. Wade, talking at Wagga (N.S.W.)
on the alleged failure of the Labor Govern-
ment’s land taxation, said that three years
ago the Laborites guaranteed cheap land
and plenty of it, whereas land was actually
now no cheaper than it was in 1910. Wade
shouldn’t go to the Riverina to air his half-
baked ideas about land. As a matter of fact,
values there had been climbing up steadily
for years, and it is only during the last 18
months or so that they have been steadied.
Moreover, in spite of the Federal land tax,
they would probably have gone on mounting
higher and higher and making the struggle
of the farmer harder and harder if the State
Government hadn’t dropped Wade’s own
mad scheme of settling people on the land.
The more vigorously Wade pursued his
policy—in other words, the harder he pur-
sued the big landholders to buy them out
with borrowed money—the higher the price
of land went: and not merely the land Wade
bought, but the land everybody else bought.
The graduated land tax may not have done
everything that a land tax ought to do; but,
anyway, it put the fear of God into the
heart of the big, loafing landlord. The Wade
policy merely put the glint of hope into the
big. loafing landlord’s eye.
[S. H. Prior, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
Thus The Bulletin’s Old Soldier:—
The English Peace Society has got hold of the
Manchester Guardian, and induced it to circulate
quite a remarkable little story, calculated to make
everybody outside the Society of Friends roar with
laughter. I tear off a fragment:—
“Despite the agreement of 1911, foreign Gov-
ernments will not accord Australian war vessels
cither the peace or war privileges allowed regular
light’ng ships, unless they are part of an Imperial
fleet.”
We are not at -war just yet, so we may ask what are
“peace privileges” ? A ship of war entering a foreign
port is entitled to have its flag saluted. It is also
exempt from the jurisdiction of the local police and
magistrates, but it .cannot send armed parties on
shore for any purpose without getting leave from the
local authorities. In war-time it' hasn’t any special
privileges if it is a belligerent. A merchant ship
can stay in a neutral port till doomsday, but a war-
ship must either leave in 24 hours or submit to be
disarmed and dismantled. The Guardian appar-
ently doesn’t know that the Australian fleet has
quite a number of precedents behind it. Egypt,
Greece and Bulgaria had recognised naval forces
when they were still Turkish dependencies. India
has a sort of navy of its own. Canada is doing some
The Warning.
