Should the Referendum indorse the annexation of
trade and Commerce proposed by the Caucus, we shall
place under the Federal Government, among: other
matters too numerous to catalogue, all the sales of
all our butchers, bakers, grocers, drapers, millers,
auctioneers, pedlars, hawkers, chemists, druggists,
etc. ; and all business dealings in milk, dairy pro-
ducts, gold, coal, poisons and explosives.
—Argument against the proposed law.
Now, if you vote “Yes,”
Says the Fusionist press,
In a voice which is terribly raucous ;
What you drink and you eat,
Such as bread, milk and meat,
Will be wholly controlled by the Caucus.
Yes, the Caucus
Will step in and seize
Pickles, butter and cheese,
Tea, currants and coffee and ices,
And vend them around,
By the quart or the pound,
At tyrannical Government prices.
The trade in your foods
And your drapery goods,
Which you proudly display to your neighbor,
Will be rudely annext
On the lightest pretext
By the hot-headed Party of Labor —
Yes, of Labor.
Your carpets and mats
And remarkable hats
(Oh, say, are you listening, ladies?)
Will be branded L.P.,
And it’s easy to see
That the country will hurry to Hades.
And though you vote “No,”
Still the country will go
To the Deuce all the same, but more slowly,
If Labor be sent
To resume Government
And continue its tactics unholy ;
Most unholy.
One must hopefully look
For the triumph of Cook
(Oft described as a crude and a raw cuss)
Who is leading the band
That would rescue the land
From the murderous clutch of the Caucus.
[Edmund Fishee, The Bulletin Office, 369 Col-
lins-street, Melbourne.]

The prosecution of a 17-year-old boy at Mel-
bourne City Court, last week, threw another
sidelight on the base uses to which the
assisted immigrant system is sometimes
turned. The accused came from Cornwall
two months ago. His father, a mine mana-
ger, had supplied him with a little money,
and this he “lost” on the voyage out. Pos-
sibly he went “no trumps” with three weak
suits in his hand. Being an unhealthy im-
migrant and a moral weakling he took to
“pinching” neglected bicycles from the kerb,
and sold five of them for the miserable sum
of £3 ss. He desired to go back to dear old
England. A lady immigrant, who mothered
this valueless specimen of population on the
voyage and after his landing, stated that she
had written to his parents to send out his
return passage-money, and meanwhile she
would undertake to look after him. So the
boy was released as a first offender. If his
passage money is forthcoming his Australian
career will come to an inglorious end next
month. In the hearing of the case nothing
transpired as to how this hopeless immi-
grant “well educated and just from college,”
had come to be passed by the Victorian State
agent in England as a qualified agricultur-
ist, or why he had not been placed on his
arrival with one of the farmers who are said
to be crying out for labor. Apparently he
had never thought of getting away from
Melbourne city or of taking un any industry
save the “pinching” of bikes from the road-
side.
The Melbourne Age is in a tangle over
the election for Denison (Tas.). It won’t
support Laird Smith (Labor) at anv price,
and the Fusion candidate, whom, by the
way, it calls “W. H. Trenth,” is that old,
reliable Protectionist, but backsliddery
Laborite, W. A. Trenwith, whom nine years
ago it derided and threw clods at till further
orders. Certainly there is an Independent
candidate that it might support in
Alicia O’Shea Petersen. But her chances
are as Buckley’s, and the Age loves to be on
the winning side. So it will probablv sup-
port the hated Trenwith—in a cold, ex-
hausted sort of way.
[F. C. Jones, Elgar-road, Burwood, Victoria.]
Concerning one of Australia’s three women
candidates: —
Hobart, of all places, has a lady candidate; she is
contesting the Denison seat in the House of Reps.,
and her name is Alicia O’Shea Petersen. A buxom
matron, she has a deal of originality, and a large
assortment of political common sense, with streaks
in it. Wearing a stylish opera cloak, she came on
to the Town Hall platform without a chairman or
supporter, and with the confidence of a bookmaker
took complete charge of the meeting. She wants
to abolish the Address in Reply, Legislative Councils,
and silver trowels and spades at the opening of pub-
lic buildings and railways. She also proposes to
put her foot down on compulsory defence training
of youths. In between getting hoarse, she got out-
side a mug of what looked like water; raising her
g’ass, she exclaimed, “Here’s to my election!” Near
the end of her address, a youth impressed with her
chances of a Parliamentary income of £ 600, publicly
inquired, “How about seeing you after the show?”
Her chances are not as rosy as her cheeks.
[R. Solly, Park-street, Hobart.]
“Baron Bowyangs”: The N.S.W. Labor
Government appeared the other day at Syd-
ney Water Police Court as landlard. It
prosecuted a withered dame. 80 years of age
for rent dues which had fallen into arrears,
or, in the event of failure to pay,
demanded possession of one of its
beautiful and palatial humpies on the
Rocks Resumed Area. The aged party <
stated that she had resided on the
property for 57 years, 37 of them in her
present house. The magistrate opined that
if she didn’t pay up what she owed for rent
she would reside on the old hallowed snot
for no longer than another month. It was a
risky prosecution to take on. “The dying
rockchopner” would be an insignificant
heart-bleeder; in the throes of the adjacent
election as compared to a poor old mother
of 80 cast upon the streets like a homeless
dog to die (loud sobs) by the order of a
brutal Government (hoots!).
One surprise of the Federal nominations
was the uprising of Miss Mulcahy against
little Dr. Maloney, for Melbourne. “Baby”
Kirton, erst of Ballarat, had started a ten-
tative, trifling sort of campaign against
Maloney, who thought so little of it that he
hadn’t bothered to hold a meeting. But Kir-
ton rushed in with his nomination two
minutes too late, so “Liberalism” and the
W omen’s National League are not officially
represented in the contest for the district of
the temporary Federal capital. The Liberal
banner, therefore, falls into the lady’s_hands
by default. Miss Mulcahy, by the way, was
organising for Labor for the past few years,
and puts her conversion down to the party’s
delay in granting effective Protection, the
abolition of the postal vote and other mat-
ters. The “little doctor” will now have to
hustle, but not very strenuously.
[F. C. Jones, Elgar-road, Burwood, Victoria.]

Probably it was a place like Sydney that
the Evangelist, in “The Pilgrim’s Progress,”
had in mind when he described the town of
Vanity:—
There were at all times to be seen jugglings,
cheats, games, plays, fools, apes, knaves and rogues, -
and that of every kind.
This writer reckons he could place every-
thing mentioned excepting the particular

This critic has nothing further to state,
in reference to that badly built but highly
popular American comedy, “The _ Fortune
Hunter,” except to reiterate that it is still
badly built and still highly popular at the
Criterion.
The Alhambra (Sydney), under the old
and reliable management of J. L. Goodman,
has decided to swear off moving pictures
and nail its prospects to the burnt cork of
the corner-man and the joy song of the
serio-comic lady. Also, there may be a
fragment of faith pinned to the young thing
that flutters in pink fleshings ; but not suffi-
cient to make bald stall-holders cease
mourning for the rude old days. The first
trial of the experiment was given on Satur-
day before an expansive audience, and unless
the fervor of its applause was a mere com-
pliment to the management, or a cover for
muttered curses, the programme was satis-
factory. The Bulletin reckons it was a
tidy little show, and one that would not be
spoilt by encouragement.

“Fanny’s First Play”—which is very obvi-
ously Shaw’s latest play—was staged at the
Little Theatre, Sydney, on Monday night.
It teems with humor and observation and
bizarre viewpoints; the characterisation is
masterly; and the situations are wildly
amusing. Yet it didn’t earn half the
laughter and applause which its rare
merits deserve. The explanation seems to
this writer to be that it isn’t one of
those plays which transplant well. It is, in
the highest degree, racy of the London soil.
In fact, it is racy of an infinitesimally small
part of London. It would make little anpeal
probably, except as a roaring farce, to Hamp-
stead or Fulham, much less to Poplar or
Islington. On the other hand, to a certain
type of West Ender it would be a thing of
pure delight. All the necessary conditions
ar* supplied by the astute playwright. The
Middle Classes are scarified throughout, with
a sort of aloof benignity, suggestive of the
humorously tolerant spirit in which the
Squire rebukes one of his tenants for a bout,
of drunkenness. The brother of a Duke is
shown in the guise of a footman. Nothing
thrills the West End more pleasurably than
the spectacle of a Duke’s relative in a menial
position. The noble fellow’s splendid self-
control, in face of the vulgarity of his em-
ployers, causes the audience to murmur
thoughtfully, “Ah ! blood will tell,” “Noblesse
Oblige,” and other comforting maxims of the
sort. The sensation which occurs when his
identity becomes known is a genteel equiva-
lent of the appearance of the Face at the
Window, or of the great railway smash in Act
IV. of “The Blood on the Tablecloth.” And
then there is the device inspired probably
by the arrival of the rdvue in England, of
introducing real critics. The critics and
their views are old friends —to London ; that
is to say, to the section of London which
belongs to the Garrick, and reads the Satur-
day Review, and buys Max Beerbohm’s
caricatures, and is seen at every important
first night. To us they are total strangers.
The Little Theatre management made an
effort to introduce local color in this connec-
tion, by having the four critics made up to
resemble Sydney journalists. But it didn’t
bring the characters any nearer to the audi-
ence. Rather the contrary. Trotter, for
example, who displayed the gloriously egg-
like dome or cupola of the man Bedford,
voiced sentiments which Randolph would
burst rather than hold. And so it was with
the others.
* * * *
The plot of the play, which Fanny’s doting
father induces real actors to act, and real
critics to criticise, in an English country
house, concerns the misfortunes of a couple
of shop-keeping families. Younsr Mr. Gilbey
has gone on the loose, and received a sen-
tence of 14 days’ imprisonment for being
drunk and disorderly, at the same time as
Miss Knox (to whom he is engaged) has been
gaoled for wantonly assaulting _ the police.
Gilbey’s revel has been enjoyed in the com-
pany of a preposterously genial young woman
named Delaney. Miss Knox has penetrated
to the lounge of the Empire alone; been ac-
costed there by a strange Frenchman ; gone
on to a night club; and been arrested dur-
ing the subsequent raid on the establish-
ment. Anyone who knows Shaw will under-
stand the delicious sidelights that are thrown
on everybody as the outcome of these com-
plications. There is a family argument
between the Gilbey and Knox parents —one
of those immemorial old domestic controver-
sies -which begin nowhere and progress from
one idiotic inconsequence to another, till
all hands are half frantic —that is worth the
entrance money by itself. Everybody does
and says the most unusual things in the
most natural possible manner. Presently
we become so infected by the author’s spirit
that, when one of the characters acts reason-
ably (when, for instance, the anguished
Knox inquires as to the “intentions” of the
foreigner who has compromised bis daughter)
we are convulsed. In the epd Juggins mar-
ries Miss Knox; young Gilbey is affianced
to the astounding Miss Delaney, whom his
mother has taken to at sight; the Frenchman
delivers a panegyric on the land where such
things are possible, and all ends dementedly.
* * * *
A notable play—the best joke of the year,
so to speak—and the acting of it is aston-
ishly good. Alfred Bristowe provides, in
Duvallet, a brilliant sketch of a genuine
Frenchman —one of the hardest things to
discover on the English-speaking stage.
Kenneth Brampton (Bobby Gilbey) is excel-
lent as a Cockney youth of raffish habits.
Miss Violet Paget (Margaret Knox) is to
the life the strenuous, vehement female whom
Shaw conceives as tracking down defence-
less man with a view to marriage. Miss
Shirley Huxley has a trying role to handle
as Dora Delaney, and she overplays it here
and there, notably when the maddened
Gilbey dashes from her presence in uncon-
trollable fury. Her wide and affable grin
should surely fade, if only momentarily,
before the aged man’s despair. G. Kay
Souper, Reynolds Denniston, and Hugh
Buckler are good in more or less straight
parts. The parents are excellently repre-
sented, and so are the critics, a couple of
them being triumphs in the art of make-up.
Take it for all in all, this is a play which
everyone should see once, and which some
will see half a dozen times.

Despite the special attractions at the rival
comic show round the corner (the Tele-
graph), including Sporting Charlie Wade,
the Mount Kembla slide-for-life expert, and
Jo Carruthers, the bounding wire-netting
lifter, the National Amphitheatre (Sydney)
is offering quite as much genuine amuse-
ment as could be expected. There is plenty
of farce, make-believe, rag-time, and so on.
The star turns are provided by the four
Avolos, xylophonists, the seven Mangeans,
acrobats, and a team of monkeys under Carl
Prinz. Anyone who wants more entertain-
ment for the money than can be obtained at
the National is urged to go into Federal poli-
tics.
[Signed, by reason of the allusion to Federal poli-
tics, by John Beer, Counter Lunch Hotel, Gcorge-
street, Sydney.]

after the steer whereon the hero is bound,
the now almost-common racing machine being
brought successfully into action, and the

Sydney appears to be tlie Mecca of trained
animals. Within the past few months chim-
panzees, bears, horses, dogs, nigger pugilists
and educated pigs have made pilgrimages
this way, and now a troupe of trained seals
has put in an appearance at the Tivoli.
There are seven of them altogether, though
two do the bulk of the work, and their reper-
toire consists of balancing various articles of
furniture on their snouts, and then either
rolling over, climbing steps or walking the
tight rope. A seal band, the drum being
most in evidence, provides the noise. The
Clarence Sisters and Brother’s quick-change
act isn’t likely to be the cause of any riotous
outbursts; and Sydney Bach, the monologue
entertainer, is having a second time on
Mclntosh’s bit of the earth. O’Hana San,
Cummin and Sealiam (last nights), Osborn
and Brookes and the De Bruns are the re-
maining leading lights. Next week Marini,
the warbler, and the Patti Franks will make
their re-appearance.
THE PANTO: A FRAGMENT.
[For The Bulletin.]
From ev’ry eye approval shoots:
Joy gleams like newly-lit cheroots.
Dumb are the ignorant galoots
Who, all too often, greet with hoots,
Groans, cat-calls, oaths and jeering toots
Plays that are steeped —aye, to the roots!—
In Art....No fears, no parking doots
Beset the folk in “Puss in Boots.”
The author of the above (viz., John B.
Wowser, the Blue Ruin Coffee Palace, Syd-
ney, who hereby signs in the presence of
three witnesses and a dog, lest he should
unwittingly infringe the Act) had progressed
thus far when he was driven to desist for
lack of further rhymes. Enough has been
sung, however, to indicate to the world that
the show at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, is still
with us, and going strong.

ally does theatrical notices for this paper:—

nowadays, curse them), the one thing that is com-
mon to the entire tribe from Idaho (U.S.A.) to
Peebles (N. 8.) and further, is impatience under
criticism and furv in the face of opposition. Take
the J.C.W. Theatrical Trust in Australia. It has
actually started a paper of its own. Anyhow, the
Theatre is largely run by one of the young men
in the Trust’s employ in the sense that he holds
shares in it and writes editorials, and so forth for it.
Part of it consists of slavish adulation of those m
the employ of the Firm, part of acid assaults on thf
performers at rival shows. F'rinstauee, on this
month’s issue we find such highly edifying shafts as
the following:—
“What is Madge Temple [of the Tivoli] engaged
for—to sing songs or wear dresses?
The Lord Mayor mentioned at a banquet that no-
thing would be performed bv the Rickards player-
of an indelicate nature. Then Madge Temple en-
tered and sang ‘When I Get You Alone To-night.

tawdry- form of advertising? [i.e.. The Littl

ihat the idea is detrimental to all canons of art,
there can be no shadow of a doubt. To begin with,
it is vulgar, and vulgarity, by nature of its very
hideousness, has nothing whatever to do with art
in any form.... A golden opportunity to that abom-
mable pest, ‘the stage-door Johnnie.’.... [moans
from the scandalised chorus ladies] to be
hoped that before long the management will have
discovered a less glaringly vulgar mode,” etc., etc.
The Bulletin is, admittedly, about the only Aus-
tralian paper which leaps decisively on the Trust’s
shows from time to time. Of course, the Trust’s
loyal young servant is maddened. Hear him:—
“The Bulletin substitutes the humorist for the
critic. Nobody takes the comments of this weekly
on the theatre as anything beyond mere light read-
ing. .. .Efforts based on imperfect observation
Trivial... .provide the critic with a pair of strong
opera-glasses. .. .piffling depths,” etc., etc.
The foaming writer next proceeds to rend The
Bulletin’s literary style.
“In a notice of ‘The Belle of New York,’ it is re-
marked that Mr. Leslie Holland’s performance lacked
‘unction.’ ‘Extreme unction,’ probably. If the critic
meant unctuousness,’’ etc., etc.
The critic meant unction. He also meant unctuous-
ness. Webster, who is good enough for him, defines
“unction” as, amongst other things, “a simulated
or fictitious fervor, or emotional gush, unctuous-
ness.” The above trifle is only interesting as indi-
cating that one with a considerable knowledge of
the theatre is liable to possess but an exiguous ac-
quaintance with the English language.
The really useful lesson to be gained from the
whole outburst is that a Trust, so soon as it achieves
a position of financial importance, more or less, con-
siders that it should be sacrosanct. It mustn’t be
criticised, or its young men will give the critics a
series of good, hard knocks ; and those who oppose it
in business must be firmly exposed for the immoral,
godless and vulgar outsiders that they are. A writer
never properly appreciates how low and debased and
intellectually deficient he is, nowadays, till he finds
himself yammering pitifully on the wrong side of a
Trust palisade.

Adelaide picture shows are wisely desert-
ing the old idea of giving merely a pleasant,
unrememberable evening. The scheme now
is to offer something the patron can talk
about. Wondergraph has a realistic,
gory bullfight, and a careful, regardless-
of-expense “Tempest,” chiefly remarkable
for the fact that it improves on
►Shakspeare by acting in detail all the
previous Italian plotting which he “took as
read.” West’s deals conscientiously with
Hugo’s “Miserables” —gloomy, but a master-
piece. To the King’s are coming some much-
heralded Japs. At the Tivoli is De Biere,
who gives even the experienced theatregoer
something new in the way of appearances
and vanishings. This turn lasts for a quite
unusual 50 minutes, though it doesn’t seem
half the time. “The Monk and the Woman”
have arrived, together, at the Royal. Yel-
low and Green don’t fight to any extent
worth mentioning in sensible South Aus., so
the piece has to struggle along without the
glorious ad. it got in more benighted cities
—this critic names no names. Other shows
have varied from a Knox organ recital and
a bulldog championship to an evening of
song, story and drama, all written by one
Rosenblum, a local author of whom more is
going to be heard. And Adelaide’s already
high musical reputation has a further lift
from Heinicke’s Orchestra, a brilliant band
of 60, which packed the Exhibition at its
opening performance on Saturday night.

The Bulletin’s Expert Bellowdammer —
a young man who will go miles to
see * a murder, and who possesses no
reverence for grey hair, whiskers, sofa
stuffing, or theatrical trusts —reports
briefly thati “Antony and Cleopatra,”
now at Sydney Royal, may be right enough
for Shakspeare Societies, but that it would
be a rank failure, if produced on honest
bellowdrama lines, under the title “Driving
a Fat Soldier to Destruction.” It is hardly
necessary to add that the flippant young
Bellowdammer has not yet seen his photo-
graph in the Theatre. Persons of his sort
seem to be carefully excluded from the
“faculty” of stage criticism, of which a well-
known Sydney daily paper man has been
elected “dean.” If the Bellowdammer learns
Proper Respect, the Theatre may one day
appoint him verger, or it may even ordain
him a probationary sub-curate. But his
chances of ultimately gaining high distinc-
tion are practically nil. He need never hope
to enter a crowded theatre on a first night,
arrayed in vestments and mitre, and carrying
his crozier and shield of anonymity, the lat-
ter decorated with his official coat-of-arms,
i.e., a nound of butter rampant in a field
azure.
Jean Valjean, Hugo’s lovely example of a
regenerated ruffian, once more held the col-
lective eye of West’s audiences in Melbourne
on Saturday. Jean has excited the tearful
commiseration of the crowd for some nights
past, and scraps of Bizet, Auber and Ros-
sini have accompanied the screening of his
woes apparently for the pleasure of those
who are not yet educated up to grieving to
rag-time tunes. “Confidence” was the name
of a good film supporting the “Miserables”
item, and there was also a story of a cove
who stopped a bank rush by flourishing a
few bags of bogus coin. This same item can
be found in 25 novels at least.
* # * *
“The Speculators,” the new musical bur-
lesque at the Bijou, might be best described
as a contest between Plain Absurdity and
Common Sense, with the latter early retir-
ing flabbergasted from the fray. Hilarity
reigns at intervals in this concoction where-
in little has any rhyme or reason, and the
plot is so extremely thin that the wind blows
brown paper through its bones. The finale
of the first act consists solely of the long and
silent evolutions of 20 supernumerary sol-
dier ladies in a stockbroker’s office. The story
concerns the chase of some mysterious scrip,
and the hunt is conducted with most of the
principals close on the quarry and a tuneful
chorus whipping in. Song and patter inter-
rupt the pursuit, which is also occasionally
adjourned for the perpetration of some terp-
sichorean stunt by the. clever legs of Miss
Eugenie La Blanc. This little lady has jet-
tisoned the quaintness which made her an
eccentric golf caddy in “The Grafters,” and
she is now a more conventional male imper-
sonator with her active understandings as
her strongest suit. Miss Teddy Martin,
Miss Gertrude St. Clair and Carlton Chase
play their little parts without offence, and
Bert La Blanc and Frank Yack, as the con-
ventional Jew and Dutchman of ’Murkan
vaudeville, indulge in irresponsible business
in which the latter looks like an elephant
trying to be a Pierrot. Which remark is
the subtlest reference the writer can think
of to suggest the comedian’s art and ombong-
pong.
“Zigomar” found an asylum at Melbourne
Majestic last week. That detective story,
wherein Zig conceals a heavy safe in a sewer
what time Broquet, the ’tec’, goes blood-
hounding after the lost property, gave the
congregation all that is required in the way
of thrill. Two comedies called “Jealousy”
and “Boulter’s Niece” serve to raise the
spirits. of those who were depressed by the
narration of “Near to Earth.” This week
the Majestic is putting in motion “The
Mystery of the Corner House,” a murder
drama wherein Beer is shown as the cause
of many cadavers, and Rum paves the broad
road to the cemetery.
*
Many pounds’ worth of boiled shirt and
chiffon were present at Melbourne Princess’s
on Saturday when “A Woman of Impulse”
did a tearful but select moan. Lady Lang-
ford, whose shallow, spendthrift career might
be gleaned from the butt of her husband’s
cheque book, so adores her father that she
saves him from the consequences of theft,
and thereby enmeshes herself in a web of
intrigue wherein one Navourac plays the
polite evening-dress spider. Navourac is
hard to realise as a logical character. Ap-
parently he is a political adventurer with
the wandering look of a decadent poet. He
seems to have got the Langford female in
his power for motives that are as vague as
the proceedings are preposterous. Even after
long cogitation on the subject, this writer
isn’t at all clear whether Navourac requires
the fair charmer to annex the secrets of her
husband, a tame diplomatist, or else desires
to use her as a tool in helping to rid his
bleeding fatherland of a foreign despoiler.
What is painfully obvious, however, is that
he involves the lady in a mesh of falsehood
and conspiracy, culminating in an early
morning scene in his private rooms wherein
she explains her presence and incidentally
conceals her father’s wickedness by a bogus
confession re non-existent misbehavior with
her masterful friend. Thereupon the lard is
in the conflagration, which burns with a
blue flame until the disclosures of several,
including Navourac, relieve the situation and
restore confidence all round. Navourac fades
out of the story after that, and Lady Lang-
ford’s pa dogs himself to the grave by a
mixture of whisky, weak heart and inflam-
mation of the conscience. So it only re-
mains for Hen. Plimmer to embrace his wife
and forgive her for all her good intentions.
His playing of the Langford baronet is
bright and convincing. Miss Beatrice Day’s
artistic and delicately-touched picture of
Lady Langford has all the importance of an
Old Master, and Mrs. Brough nicely pre-
sents Mrs. Dudley as one with just sufficient
civilisation to be a lady and just sufficient
claws to be a cat. Miss Parkes, G. S.
Titheradge, Arthur Styan and Sydney Stir-
ling are right enough in their resnective
parts, while Navourac is H. R. Roberts
divested of his usual shining virtues. Roberts
is not always successful, especially in the
emotional passages, But allowing for the
patent artificiality of Mr. Navourac, his im-
personation is very passable indeed.
At Her Majesty’s, Melbourne, the last
nights of “The Count of Luxembourg” have
arrived with rather indecorous haste. On
Monday (12th) “The Cingalee” will have a
tarewell revival for four nights, and then
the theatre will pause to take breath before
the firm’s other musical co. comes along with
“The Sunshine Girl.” on the 17th.
“Faust” passes out of notice at the King’s
on Friday. Next night an Anderson Co., the
one wherein Cyril Mackay and Roy Red-
grave stand for virtue and otherwise, will
appear for the first Australian time in “Life’s
Shop Window.” Which play is adapted
from a story of the same name written by
one Victoria Cross, whose novels are gener-
ally understood to be more “nutty” than
righteous.
In place of De Biere, who departed the
programme last week, H. C. Clarke and M.
Dale Owen are appearing in a slop-made
duologue “sketch” at the Opera House.
Theirs is about the weakest effort in humor-
ous comedy and trimmings that Melbourne
has lately experienced. The lady still talks
like the gum-chewing waitress at the Battles-
burg Hotel, and Clarke is the same old
Blackie Daw, but without a Wallingford to
play up to. Cassie Walmer is back again.
Scott-Leslie offers a mild entertainment of
the drawing-room variety. Reynard bursts
into baritone song under highly pretentious
conditions., and Henri French continues to
be droll up to a point. Also, Madge Temple
sings of Billy and other matters in a style
which has unobtrusive art to recommend it
and a well-tailored personality to back it up.
* * * *
At the Athenaeum Hall, where Joseph
McCabe is concluding a brilliant series of
science lectures, and crowded audiences are
viewing their evolution on a screen, the
demon of frivolity awaits his opportunity to
take the platform. Next Tuesday (13th)
“The Dandies” start a six weeks’ season.
Three or four years ago the Jesters had a
successful innings at the Athenaeum Hall.
The Dandies, being better known exponents
of the same sort of trifling, are expected to
top their predecessors’ score.
Clara. Butt, the coming event at the
Auditorium in Collins-street, casts her tall
shadow across the threshold of that long-
expected concert hall. The Auditorium
thus far stands revealed in all its pride of
up-to-date completeness to private view
only, but already the Taits’ box plan is open,
and on Saturday, the 17th, the first of the
Clara Butt concerts will start the new build-
ing on its business career.
“Man and Superman,” as played by Julius
Knight and Co., at Melbourne Royal, “knocks
spots” off the recent Repertory Theatre per-
formance of Bernard Shaw’s comedy in the
matter of staging. The scenery in the second
and third acts gives much more scope for
effective grouping and by-play. Apart from
the gain in picturesqueness, the acting, as a
rule, has more force of realism. The Malones,
son and father (Herbert Ford and Green-
away), the Mrs. Whitefield of Maggie Knight
and the incidental prim old spinster of
Emma Temple are strikingly presented,
whereas those characters merely outlined the
author’s creations in the amateur show, and
the Malone outlines in particular were crude.
One gets a fuller sense of Shaw’s humor and
satire from the present production, yet the
performance is hardly more entertaining
than when Gregan McMahon appeared as
the dominating centre figure in the narrow
Repertory circle. After all, “Man and
Superman” is mainly a one-man talk. The
philosophy of the verbose and incredibly
wall-eyed John Tanner, in which truth and
fiction race neck and neck, seems the pre-
vailing purpose of the play. Now, Julius
Knight’s treatment of Tanner, irrespective
of the fact that he doesn’t quite look a pos-
sible former playmate of Irene Browne, lacks
the spirit of the central character. There is
nothing of the nervous, luxurious, egotistical,
theoretical Socialist about Julius.
If Gregan McMahon was too hard, dry,
spasmodic, aggressive and repellent, the pre-
sent Tanner errs further in the opposite
direction. Julius Knight’s manner is too
kindly, his tone has only a thin veneer of
brusqueness in places. He shapes too much
like an accepted humorist when he should
seem to take himself in dead earnest. In his
hoarse, throaty utterance of audaciously un-
conventional doctrines and bizarre sentiments
some of their pungency is lost. He will do
better when he is more familiar with his
lines, but he can never enunciate them as
cleanly and incisively as McMahon did.
Somewhere between the two widely con-
trasted impersonations the real Tanner of
G. B. Shaw would be found, and when found
he would not be a real piece of humanity,
anyhow. The Tanner of the book is flinty
in conversation and putty when pawed by
the feline Ann. Miss Browne’s Ann has
felinity enough, but her tricks are glaringly
apparent, her affectations of speech and pose
get on one’s nerves, and her make-un might
be intended for a seductive Lady Macbeth.
She has no color of fresh young vitality, but
her acting in the final scene is magnetic as
well as graceful. Having taken possession
of her man, her appealing manner of bid-
ding him resume his flow of monologue sug-
gests amorous womanly pride in his elo-
quence. Robert Whyte is a quietly natural
Ramsden; the Octavius and Violet Robin-
son of Martin Lewis and Mary Goulden are
pleasing in all respects. Leslie Victor is
rather too consciously Cockneyfied as ’Enry
Straker, the chauffeur, and of the people men-
tioned at the outset of this sermon Maggie
Knight is conspicuously convincing. “Man
and Superman” is for 12 nights only.
“Maestro”: Freddie Shipinan, of America, is pre-
senting to Sydney, on tile 3)st inst., about the most
sensational baritone in the world, David Bis mam is
his name, and he is a weird cross between a very great
artist and an expert revivalist, in uttwetn n.s bon*s
he is liable to chat to the audience like an old
friend ; explain why he doesn’t go off and be dragged
back with seeming reluctance to supply an encore;
and in general gives the tricks of his trade entirely
away. In certain grand operatic roles (f’rinstance,
Vanderdecken in “The Hying Dutchman”) he is the
world’s admitted best (the compositor made it
“beast” at first). He sings always in English. One
of his aphorisms is to the effect that opera audiences
don’t really object to English, only to bad English.
His most famous number is “Danny Deever” (lvip-
ling-Damrosch). “Danny Deever” is to Bispham what
“Home, Sweet Home” is to Patti; what the “Laugh-
ing: Song” is to Dolores, what “The Blind Boy” is to
Chirgwin. Owing indirectly to this song, Bispham
has the death of a fellow mortal on his soul. Frantic
with disappointment at the great man’s refusal to
sing “Deever” for the third time, a highly-cultivated
Boston lady swallowed her false teeth, and passed
over shortly afterwards in intolerable anguish.
The recent fracas on the stage of Melbourne Opera
House between conjurer De Biere and a citizen who
wanted to tie his thumbs so that he couldn’t get
them apart, was the inevitable result of De Biere’s
advertising challenge to all and sundry. A conjurer
who announces his willingness to forfeit £5O to a
hospital if anybody can so tie his thumbs together
as to prevent him from passing a hoop between them,
is simply looking for trouble. He may bluff and
wriggle his way out of the difficulty when the aver-
age person comes along, but he can’t achieve the
impossible against a man who refuses to be bluffed.
It isn’t clear that the conjurer can ever do the hoop
trick satisfactorily if his thumbs are secured with
unfriendly twine —limp, but unstretchable. Cer-
tainly, he cannot do it if they are tightly held with
a bowline. But why demand the impossible? The
trick that De Biere performs so neatly and amaz-
ingly when he is working with his own string,
which, having wire in it, yields to pressure and re-
mains outstretched when one thumb has been slipped
out —is as ancient as all the tricks that every con-
jurer performs. It is known to have been worked
100 years ago. Possibly it is as old as the Pyra-
mids. Few conjurers can do it. Small knuckled
thumbs, as well as great dexterity, are essential to
the performance. A few weeks ago a young man
was giving a display of the same trick at Luna Park,
and his crude imitation of De Biere’# finished feat
merely showed the difference between the amateur
who knows how a thing is done, and the gifted
artist who does it to perfection. 'The ring-chain
trick is another of the same family. All conjurers
know about it. Only the most skilful attempt it in
public.
“The Iconoclast”: Shakspeare’s alleged birthday
was celebrated on April 23. The true anniversary
was not due until May 3. Why ? Because England,
being a Protestant country, would not adopt the
RC. Gregorian Calendar until it was compelled by
Nature herself to do so in 1752, when 11 days had
to be thrown bodily out of the year in order to get
the solar circuit into its true path, and put the
seasons back into their places. The Catholic
nations adopted the calendar in 1582. when 10 days
were dropped out for purposes of adjustment. The
Gregorian, or New Style, was adopted by Sweden in
1753. Germany and Switzerland had adopted it in
1700; but Russia (and Greek Church countries
generally) have not done so yet, so all foreign let-
ters sent thereto are headed with two dates—O.S.
and N.S. Shakspeare is said to have been born on
April 23, 1564, and to have died on April 23, 1616,
aged 52 years. But both dates were according to
Old Style. Cervantes died on April 23, 1616, aged
69 years; but Cervantes died in Catholic Spain,
which for 34 years had adopted the Gregorian Cal-
endar. So, instead of Shakspeare and Cervantes
dying on the same day, the former died 11 days
afterwards.
A Sydney critic objects to Lily Brayton’s red-
haired Cleopatra, on the grounds that “it is impos-
s’ble to suppose her other than a brunette.” Why? Per-
haps because Shakspeare, who certainly never saw her,
and in all probability never heard of the Lagidae,
speaks of the typical lover seeing Helen’s beauty
“in a brow of Egypt.” Being partly Greek, Cleopatra
would have been as liable to be red-haired as a woman
of the Jewish or any other normally darkish race. The
Greeks were fair-skinned, violet-eyed, and often
golden-haired people, like their kinsfolk the
Georgians, who have retained their old-t’me beauty
down to the present day. What more likely than
that one of the famous charmer’s red-haired forebears
handed down the rufous strain to her? Certainly her
conduct with Antony and others, and her subse-
quent uncompromising methods with the asp, arc
suggestive of the coloring that has always gone with
verve and pluck and the joie de vivre.
“Floater”: The Taits, who directed the Australian
tour of Margaret Cooper, the pianologist, are re-
gretting the indiscretions of that remarkable lady.
Margaret was never threatened with humility or
with that reticence which is the better part of diplo-
macy, so when an opportunity offered on her de-
parture from Australia she biew off some hot air
anent the present or ultimate degeneracy of the Aus-
tralian race. To a Westralian reporter she confided
that “the Australians hadn’t enough reverence for
their betters” [presumably the English], and that
“Australia needed a leaven of Engl’sh aristocracy to
elevate and otherwise improve ‘the colonials.’ ” Those
observations were naturally bad for Margaret’s busi-
ness, for the Very Best People of Perth and Fre-
mantle stayed away from her chatter-at-the-piano
with great persistence, and often left her frowning
dreadfully at a wilderness of chairs. Something
similar happened in South Africa. At Johannesburg
she openly poked borak at the local elite by solemnly
plaving them into their seats when they strolled in
late; while her husband, Ilumble-Crofts, added to the
general unpleasantness by remarking that a certain
Johannesburg clerk “was the first gentleman he had
met since he left England.” So the lady became
rather disliked, and people started asking. “Who is
this Margaret Cooper?” and things like that. And
to put it bluntly: Who is Margaret Cooper? Apart
from a certain vaudeville prestige, she is, socially
speaking, very small potatoes; while her hyphened
husband is the son of an obscure country parson and
has never, to the public knowledge, done anything
more strenuous than play the incendiary to innumer-
able cigarettes.

JUGHT TO BE KILLED.
The Test who insists on explaining everything
in a loud voice just because he’s happened
to see the show before.
The most astonished person in Melbourne
was Thomas Jones Chia when the Judge
sentenced him to five years “with hard” for
having tried to murder his former boss be-
fore he had found pluck to murder himself.
The wretched little Chinese cracker had
quite confidently hoped he would be at
once set at liberty to leave Australia with
the alleged intention of returning to China.
Which allegation would probably have been
as false as all his yarns about his wealth,
his royal rank, and his castle on the Lake
of Chung Loo Soo, seeing that the Chinese
penalty for trying to murder a Government
official is something with snick-a-snees and
boiling oil in it. Chia’s idea, no doubt,
was to inflict himself on one of his numerous
relatives who live outside of Chow jurisdic-
tion. The exact legal value of his little in-
discretion was beyond his understanding,
apparently. When the Judge told him that
he had been an artful bounder and a calcu-
lating criminal the amazed prisoner began
to look welly sick. Still he retained his
feet. The sentence was practically a knock-
out. Chia reeled up against his fate, and a
warder took him to. the cells below. The
Law hasn’t Buckley’s chance of getting any
hard labor out of the exploded firework. He
is not built that way. His is the tempera-
ment of a turtle dove in the physique of a
sardine. The governor of chokee will have
to permit Chia to sit by the kitchen fire and
tell fairy tales to the warders.
A f6w minutes’ education in “push” slang
was afforded a South Melbourne Bench re-
cently when a youth, accused of calling the
police “rotters,” explained that he was with
a mate who was “giving it tongue,” when
he saw “a couple of rozzers”—not “rotters”
•—coming along, and “gave him the office.”
His mate had had a few “tastes” in, and he
thought he might be “hooked up”—hence
the gentle warning. The Law professed
ignorance of the word “rozzers,” but the
youth assured it that “State school nippers
would answer in chorus if they were_ asked
what it meant.” But whence comes it, and
how and why?
The first chapter of the Book of the
Lamentations of Jones: —
It isn’t always, the lucky man who wins the Art
Union prize, though it is almost always the unlucky
one that is bitten by the clog. A motor-car was
the star feature of a recent Melbourne hospital
gamble, and it' was won by a Laoor union official.
He now regrets that he didn’t draw a blank. A
motor-car can’t be used on a worker’s wages. Even
though he never starts the engine going the garaging
stands him in a matter of 15s. per week. And then
there are “sundries”—thousands of them. The Mel-
bourne winner tried to sell his white elephant at an
alarming sacrifice. In vain. People used to call to
inspect it and insist on being taken for drives. They
would then coldly refuse to buy, and the unhappy
worker would pay out some more cash for petrol,
new tubes, tyres at £lO each, and so on.
It is an old car, and old cars are like
old clothes —of little use save to the owner.
At latest advices the owner was thinking of cutting
his losses by giving the car away. His position
resembles that of the lady who lately won, as prize
in a church bazaar, a block of land at Laverton —
which is an exploded township of the “boom” days,
out near Werribee. (The first owner had vainly had
it on sale for years at £2 the lot. In despair she
offered it to the church as a raflle prize.) The
lucky winner paid £2 2s. transfer fees, a small
accumulation of overdue rates, and sundry other
items, and then settled down to enjoy her property.
Later she decided to sell. That is as far as she
has ever got. She may get ss. for it some day, if she
is fortunate. Meanwhile the municipal rates have
to be paid; so the longer she keeps it the more
it costs her. She has made several efforts to give
it away as another church bazaar prize, but the
local, churches are shy of it —very shy. Does any-
body know a way of utterly annihilating real prop-
erty? If so, will he please write to Mrs. Smith,
13 Brown-street, Jonesville?

A most promising lawsuit lias been
strangled at its birth in Adelaide. The
powerful Canowie Pastoral Co. had an ex-
hibit rejected at the Show in March. After
a heated correspondence,
the co. took the Agricultural
Society into the Supreme
Court, calmly claiming both
the prize end a declaration
that “the finding or opinion
of the alleged committee of
experts” was “contrary to
the ordinary priniciples of
natural justice.” Symon
and Piper, K’s.C., were busy
arguing a merely technical
application to strike out
pleadings such as that just
quoted, when Judge Murray
took a hand. Did the society
make any imputation
against the honor of the co. ?
“None,” said Piper. Did the
co. make such against the
society? “None,” said Sy-
mon. Then, said the Judsre
(in carefully selected judi-
cial language, of course), go
outside and settle it. Ad-
journed sine die. The only
valuable item in the expensive proceedings
was the admission by .Josiah Symon-—re-
markable from a lawyer—that it is a
mistake to write letters when you are
angry. “Correspondence led to friction,”
he said. “It is quite marvellous, when
two men get together, how difficulties
disappear in five minutes.” if people with a
grievance remember that, lots of lawyers
will starve.

Twice again in Melbourne inquiring
friends have been told that hospital oatients
were getting on well, whereas subsequent in-
vestigation proved them in fact to be dead.
The Melbourne Hospital told one man that
his wife was “very comfortable” when she
had been deceased a whole week. Smith to
Brown: “Hello, old man, what’s your
trouble? Why those tears?” Brown: “I’ve
just rung up the hospital. They tell me my
wife is doing splendidly.” Smith : “I’m aw-
fully sorry, dear boy; Can I do anything
for you in the way : of arranging for the
funeral ?”
Sharp Cry of Protest from “Jeames” :
1 notice that Mr. C. G. Wade, in the course of an
assault upon his enemy, .Holman, accused the latter
of “fraternising with the Dukes at tne Carlton Club,
London.” Also, the young gentleman who does the
political notes for The Telegkaph mentioned bit-
terly that the Associate-Premier of N.S.W. had been
publicly entertained at “the Carlton Club,” and he
inquired, inferentially, what Holman’s outraged con-
stituents intended to do about it. The place where
Holman really fed and orated was, of course, the
City Carlton; and that anyone, even a daily paper
journalist or a State politician, should mix the two
resorts up, is to a Londoner, and one who has waited
on the best people in his time, simply stupefying.
The City Carlton is what is known as a “pot house.”
Practically anyone who is solvent, who has contrived to
avoid gaol, and who has at his command the Tory
watchwords or gags Of the period (this year they are:
“Winston is a d —d outsider” and “the little Welsh
attorney is ruining England”) —anyone, I say, with
the above qualifications, can belong to the City Carl-
ton. It absolutely gleams with Jews during the
lunch-hour. Most of the other members are stock-
brokers, solicitors, company promoters and uncon-
victed robbers of other sorts. There is a small
leavening of titled guinea pigs, young men of well-
known families who have gone into the “City” to
sell it motor-cars or what-not, and Parliamentarians
with city interests. This helps to redeem the inten-
sely Hampstead Heathian appearance of the members’
list. But dukes are as scarce there as they are at
(say) the Junior Athenaeum, to which a butler of
my acquaintance has belonged for years; or the
Junior Constitutional, of which a tailor, who has
made many a livery for me, is one of the oldest
members.
A very different proposition, indeed, is the Carl-
ton. It is the Conservative Club of the Empire.
The ordinary citizen has to have his name down for
a matter of 20 years before he can so much as come
up for election. If he is at all well-known (which
is to say, if he has the usual number of enemies), or
if his sponsors have, since they put him up, grown
unpopular, or turned Radicals, or been hanged, or
anything like that, he will be blackballed. It is one
of the few remaining London clubs, membersliin of
which confers a certain amount of social distinction.
Ernest Terah Hooley offered £lO,OOO to the Union-
ist partv’s funds, chiefly on the understanding that
he would be elected to the Carlton if it was ac-
cepted. The man who made an offer of that sort in
reference to the City Carlton would be ripe for Col-
ney Hatch. And, finally, strangers aren’t entertained
at" the Carlton. The City Carlton stands on .much
the same plane really as the Royal Automobile Club,
which numbers about 21 Hebrews to the square foot,
and to which practically anyone can be immediately
elected by the simple device of donning a pair of
motor goggles and getting photographed in a taxi.
From a man in th'e business:—
One of the most difficult problems of the police
magistrate has been what to do with the wife-wal-
loper. Here are four instances: No. 1 was a beetle-
browed son-of-a-tough, who had knocked his pale,
anxiety-corroded wife out of shape with his hoof.
Sentence, two months without the option. A night
or two later the magistrate observed the woman
slowly beating up the pavement in a hopeless, hag-
gard way. The story was simple; there were young
children to be fed and warmed, and the magistrate’s
conscience revolted at the recoil of the punishment
he had decreed. No. 2 was before the same court,
and the evidence showed that this husband-animal
had brutally ill-used his wife (for no apparent
offence) in the presence of her four small children.
It was a fair thing to tear him off three months’
hard labor, but the magistrate recalled the wan face
of the woman in the street, and fined the bread-
winner in the cage £3, in default one month’s im-
prisonment. Next day the wife called on the har-
assed dispenser of .justice, and explained that the
money couldn’t be raised, and that the prospect in

SUDDENNESS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Picture plays can be a means of education... .1
can only protest against the really improper ones;
but as I have not been to either surf-bathing or pic-
ture plays, my opinions are really valueless on these
matters. I am over from Maoriiand on a campaign
against the drink traffic, and feel this gigantic evil
is as much as I can take in hand this year.
-—The Modesty of Mrs. Lee-Cowie.
God made the world in one day short of seven,
And finished earth before He started Heaven.
Stars, planets, trees and rivers, stones and sticks
He made in evenings and in mornings six.
Since then the world has slowly, surely worked
To make complete the job that first was burked.
No more a week makes system from the mud.
It takes us thirteen weeks to grow a spud.
We make no constellations in a minute.
It takes the wheat three months to grow life in it
No more a system builds ’tween dark and dawn,
No more three shifts can trim a stellar lawn.
We go but softly that we may go far.
He fails who ties his waggon to a star;
We know our father had to hurt his foot
Before he found out how to make a boot.
We know that hunger gripped him in a vyce
Long years before he learned that chops were nice.
That he was worn by travel —tried by force
Ere need showed him how to break in a horse.
And that of sunburn all his skin was full
Before he robbed the first sheep of her wool.
And so he grew, full painfully, with time —
Lifting his weary head from out the slime.
And slowly learned, by pain and shame and slights,
To take command of all his appetites.
But Missus Lee —who knows not picture plays,
And hates joy as she hates the surfers’ ways—-
Has come to smash the evil grown of beer
And thinks ’twill be enough to do this year!
Oh, Missus Lee ! Ye shall not banish yet
The golden wine until my sun has set.
Beshrew me! Don’t abolish —there’s a dear,
Please don’t abolish alcohol this year!
Next year I may be dead and then you may
Kill the gigantic evil as you say.
But hasten slowly! Think how long it took
To write Blue Ruin’s secret in a book.
The savage tapping at a toddy palm
And gladly sampling the fermented balm !
The Primal Boozer with the First Fat Head—
The Primal He who went in boots to bed.
He who the first quart of the Bubbly supped
And was by the First Barman Piek-me-upped.
Consider all the years it took to make
Varieties of all things the thirst to slake !
The aeons in which students pale did win
By step and step from Lemonade to Gin !
And think you this great work Shall disappear?
No, no ! dear Wowserina ! Not this year !
N.S.W.

A young lady-doctor at Adelaide Hospital
has had bad luck. And the patient, a boy
of 15, has had worse. He was struck in the
abdomen by a broken leather belting. The
wound seemed long, but not deep, so, having
attended to it, the doctor-girl sent him home.
A man-doctor saw him on the scene of the
accident, and another at his home ; and both
considered it a superficial cut. Yet, in two
or three days he got worse, and died quiclAy
of peritonitis. Then a post-mortem found,
right inside the bowels, a two-inch steel belt-
fastener. The Coroner asked about the boy’s
clothes, which somebody had described as
being undamaged. He sent for them. There
were three garments, and every one had a
small but clear hole in it! Then the fur
flew. Coroner Ramsay Smith is afraid of
nobody, and the things he said will probably
lead to a reorganisation of the junior staff
of the hospital. Some of the acidulated com-
ments
It is useless to expect anyone to believe that an
honorary surgeon or physician seeks a post out of
pure love for the sick p00r.... The Hospital Board—
I do not say in its wisdom ; 1 am going to use no
ambiguous terms —I say in its ignorance, because I
am certain that the majority of the members of that
Board had not the least idea of what they were doing
... .It is useless to imagine that the public, or even
the Hospital Board, can long be kept in ignorance of
the damage that is due by entrusting, or making
possible the entrusting, of the diagnosis, treatment
and care of serious cases to officers of the institution
who have not the skill and experience necessary, and
that are demanded, for the protection of life and for
the general comfort and cure of the patients.
Ramsay Smith was one of the two senior
men whom Kingston brought out from Eng-
land to run the Adelaide Hospital when the
big strike of local doctors took place 12 or
15 years ago; so he knows a few things
about the inside of the institution and isn’t
the usual magisterial prattler.

“E.0.R.”: I was accidentally digging
among the by-laws of the City of Hobart
(Tasmania.) in search of the regulation
dealing with insane perambulators when I
found this :—
No person shall ride, conduct, push or impel a
machine upon any footway made or set apart for the
use of foot passengers except for the purpose of
going to or from any' house or premises.
So, if you are going to the Devil, or going to
throw* yourself into the Derwent, or going
to perish on a mountain top, or going to cut
your throat in the middle of the Great Afri-
can Desert you must keep off the footpaths
of Hobart. But if you start on a bike or
motor-car in Hobart and are bound for
Buckingham Palace, London, or the Jumna
Musjid, Delhi, or the Mosque of St. Sophia,
Constantinople, you may drive on the pave-
ments of that humble Strawberry Island
city till all is blue. For you are assuredly
bound for “premises.” The miscreant who
said that Buckingham Palace wasn’t “pre-
mises” would, deserve, for his disloyalty, to
be torn asunder by wild sheep.
* * *
“Hamer”: There is something weirdly in-
human about the icy imperturbability of our
courts. Take a recent gun-loosing case. A
taxi-cabman is speaking: “Some words took
place, and Blank produced a revolver, saying
something about ‘fixing her.’ I said, ‘You
can’t do that sort of thing in
the car.’ A few minutes later I
heard the report of a revolver.
I thought something had hap-
pened, and rushed in.” They
manage their court ceremonies
better in the Latin countries.
There, when a citizen is ac-
cused of (say) stealing goods
to the value of 3s. a cer-
tain amount of glow is intro-
duced into the case. The ac-
cused interrupts the prosecutor
with a passionate declaration
that he is innocent, and makes
a public request to the Deity
to censure him with lightning
if he has sworn falsely. If
there are five witnesses, they
introduce at least 20 different
emotions before the advocates
have a chance. And by the
time the defending attorney
has dealt, in a voice breaking
with emotion, with the import-
ance of the accused to his aged
mother, grandmother and invalid sister-in-
law, and burst into tears, the whole Court,
including the Bench, is in such a condition
that the only way out is embraces all round.
Why can’t we introduce a little of this here?
It would be worth a very solid term of gaol
to see Magistrate Smithers, of Sydney, (say)
publicly kissed by a fowl-stealer and two
policemen.
* * * *
A point is continually cropping up in
industrial matters that demands prompt
legislative settlement. That is whether an
employee can be compelled to work over-
time. All industrial awards fix a minimum
rate of pay for a minimum week’s work, extra
time to be paid for at an extra rate. It
appears however (at sea, anyhow), that an
employer can demand extra labor from his
employee, even if the latter does not desire
to work over the award hours. A case of
this sort recently came before the Water
Police Court (Sydney), when a seaman
on the Eden was prosecuted for refusing to
work. The master had ordered him to help
wash the decks on a Sundav, but the sailor
said it was his watch below, that he was
tired out, having already been on duty far
too long, and was not having any more just
then. He argued that the ship’s safety
might depend on him when he resumed duty,
and that, as he didn’t want overtime, and
the decks could wait, it was up to him to
have a sleep. The magistrate decided he
would have to donvict the seaman of refusing
a lawful order: but in view of the extra-
long hours he liad been on duty, let him off
with six hours cells. Such a prosecution
would be quite imnossible ashore, and, in
the circumstances described, should be equally
impossible afibat.
It takes a good deal to shock a parrot, but
there was one badly shocked in Adelaide
lately. It was not surprised. There was no
time for that. It was just shocked. It was
a very old bird, entirely out of luck. When
it escaped from its suburban home for a fly
round it happened to have a few inches of
steel chain dangling from its leg; and the
three wires that offered such a convenient
perch happened to be those conveying the
tramway power from Port Adelaide to the
City. The chain touched one wire and the
bird another, and that was all —just a flash
of lightning and some roast parrot. Then,
as a touching tribute to the bird’s memory,
every tramcar in and about the city stood
still, just where it happened to be, for the
space of about 5 \ minutes —after which the
remains were found and pulled down. It
was the busiest hour of the evening home-
rush, too. The tramway officials will tell
you all about the Blasted Parrot on the
slightest provocation.
* * * *
“Pontius Potter” : I have discovered what
keeps people alive in those dingy houses that
Randolph Bedford wrote about (B. 20/3/’I3L
Sometimes it is a black-leaded window sill
or door-step, and other times a door-knob.
Opposite my humpy is a terrace of five cot-
tages. The windows
are hung with fly-
blown lace curtains,
and tousled-haired
youngsters are usually
squashing their noses
on the dirty glass.
Cobwebs of a genera-
tion prevent any sun
penetrating the fan-
lights, and the var-
nish is coming off the
front doors in flakes.
But each portal is
provided with a large
brass knob. This art-
ful addition by the
jerry builders keeps
the inhabitants from
being rushed off to the
lunatic asylum or the
gaol too frequently.
At most times of the
day a female can be
found attached to one
or other of the handles
with a rag and some
polish, doing her best
to make the knob out-
shine the one next
door. The woman in
the end house dropped
out of the competi-
tion recently, so I was
not surprised when
she strangled herself
effectually in the
woodshed with a
clothes line.
A real John Weary turned up in a Sydney
police court last week. He was charged
with stealing a set of harness, and, when he
cheekily asked the owner of the horse-clothes
whether he (Weary) hadn’t once worked
for him, the harness-owner first contradicted
him flatly, and then explained to the Bench
that he had once given Weary a reference
“just to help him along.” However, that
was in the far back days when Weary had
borne a good character. Later on. the weight
of the good character had proved too much
for Weary, so lie had cast it off, and, in a
moral sense, collapsed in a heap with the
stolen harness. The verdict was live months’
rest. The moral of the social tragedy seems
to be (a) that a charity reference is liable
to turn into a double-edged sword and a two-
headed adder, and (b) that very little save
yawns is to be expected of a human being
called Weary, or Restwell, or Feetup.
Melbourne Board of Health is arranging a
regulation making it illegal for a grocer to
puff wind into his paper bags, as a means of
opening them. The practice is said' to be
a possible source of infection in certain
diseases. In short, another fatal blow.
The cry of “Bill” :
The boom that Manly has been on since surf-
bathing began to rage is in danger of a slump un-
less a cold, unrelenting eye is focussed on Sydney’s
City Council. Owing to the garbage-destructor at
Moore Park being unable to keep pace with require-
ments, the tip is in a horrible condition. Even the
Health Department has become insistent on improve-
ment, and the brilliant Gaud Mayor Cocks was de-
livered a few days ago of the remedy. This was to
turn the footsteps of the garbage cart from Moore
Park to the water-front, where the festering luggage
of the vehicle could be placed aboard punts, carted
outside the Heads and cast upon the bosom of the
current, which, by the way, sets into Manly beach.
It is the simple scheme of a plain man. The gar-
bage problem is solved; the surferagettes have no-
thing to do but pack their bathing costumes in their
purses and proceed to Coogee or Bondi; apd Manly
—well, Manly can take a spell to count up the un-
earned increment since the Bronzed Girl and the
Hairy Sunburnt came to gambol on the beaches.
A STUDY IN

“Now, dearie, you must

LOOKING ON.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
An immense crowd witnessed the march of 20,000
cadets through Sydney on Saturday afternoon.
—News item.
I love to sit upon a roof and watch processions pass,
To scan the young battalions through a borrowed
opera-glass ;
It’s fine when lusty, marching bands, with music
fill the street!
I love the shine of bayonets—the tramp of booted
feet!
The silken flags with crowns atop that pass hemmed
in with steel
Are most inspiring, too —for them great reverence
1 feel.
Yes, though I’m undemonstrative, I feel inclined to
yell,
When patriotic ardor makes my loyal bosom swell.
I am not old or feeble yet, no snow is on my hair ;
But here I sit like any old campaigner in a chair.
The clash of cymbals stirs my blood, the bugles stir
my feet;
The twinkling sticks the drummers ply upon my
strong heart beat.
Some primal lust the music stirs, but I must stand
aside,
And watch the silken banners toss upon the steel -
bound tide.
“Too old !” The weight of twenty years my bowed
head rests upon ;
So, when the young battalions pass, you’ll find me
looking on !
When brave young troops go by,
A veteran am I !
N.S.W.
METHUSELAH CRADLE.
WHERE THE BOOT PINCHES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The bootmakers in the electorate represented by
Minister of Customs Tudor are wanting to know a
lot of things from the Labor candidate. The exist-
ing tariff has enabled cheap foreign makers to cut
great swathes out of the local trade, and the Rich-
mond shoemaker is finding the greatest difficulty in
sticking to his last.
“1 am a stout Protectionist,”
The sitting member said.
“If for a moment I desist,
And workmen find they rrtust subsist
On thinnest air instead
Of customary hread,
I greatly hope it’s understood
That my intentions all are good.”
The worker sitting by the wall,
With not a thing to do,
Knows that a harder fate may fall
Than half a loaf. It’s none at all.
He realises, too,
Through inward pangs a few,
’Tis in his stomach, now so slim,
That foreign boots are pinching him.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
A HOLLOW MOCKERY,
HOUSEWIFE: "But you appear a big , strong
man.”
THIRSTY MENDICANT: “Ah, if madam could
only see how hollow I am."
“Doubter”: Must have been a special
brand of dingo (or whisky) that, according to
“Gouger” (B. 3/4/T3) attacked the old hatter in
the hills. The ordinary species of the beast is the
most cowardly on the continent. Knew an old gin
who had to pass through two miles of scrub coming
home at night from work. Often she would meet a
pack of dingoes (after the goats), whereupon she
would yelp like a dog, and run towards them. But
she never caught them. The way they hurled them-
selves at the horizon was a revelation in velocity.
* * * *
“Mallet”: I don’t know which State-
“E.S.S.” writes about (B. 10/10/’l2) when he says,
“the mountain duck is no better to eat than a bar
of soap or a tom cat.” The mountain duck, such
as lie describes, is much sought after by bushmen
in Westralia, and I can testify to its edibility. As
regards the musk duck, all “E.S.S.’s” remarks are
warranted, although I have heard that this species
is also edible if, immediately on shooting, the musk
bag (which hangs under the beak) is cut off. Can’t
testify to this from personal experience, however,
as I was always overpowered before I had a chance
to cut anything but a passage through the atmo-
sphere.
* * * *
“Mallarapoonie” : Many Australian Bingliis
have a wonderful power of voice manipulation. Have
heard them run through gamuts of sound, as a com-
mon thing in their sing-songs and corroborees, such
as no white person’s voice ever had register to com-
mand. More than that, it is no uncommon thing to
hear a native holding converse with another on the
intake of his breath —-a practice quite unknown
among whites. The peculiar throat formation of
many savages makes for almost habitual ventrilo-
quism. One of the best exponents of the art of
voice-projection I ever heard was a Wogite gin at
Bradshaw’s Run (N.T.). A party of Jumonjus from
the Fitzmaurice River, after some trouble about
cattle, had taken to the mountains, where, about
two miles away, they could be discerned like ants
against the skyline. In the still of one frosty even-
ing a brass-throated old gin on the station was told
off to expostulate with them. Beginning her utter-
ances somewhere down in the basement of things,
she ran up, in a series of weird tremolo modulations,
a scale of about six octaves to the top C. A feature
of each effort was the abruptness with which it ter-
minated. Silence seemed to fairly shut down on the
tail of it like the lid of Tophet slamming on the
protestations of a Lost Soul. One could hear the
last shrill quaver of it float out through space, and
t the echo of it vibrate and die out among the hills.
It was a, wonderful example of the range of a
human voice; and each time we could hear the
answering hail come back from the mountain top,
like a small, thin, gramophone-voice wailing in the
wilderness. Don’t know anything so penetrating,
unless it be the shriek of a State Fright when the
axe of Nationalism suddenly commences operations on
its backyard fence.
“Sebrof”: If there’s to be any skiting
about useful plants, I’m into it on behalf of the
cocoanut palm. Here are some of the uses to which
the natives in Ceylon put the tree: Leaves, for roof-
ing, mats, baskets, torches, fuel, brooms, fodder for
cattle and manure ; stem of the leaf for fencing, yokes
(d la John Chinaman) and fishing rods; the cabbage,
or cluster of uneX-
panded leaves at top
of the palm, for pickles
and preserves; sap,
for toddy, distilling
arrack (an intoxicat-
ing drink), making
vinegar and for sugar ;
the unformed nut, for
medicine; the young
nut, for drinking; the
green husk, for pre-
serves ; the nut, for
eating and for curry;
the oil, for rheuma-
tism, for anointing the
hair, for soap and
candles; the shell of
the nut, for cups, ehar-
c o a 1, toothpowder,
spoons, hookahs, beads,
bottles and knife-
handles ; the coir, or
fibre, which envelops
the shell within the
outer husk, for mat-
tresses, cushions, ropes,
lavas, fishing-nets,
brushes and mats; the
trunk for rafters, laths,
railing, boats, troughs,
and furniture. On one
occasion, a ship from
the Maidive Islands
called at Galle, and
was found to be built,
rigged, provisioned and
loaded solely with the
produce of the cocoa-
nut palm. If any Abo.-
writer goes one better
than this, I’ll know
exactly what- to call
his grandmother the
ancestor of; and. by
thunder, I’ll do it.
“W.A.C.”: Where are the highest ant
hills in Australia? I’ll quote North Queensland on
spec. Recently a telegraph wire (18ft. from the
ground) was earthed through contact with an ant
sky-scraper. But the lineman who reported the cause
of the fault had his work cut out to make the depart-
ment believe the news; in fact, it wasn’t until he
produced photographs of the locality that his reputa-
tion was rescued from destruction.
GROGAN (more in grief than anger): “ D’ yez see that now? After all the civility and
kindness I've been showin ’ that felly Ryan! An’ see him there, lyin’ dead drunk
over the way at Cassidy's!”
The Adelaide dailies lately reported a
mishap which sounded comic, but was really serious.
A Geranium Plains farmer sold an old Angora billy-
goat, but the affectionate beast made back to its old
home. It found its old master busy over a seed-
drill, and butted him from behind. When that hap-
pens in “Steele Rudd’s” stories, or in an American
Sunday supplement, it is always considered a matter
for merriment. In this case, the victim of the prodi-
gal goat was taken up stunned, with several ribs
broken, and other injuries hard to see the end of.
* * * *
“Eddyson”: Oakley made Rock’s ac-
quaintance in Sheehan’s bar at The Punt, and found
him more than a cut above Sheehan and the rest of
the adjacent male population. Rock was a big,
lean, ginger man, a prospector, with literary lean-
ings and fairly accurate opinions of politicians.
Oakley liked him, and agreed that they should travel
in co. as far as Two Kegs, which was Rock’s head
centre. “Bechune us two, Rock’s ez mad ez a moon-
sthruck ca’f,” whispered Sheehan before Oakley
drifted out. “Ye’d bether kape a live eye on him.”
This opinion did not impress Oakley, who knew
that out-backers are prone to regard the reserve of
a superior spirit as a form of dementia. On the
long tramp to Two Kegs he found nothing to
justify Sheehan’s estimate. Rock was talkative and
genial, with a touch of humor, and fresh, quaint
views of men and matters. He had small difficulty
in persuading Oakley to pass the night at his hut.
He prepared a good bush meal, provided whisky
and tobacco, and, later, told the story of Laver’s
finish. Laver had been his mate for 50 years, and
had broken his neck in a shaft close to where Rock’s
hut was built. Rock seemed much moved by the
awakened memories. “I wonder you go on living
so near the hole,” said Oakley. “I can’t leave it,”
replied Rock. “God knows poor Bill’s lonely enough
as it is! ” The night was fine and warm, and
Oakley unrolled bluey, and slept out of doors. He
slept well till on towards two in the morning, when
he woke to find himself being dragged quickly over
the rough ground. Rock had him by the left leg,
and was doing the hauling. Oakley’s host was in
his shirt, and his ginger hair was frisking in the
breeze. Oakley yelled, but Rock took no notice.
The prospector was talking to himself. “Right you
are, Bill. I’m coming! I’m coming! Square deal
it is, Bill.” Oakley kicked with his free leg, but
Rock paid no more heed than if a mosquito had
trodden on him. He brought Oakley, with many a
bang, and half a hundred cuts, scrapes and bruises,
to the mouth of the abandoned shaft, and the poor
devil had just time to realise what was happening
when he was tumbled down, neck and crop. The
hole, though deep, was narrow, and Oakley, as an
old miner, had just sense enough to do the best he
could for himself. He threw his body across, put
out arms and legs, and worked a brake, striking
footholes about 30ft. down, and bringing up there.
There was silence for a moment; then Rock’s face
appeared at the moonlit mouth of the shaft. “Be-
low!” he said. “Below! There’s company for you
at last, Bill.” Oakley succeeded in making the sur-
face again, but not without a long struggle. Then
he left, minus his swag. It seemed to him that
Sheehan was right, after all.
“Mulga Bill” : Talk about Australian wit
and humor! Get thee to a pubbery in an up-
country town at race-time. • First night at our re-
cent outbreak all the boots outside the various bed-
rooms were mixed up and filled with water. (Roars
of laughter!) Next night, mere ordinary horse-
play ; nothing brilliant until dawn, when about a
dozen roosters, that had been smuggled into bed-
rooms by the talent began to crow long and dis-
mally. (Shrieks!) The third night a barrel was
rolled into the front corridor and a blithered
member of the comic coterie was mounted on a
“borrowed” horse to leap over it. When the con-
vulsions of merriment had faded, the blithered
humorist was escorted to the local hospital (he
was still there when I left a fortnight later), and
the overjoyed pubkeeper had collected a cheque
covering the cost of the damage twice over. No-
body grudged it ; it was the Funniest Time on Earth.
BETWEEN TWO
DRINKS.
THIRSTY SAM: “ How
happy could I be
with either.”
Victoria isn’t finished yet. At Merbein,
just beyond the terminus of the Mildura line, so situ-
ated that 70 miles of easily-built railway would
serve it, there is an area of nearly threequarters of
a million acres of cultivable land lying practically
unused. It is held on grazing lease, terminable by
Ministerial decision. R. Barr-Smith, F. J. Fisher
and Arthur Crozier use it to run a few sheep, whereas
a thousand families should, and some day soon will,
be living comfortably on it. The annual rental of
this principality amounts to £4436. The Weary
Ministry could throw open these blocks to-morrow
and have a railway built in six months ; but it is
too busy battling for State rights against the Ref-
erendum proposals to attend to its own proper
business in that way.
THE SHEEP CURE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The report on inebriate retreats, Mr. Murray re-
marked, should be sufficient to indicate to the public
that a valuable work was being done on lines
of economy,. . The Lara institution found it economi-
cal to raise its own mutton, and he believed firmly
in attaching as large areas as possible to inebriate
asylums, since, quite apart from the economical
standpoint, it afforded the patients healthful exer-
cise.—Melbourne Argus.
When you chase the jumbuck, cursing, eke from
hither unto Von,
And you find, at yon arriving, that the greasy
blighter’s gone,
And 3'ou have your sweat for nothing, while about
a quarter-peck
Of yard-dust has proceeded down your warm, per-
spiring neck —
It’s then you think a cool, damp thought of how,
when you were dry,
You’d turn to beer for quenching, in the wicked days
gone by.
Blue ruinwards the track was never dusty, never
rough,
When you browsed among the beer-leaves, and you
never had enough—
When you chased the long, moist sleever up and
down the dear old town.
Looking fondly, full and foolish, on the beer when
it was brown;
And a pint of beer, at thruppence, gave much joy
for little cash....
Hi! Oh, hi! you cow! (Bump, wallop.) Oh, you
crimson— (biff and bash) !
Noon, night, early in the morning, when the street
was whirling round.
And your truly trifling troubles in a sea of beer
were drowned —
All the time you wore good clobber: one clean collar
every day,
And a shirt-front dawn-reflecting, as you corkscrewed
on your way.
Ah, j'ou never thought that some time, when your
beer had done you brown,
You’d win back in shirt-’n’-trousers, chasing jum-
bueks up and down!
Long the day, and dry and dusty, beerless, brown
and bustling now,
When you labor with the ready sweat of sheep-work
on 3 - our brow.
Long the day and short the liquor, as in clouds Of
dust you go
Kp and down, and in and out, and round and round,
and to and fro ;
Yet it’s cheap you are, and useful, with salvation
drawing near,
When you mend - our broken manhood, chasing sheep
instead of beer.

“Eryander”: That “snake-sense” men-
tioned by “K.H.K.,” (B. 10/4/’l3) is not so very
uncommon. I have known several men who pos-
sessed it, and one woman—-the last unerringly. She
was the wife of a friend with whom I used to stay
sometimes, near Tambo, in Gippsland. Mrs. Blank
always knew when a snake came near the house;
or, at all events, when she said “Snake!” snake it
always was. One sweltering summer night, when
her husband was going to throw open the front door,
she stopped him. “Get the gun, Tom,’’ she said,
“and I’ll hold a candle low down. There’s a snake
somewhere just outside.” Cautiously the husband
opened the door, and the light fell at once on a big
brownie, coiled comfortably right upon the step. Of
course he bolted, but the charge of No. 6 caught up
to him. I asked the lady if her warnings were
founded on any special bodily sensations. “Not
that I am aware of,” she said. “I just seem sud-
denly to know ; that’s all I can explain about it.
It began with me as a girl of 14, when I found a
tiger snake in my bed. Ever since I’ve had this
peculiar instinct, or whatever you like to call it.”

“Platman”: “Wal” rushed in where a
well-seasoned beach-comber would have feared to
tread when he recently denied my statement that
Cooktown (Q.) blacks and those on the east coast of
the Peninsula did not use the boomerang. Writing
from the spot, I can state definitely that the weapon
is not used here (Cooktown), or by the Annan
blacks. Also, in the collection of native weapons in
the Sydney Museum a card over the boomerang col-
lection reads: “Unknown in Tasmania
and (perhaps) in York Peninsula.”
Joseph Banks in his journal notes
that the natives of Sting Ray (now
Botany) Bay had short wooden
swords shaped like a scimitar, but
although he, with Cook’s party, spent
some five weeks on the banks of the
Endeavor, and gives a description of
the weapons used (with a diagram of
the throwing stick or womera), he
makes no mention of anything resem-
bling the boomerang. It is a fair de-
duction that even at that period there
were none amongst the local blacks,
for it is hardly likely such a careful
and accurate observer as Banks would
have failed to note what is recognised
as one of the most peculiar of primi-
tive weapons. Some of the old hands
state that in all probability the exhi-
bitions of boomerang throwing seen by
“Wal” were given by boys recruited
from the Johnstone River or Palm
Islands. If the old hands are wrong,
I don’t know wno “vVal” is going to fall back on.
Personally., I always fall back on an old hand.
