“Sardonyx”: Seems they're talking of
dynamiting the flying-foxes that have found hitherto
a peaceful home in the National Park, near Sydney.
Dynamite is no use in such a case, unless a big
charge could be fired right over the top of a roosting-
tree. Have known a charge of “dinny” exploded
at the foot of a tree. Tore a terrific hole in the
ground, but did little damage aloft. The most
effective “fox” antidote is just plain gunpowder—
with a charge of No. sin front of it. Nothing like
the gun, as I ascertained while doing my share to-
wards a total of over 3000 corpses for five gur.s in
about three hours. And the bright smell haunts me
still.
“A.A.G.” : The casualness of Black Sister
in the matter of adding to the population, as men-
tioned by “Leichhardt'’ recently, is correct enough
Knew a Maori woman who climbed out of a buggv
near Hastings (M.L.), and inside half an hour
climbed back with an addition to the passenger list.
And she hadn’t borrowed it, either. But White
Sister can be just as casual, as witness the case not
long ago in Gisborne (M.L.), where a European girl
went out with her boy, left him suddenly, and in a
short period returned with a chee-ild that she had
given birth to in the scrub. Her companion gave
evidence later that there was nothing unusual about
her when she came back to him, except that “she
looked a bit cronk.”
“Bulloak” : His name was Count Boris
Grovenski before Poland became too hot for the
soles of his feet and he took to shepherding sheep
for Morrison, of Australia. He was tall, thin, very
dignified; and while his sheep grazed he studied
books in Greek and other cob-webbed languages.
Naturally, the reprimand of Morrison was frequent
and characteristically torrid; but his Excellency
accepted it in calm silence. Even the longest Count
will turn, however, and one day Grovenski revoluted.
Drawing himself up, his aged and crumbling topper
in one hand, the other hand pressed to his heart, he
bowed and said: “Have you quite feenished, Meester
Morreeson?” Morrison reckoned he had. “Well,”
says the Count, “I have von leetle commun-i-casiong
to make to you. Shephaird your perludy sheep your-
seelf. Ihe last I saw of him he was heading slowly
for the horizon with his library on his back.
SKETCHES AT OUR BUSH PUB.
An impromptu candlestick.
“Sarto”: Permit me to settle this barb-
wire fence scuffle. It is positively unwise to sav
that a small swag couldn’t be pushed through a five-
wire fence (with two barbs) erected 10 years be-
fore. I know of two separate fences built of sleeper
posts and six wires (three plain and three prickly),
with the difference in their structure that one has
two iron droppers in each panel (24ft. panel in each
fence), while the other hasn’t. The fence with the
droppers (erected about five years) is a hard propo-
sition to a small-sized jim-jam to get through, while
the other one (erected, say, three years), in which
any two of the wires can be made" to meet, would
give easy access to a battleship if it wasn't too
large. Each of these fences encloses a well-grassed
paddock in the middle of a common, while outside
is practically void of grass; yet there is not one
instance on record of stock having been ruined on
either. Any wire fence (plain or barb) without
droppers will slacken; any with droppers will defy
Old Nick to get through after his tail, provided the
posts will stand.

S.S.S. Sivani, the old Ki-wai corporal
of police, told me how the Fly River men get
birds of paradise. Armed with ' bows and arrows
they creep to their locality (where the dancing
trees are) before the birds home at sunset. The
hunters, climbing a high and leafy tree, stay there
ill night, and when the birds begin to dance in
the hours at dawn they shoot them, silently. One
arrow will often spike three birds. At
Mauviani village I saw a rose-pink bird,
and traded it from a native for lib. of
tobacco. I did not know it was against
the law to purchase them, and, in my
youthful innocence, showed it to a newlv-
made and most conscientious R.H. lie
shook his head sagely and said I must
g,ve it back or go to gaol. I would
sooner have bought his old gaol for cash
and given it to a blind beggar for road
material, but he wouldn’t listen to
reason. I never saw another like it (an-
other bird, I mean, for gaols like his
were as common as lies). Up at Lake-
kamu, amongst the mountains, I saw four
Heginas dancing on a bow before a back-
ground of purple orchids, and a royal
Miie butterfly, big as a man’s palm, silently flut-
tering in the vicinity. It was the poetry of God.
Even when Croesus, the richest monarch of his time,
was up a tree, he was not attired like one of these.

F.11.R.”: Talking about bees recently,
someone remarked that honey was about the purest
of our foods. Well, if bees are domiciled within
miles of an ordinary farm, I’ll back them as scaven-
gers against flies. Look casually around the foulest
corner of a pigsty, and there you’ll find the busy
beast. In drains, cowyards, or any of the many un-
savory , , slimy corners that abound on an ordinary
farm, 1 11 back the bees to be busiest on any sunny
morning. If the bee is the clean and spotless fowl
that she is alleged to be, what is she doing there?
C iows, hyenas, alligators, sharks, etc., are denounced
as scavengers, but I never saw a shark flying about a
pigstye, or a hyena on the wing near a pile of
carrion. I believe the bee leads a double life, and
calls herself a blowfly in her dissipated moments in
order to-disguise her identity.
THE DARLING RIVER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I am the Roadway and the Route
Whence Life’s essentials come;
I bring Invention’s ripened fruit—
The segment and the sum.
1 am the Harbinger of Hope ;
1 am the Commonweal,
\\ hen through the Gates my steamers grope
With mud from stem to keel 1
Because these boats dread dangers dare
I bring you bread and meat,
The dainty robes your women wear,
The daintier things you eat.
The lager brewed in Adelaide,
A thousand miles away,
Upon your tables shall be made
The order of the day!
And when the western world is still,
And all its troubles cease,
It shall be mine alone to fill
Your fragrant pipes of peace.
From where the sprawling camels swing
(My drab co-partners these!)
To homestead and to camp I bring
My sunset argosies.
The ocean-roaming “salts” may laugh
My narrow paths to scorn,
But I am Famine’s cenotaph,
And Cornucopia’s Horn !
The deep-sea skippers all may sneer
Through tempest-trodden brine ;
But this I ask: Can any steer
As loyally as mine?
Which one can ride upon the flood,
Soup-thick, and inches high ?
And could he fniss the murd’rous mud,
Or dangers dark defy ?
It’s easy, with a thousand leagues
Of charted ocean room,
To dodge the Storm-God’s wild intrigues
And Davy’s crack of doom.
But these brown men of mine, I vow,
Are braver than the best
Who ever aimed a liner’s prow
North, south, or east, or west.
They sport no gold medallions, and
They wear no diamond pins ;
But, oh ! how -well they understand—
And suffer for their sins !
N.S.W.
R. J. CASSIDY.


A DOUBTFUL PLEASURE.
MELBOURNE, Monday.—The Liberals in Victoria will have
most to fear from a superfluity of candidates... Mr. C. James, a
young lawyer, is challenging Mr. John Quick in Bendigo, and
challenging coincidently the Labor candidate. Entering the
field as a Freetrader in a district which is supposed to be moth-
eaten with Protection, he is gaining more support than either
party cares about. .. The younger voters, and the women voters
in particular, seem to be giving acceptance to Frectrade views
to an extent that was not quite hoped for by the propagandists.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Joe Cook has got back on all the news-
papers that ever peaked at him. In his
nomination paper he described himself as a
journalist.
[S. IT. Prior, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
Dr. A. K. Newman, of Wellington, M.L.,
has completed his bonk, “Who Are the
Maoris?” a job which has been on his mind
these 15 years past. He takes up Percy
Smith’s swag where M.L.’s ex-Surveyor-
General dropped “Hawaiki” in the Malay
Archipelago, and carries it right up to the
top of Northern India, claimed to be the
original home of the Henare family. New-
man is a politician of long standing (that is
to say he started to sit in the House of
Reps, in 1884), and is one of two members
of the Tory party whom louseph Ward never
forgave for their criticisms during the
trouble in connection with the winding Up
of. the Colonial Bank. (Seeing that the
editor of this paper had his umbrella stolen
in the Colonial Bank’s Dunedin office in
1883 he doesn’t feel very forgiving either.)
Newman is a cheerful identity, a good sport,
and remembers with difficulty that he is 64
years of age.
About the strangest jest the Northern
Territory has seen happened the other day,
when the Director of Lands (salary £B5O a
year) was called out on strike as a member
of the Australian Workers’ Union, and put
on picket duty outside Government House.
And the second-best jest happened when an
editor who had criticised the local Judge
was brought up before the embittered plain-
tiff, and as the Judge found on his own
unanimous verdict that he had done nothing
to deserve the criticism the editor got the
shock of his life. In other words, he was
fined £lOO and sent to gaol pending pav-
ment. Then Darwin rose as one man, and
as Tory and Laborite and priest and atheist
all wrnnted to subscribe towards/ the fine
it was necessary to restrict the amount that
each man might pay. So nossibly the Judge
also got the shock of his life.

Weird advt. from Mortlake Dispatch
(Vic.) :
NOTICE.
If the person who poisoned my two dogs does not
apologise within 7 days, I will pray that he or she
may die of cancer.—A A .
Godfrey Watson, starter to the V.R.C. and
V.A.T.C., who died the other day, was one
of a band of ultra-sporting brothers. There
were nine of them. They were all born in
Ireland; and they could all do one or more
things in the horsey line superlatively well.
Godfrey was perhaps the finest horseman of
the lot. He rode innumerable steeplechases
in Australia, and never one bad race.
Once, while negotiating the big fences at
Caulfield, his horse’s belly-band broke. The
journey was only half finished. Watson
somehow got the saddle overboard, and
finished the race bare-back. There are not
many who can cling to a horse’s naked hide
over so much as one tall obstruction, let
alone a dozen or more. An elder brother
in Ireland hunts the Meath Hounds. His
language when annoyed is dreadful. Incident-
ally, he mostly is annoyed when hunting. As
a four-in-hand whip he is considered by good
judges to have no superior anywhere. Tom
Watson, who starts horses in and about Syd-
ney, has hunted, steeple-chased, and played
polo during most of his life. He is too stout
now to ride anything smaller than a dinosaur
across country, but the old interest in the
game abides. No country but Ireland could
evolve a family just like this.
LOOKING FOR WORK.
The first Federal note, which is to be issued with
some ceremony to-day, is to be numbered by the
Hon. Judith Denman, daughter of the Governor-
General. A presentation will be made of the note
as a memento of the occasion.
DIRTY DAN: “ Look 'ere, mister! This num-
berin' notes ain’t no womans work—I don’t
mind doin' it on the same terms."
Adelaide’s veteran philanthropist, Edwin
Smith, has been throwing money about
lately. He has just started, at the age of
83, on an English trip. Before leaving he
collected £5OOO for the Blind, Deaf and
Dumb. He gave £2OOO himself, got £lOOO
from another veteran, Barr Smith, and
struck his friends for the balance in hun-
dreds and fifties. As soon as he had gone,
his attorneys announced that he had left
cheques of his own for pretty well every
other charity in Adelaide —about £5500 in
that lot. Beer was the foundation of the
Smith fortune, and judicious investment has
done the rest.
From the man whose name is where the
law says it should be: —
The Australian backbone was handed the following
crumb- of discomfort, in regard to the proposed log of
the Rural Workers’ Union, in the Argus of the 28th
ult. .
“The milker is to do nothing but milk, wash up
the cans and clean the bails. The laborer who grows
the feed is another kind of specialist. His particular
duties are to plough and harrow, and for this, under
the Rural Workers’ log, he is to receive £4 3s. per
week.”
Looking up the references I keep for such occasions,
I find from a previous Argus statement that a week’s
wages for a ploughman (48 hours) are two golden
quids, and not £4 35., as stated. However, in a
subsequent Argus computation, the allowance for
“looking after horses” is put down at 7s. 6d. ; for
keep, 165.; for “overtime, say, 10 hours,” 12s. 6d. ;
and for allowance, when employed less than four
weeks, 6s. This puffs up the total to an improbable
£4 2s. for a week of 58 hours, which is not quite
Is. sd. per hour for a heavy 10 hours; and it quite
omits to include the 40s. a week which the horses’
fodder will cost and the 635. which the vet. will
charge when one of the animals suffers from dry
bible or some other weird complaint. If these items
are included, it will be as plain as mud that the
ploughman’s wages are £8 9s. per week. Meantime,
the farmer-man, newly-arisen in the morning from
a deep sleep, reads his Argus, and having no time to
sort out lies from truth, votes against Labor as
usual, and then goes peacefullv to feed the pigs.
[Frank Henty, Glen Iris, Victoria.]
The Bulletin’s “Oldest Inhabitant” en-

From “Rosna” in London :
Premier Peake and Speaker O’Loughlin of S.A.,
and Victoria’s Premier Watt have arrived in a bunch
to look at this little isle, and the local reporter is
wrestling with Australian geography in the old, old
way. The Westminster Gazette called Mr. Watt
“Premier and Leader of the Liberal Party of the
State of Victoria, N.S.W.” Another scribe, in a
violent outburst of rhetoric, said he had taken on
the mantle of Elijah, was the Randolph Churchill of
the Victorian Assembly, a Prince Rupert in debate
and quite Disraelian in character. He forgot to
mention he was also the Aristophanes of Melbourne,
the Solon of Australasia, the Julius Caesar of the
Southern Hemisphere, and the Apollo Belvidere of
Canberra, but these points may be noticed later.
One of our Junior Iconoclasts is inspired
by Shakspeare celebrations, St. George’s
Society doings, etc., to Blasphemy and Dis-
loyalty : —■
How few know the facts about the patron saint
of Merrie England. He was a Cappadocian by birth,
and by trade a swindling army-contractor in the
pork-supplying line. He was likewise that old-time
Bishop whom Edward Gibbon gibbetted as a prize
rascal and confidence man. When the Arians got the
upper hand of the Athanasians, George was made
Primate of Egypt. He was wealthy, having acquired
a fortune by supplying bad bacon at high
prices to the defenders of his country. As Arch-
bishop of Alexandria, he got a monopoly of the nitre,
salt, paper and funeral trades. He also made a bit
here and there as an informer. And he was the origina-
tor of the house-tax. The speed and diversity with
which George garnered in, boodle, in those days,
fairly stupefied his contemps. Finally, the people
arose and threw him into prison, and, in doing so,
man-handled him so severely that he died. His body
was cast into the sea, a.d. 363. Pope Gelasius
(a.d. 494) was the first Catholic to acknowledge St.
George, and place him among the martyrs. How-
ever, such distinguished Cappadocian saints as Basil
and the Gregories were ignorant of the holiness of
their companion to the last. His ghost got into the
hagiology of both the Latin and the Greek churches,
and finally he became the national saint of England.
Tradition did much for him. The Arian persecutor
was transformed into a young and handsome prince,
a soldier martyred at the command of the Roman
Emperor Diocletian, a kind of Christian Perseus, of
whom a thousand prodigies were reported. Among
other exploits he was represented to have slain ail
’immense and fearsome beast, yclept “The Dragon,”
and saved the life of a king’s daughter just as this
dreadful minotaur was about to devour her. The
Crusaders first introduced him to Europe. In 1348
Edward 111. of England made George of Cappadocia
the patron saint of the Order of the Garter; and
from there he marched swiftly to his present proud
position.
“Hori” takes some snapshots:—
After taking a long course of the Dominions
Royal Commish, I sum the members up this way.
Britisher Lorrimer, Canadian Foster and Maorilander
Sinclair ask the most sensible questions; Tatlow
the fewest and Garnett the least important. New-
foundland Bowring is the politest. Garnett is the
most bumptious and cross-examines witnesses like
a police court lawyer until he can bamboozle them
to give the answer he requires. He is also the Beau
Brummel of the crowd and looks like Wilson Barrett
in “The Silver King.” Haggard is the flag-wagger ;
he uses the best language and asks questions spas-
modically. Vincent is a typical big-gun English
chairman and great at curbing too voluble witnesses.
Donald Campbell (Australia) is the youngest.
Vincent is the handsomest and Haggard the plainest.
Haggard’s trousers are a sculptured nightmare. They
resemble the trousers to be found on statues to de-
parted suburban mayors. He can put them on to
face either way and it makes no difference. Secre-
tary Harding upholds the dignity of Hold Hingland.
His attitude suggests a personal affront that so
much of the Commission’s time should be wasted on
mere colonials.
The way Parliamentary candidates describe
themselves in their nomination papers is farci-
cal but it’s the people’s fault. For instance,
W. G. Spence describes himself as a miner.
Now, it is 35 years or thereabouts since
Spence heaved a pick underground, and the
fine old man—7o or more now—couldn’t be
a miner again if he tried. Josiah Thomas
is another “miner.” and it is close on quar-
ter of a century since he did his last little
bit of sulphide gouging. As a matter of
tact, both of these men, and dozens besides
have made a profession of politics ; yet men-
tion professional politician and people shy
off. though if you asked them to go to any-
body else but a professional toothsmith to
get a tusk drawn they would suspect there
was lunacy in the family.
IS. H. Prior. The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]

BARRISTER
STAR EE, OF
MELBOURNE.
William Thompson Busby, of the Com-
mercial Bank of Sydney, who died last week,
was son of Dr. George Busby, who went to
Bathurst, in 1532, as Colonial Surgeon.
The doctor’s wife (who died in 1907, aged 97
years, 75 of them spent in the “City of the
Plains”), described her first journey over the
Mountains in a pamphlet a v e ar or two
before her death. Penrith was the first sfim;
the second night was spent at Springwood;
“The Weatherboard” was the next; Mount
York then, followed by Wallerawang, and
Sorn Bank, the home of the Campbells. The
house built for her at Bathurst still stands
on the bank of the Jordan Creek, in Ilowick-
street. Another Busb.v son, George, was
manager of the Commercial Bank at Bath-
urst for many years.
April 27 was the 17th anniversary of the
death of Henry Parkes, who began to be a
Minister in N.S.W. as far back as January,
1866, and finished the job of being Premier
of N.S.W. in October, 1891. He was Premier
five times, like Cowper and Ilobertson.
Martin was Premier three times and Pibbs
three times. Henry Parkes’s youngest son,
Cobden, will presently come of age, when
the annuity granted by Parliament to aid
the widow and family will cease. An appli-
cation, it is understood, will be made to the
N.S.W. Legislature to continue the pension
to the widow. There is also a daughter un-
married. for whom, it is suggested, that pro-
vision should be made. Only the children of
the second wife were provided for in the
Act. The offspring of the first marriage
did not want help, and the third wife was
childless.

more reporting on the Greater Sydney mat-

him, no matter who takes his place; for

SIMPLY DREADFUL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Probably very few of the Residents knew that the
Governor of Victoria (Sir John Fuller) spent some
time in Hamilton on Friday.
-—Awesome whisper in Hamilton (Vic.) paper.
They never knew!
Those loyal folk of Hamilton,
Although the sun, as usual, shone,
And skies were blue,
They never knew !
The Governor was in the town,
And in the streets walked up and down
On his OWN feet! A splendid sight!
He first advanced the left. The right
Then swung ahead, ere something cleft
The atmosphere! His peerless left!
So he progressed o’er ruts and bumps
In wonderful vice-regal jumps,
Spurning the earth he hopes to rule
Like any common walking fool!
It is a dreadful thing to say;
The Governor was there that day,
And no one knew
(Except a few).
They might have gazed on Greatness there.
But no one told them ! Unaware,
His Ex. passed through
And no one knew!
victoria.
FRANK R.
EVERYBODY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Councillor Bell, of Ballarat (objecting to the
flouting of St. George’s flag in citizens’ faces before
the city hall) : “There are other flags beside the
English. These Englishmen think they are every-
body.”
Another alderman: “They are everybody! I say
they are everybody!”
There may be sundry other lands
Than dear old England, it is true;
There may be certain other bands
Of people scattered ’neath the blue;
But they are neither here nor there —
They’re nothing and they’re no one, sir.
We do admit at times the French
Are in existence, as it were,
And that the Germans, too, entrench
Upon the earth ; but you’ll concur
With us when we declare all such,
In Heaven’s eye,' are nothing much.
He’s everybody who can say
With truth he’s English through and through.
The rest are human in a way,
On sufferance—a paltry crew
We merely suffer to • exist.
Wiped out, they never would be missed.
Creation’s lord’s an Englishman;
He stands alone, with little fuss.
That’s why, throughout the cosmic plan
The gods are all superfluous.
The Superman, grown to the full.
Is with us now. His name is BULL!
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The State revenue returns for the month
make no fresh disclosures. The Gadarene swine are
still rushing down the steep place with the bad
bump at the bottom. The shiftless N.S.W. Govern-
ment has on various accounts spent £22,919,029 this
year, or £4,030,236 more than it did in the cor-
responding 10 months of last year, and most of the
extra money has, of course, been borrowed. The
straight-out loan expenditure has reached £7,136,747.
The Government is now largely loafing on casual
overdrafts; the revenue and loan accounts, which
were in credit three years ago, are overdrawn to the
extent of £6,482,676. Money has been squandered
so recklessly, and such wretched value has been got
for it, that the railways already begin to talk of
increased fares and freights. Increased costs are put
forward as the excuse; but the increased costs are
very largely the result of the fictitious prosperity
which has followed the slopping out of mill ons of
borrowed money. While this money is still being
slopped out, it is easy enough for the public to come
up to the scratch and pay the interest. When the
slop stops and the interest doesn’t stop there will
be a squeal.
Victoria doesn’t show what it spends. In its re-
venue returns the old spring is missing. For 10
months the receipts were £7,920,402, or only
£27,487 more than last year; and the Common-
wealth for last month alone paid it £26,652 more
than it did in April, 1912. In Queensland the
alleged surplus is now £496,551, or £207,500 less
than it was at this time last year. The increase in
railway revenue ( £220,438) hasn’t equalled the in-
crease in railway expenditure ( £229,543) ; and on
top of that there is £148,059 more interest to pay
on the public debt. Westralia has managed to pull
down its accumulated deficit from £396.495 to
£391,548 in the 10 months, though one can’t forget
Westralia’s vicious old habit of charging “repairs to
glass houses” and such-like to loan aceount-r—the
simplest of all ways of faking a surplus. S. Aus.
preserves its usual profound silence on a matter of
such utter unimportance as its financial position;
and Tasmania, while it doesn’t mention its out-
goings, confesses with pride that it has in 10
months collected £18,473 more from its own people,
while the special Commonwealth dole to the poor
sister just about doubles that amount.

With the A.M.P. every year is a record year. It
might have got old and fat and lazy, and it threat-
ened to. It was too far ahead of all others: it was
getting big figures too easily, and while it was doing
better than others, it was not doing as well for its
policy-holders as it should have done. Then came
the amalgamation of three other offices, and a general
shaking up, and the A.M.P. found itself blessed with
some real competition. After 54 years it had achieved
an annual income of £2,509,729; after another ten
the figures have mounted to £3,855,890. And that
is not the best part of it. When they were making
their great dash the Yankee offices piled on tre-
mendous figures ; but they did it at tremendous cost,
whereas the A.M.P., under somewhat similar circum-
stances, has actually reduced its expense rate. By one
method of reckoning, the figures have certainly crawled
up an inch or two during the past few years; but
they are still less than they were 10 years ago:—
Expenses Compared with Total Income.
1903 £8 17 0 per cent. 1908 £8 19 6 per cent'
1904 8 16 7 ~ 1909 8 12 11
1905 8 15 0 „ 1910 8 7 1
1906 817 2 „ 1911 815 2 ’
1907 8 16 5 „ 1912 8 i.y o
In 1911 there was a spring of £30,272 in the out-
goings and last year another of £16,259. But to a
great extent this was the result of the higher cost
of living, and a very proper consequent increase in
salaries, etc. ; and the office got some compensation
in the higher earnings of its own money. Further,
if the amount of new business is considered (and it
is the new business that costs the money) the society
is getting more for its outlay than ever it did in the
past 20 years. Allowing 7 p.c. for the cost of work-
ing the old business, and 3 p.c. for the new annuity
(single-premium) business, and assuming that the
balance of the exes, was absorbed in getting the new
business, this is how things have been going

The reduction from the figure shown for 1908 ought to
make policy-holders feel good; for reduced costs
mean bigger bonuses, other things being equal.
And, as mentioned above, other things are better
than equal. Thus, besides the reduction in costs,
there has been an improvement in interest earnings;
and that means bigger bonuses too. Bonuses come
from three sources: (1) a lower expense rate than
was assumed when the rate of premium was fixed •
(2) higher interest earnings than the actuaries
figured on; (3) a better death rate than was
assumed, which means the receipt of premiums for
more years than the mortality tables' had led the
office to expect—and that, again, is largely a matter
of carefully selecting the risks. The premiums which
the office expects to collect on the policies in force
total £31,024,847. Of that amount the actuaries
estimated that £6,337,262 would be required for
expenses—an average, that is, of 20.4 p.c. But the
actual expenses and sums written off last year were
only 14.5 p.c. of the premium income: the balance
\\ a® profit, available for bonuses. The actuaries
assumed that interest at the rate of 3 p.c. in some
cases and 34 p.c. in others would be earned. This
shows what was actually done:—
Interest Realised on Average Funds.
1903 £4 8 10 per cent. 1908 £4 9 4 per cent.
1904 4 9 0 „ 1909 4 9 8
1905 4 9 8 „ 1910 4 9 8
1906 4 9 7 „ L9ll 4 9 8
1907 4 911 „ 912 410 5
The difference between the assumed 3 or 34 p.c. and
the actual £4 10s. sd. p.c. is also profit, available
for bonuses. The balance of profit comes from the
favorable mortality experience before-mentioned. On
that point, however, no information is available for
the past year.
The first thing to be looked to in an insurance
office is solvency. The fact that an actuary makes
a report on which a big bonus is declared is not
in itself proof of solvency. There are two sides
to the business: the actuarial and the financial.
The actuary says—“ Assuming that your funds will
earn (say) 3 p.c., you ought to have so much put
by at this moment towards meeting future claims.
Anything more that you have is surplus available
for distribution.” The financial man says—“We
have so much,” and the actuary takes his word for
it. If his word is good, all right; if it isn’t, the
more inflated the bonus now, the worse the trouble
presently. The quality of the assets —whether they
are worth and are likely to go on being worth 20s.
in the £—is therefore of the utmost importance.
This shows the distribution of the funds now and 10
years ago:—
1902. 1912.
Loans on mortgage . . 46.8 p.c. 40.5 p.c.
Public securities . . . . 23.0 ~ 37.2 ~
Premises .. .. .. 1-9 ~ 1.7 ~
Foreclosures . . . . 2.9 ~ 0.4 ~
Loans on policies . . . . 21.3 ~ 16.8 ~
Other assets .. .. 4.1 ~ 3.4 ~
The changes have been all for the better. Public
securities are the best securities there are ; and there
has been a fine increase under that head. Foreclosures
are, on the whole, about the worst; and these have
been got rid of until hardly any are left. The
premises account is commendably small. Furniture,
etc., has been written right off. Loans on policies
have shrunk ; and since one of the worst things you
can do with an insurance policy is to pawn it, that
shrinkage is good too, though the loans may be
profitable business for the society. Altogether the
position from this point of view is admirable. The one
complaint that can be made is that though the Invest-
ment Fluctuation Fund amounts to only £36,463,
which isn’t nearly large enough to provide for con-
tingencies in a business of this size, only £IO,OOO
has been added to it during the year, though
£22,042 of profit was made on the sale of fore-
closed properties and other securities. It would
have been far better to have given the Investment
Fluctuation Fund the benefit of the whole lot. The
actuary, however, makes amends by recommending
that £155,000 of his surplus should be transferred
to a contingency account; but that is an internal
reserve for the protection in the first place of the
actuarial calculations.
As to bonuses, here is a comparison stretching
back 25 years:—
1888-1893 (boom years) .. 40.3 p.c. of premiums

But for the 114th time The Bulletin remarks that
big bonuses, though important, are not everything.
In making comparisons between various offices, for
instance, these things have also to be taken into
account: (1) The amount of the premium. It is
quite easy for an office to put a bonus back into
the policy-holder’s trousers pocket if it first takes
in out of his vest. (2) The amount of the loading
for some alleged defect. Some offices load one-third
of their policy-holders, and don’t always mention the
fact either. (3) Whether premiums are reckoned on
“nearest birthday” or “next birthday.” It isn’t a
large matter; but the office which charges you as if
you were 40 when you are actually only 394 can
naturally afford to do a bit better for you in the
way of bonuses than the one which reckons you only
39. These remarks are, however, thrown in on
general principles, and have no special application
to the A.M.P.
industrial department.

In banking more than in most things the fat pig
gets the grease. The little fellow, no matter how
strong he may be for his size, has to battle for his
business: to the big, imposing fellow a steady stream
flows automatically. The ’Asia is one of the big
fellows. When the banking crisis happened 20 years
ago it didn’t have to close its doors, and that gave
it a big lift. Out of the profits it made in those
days it was content to pay a comparatively small
dividend, and used the balance to write down assets
And probably they weren’t a bad lot of asests in the
first place, because during the land boom, the ’Asia
had a general manager who didn’t lose his head—
and then a railway accident cost him his life before
he could see the wisdom of his policy. After that
hard writing down there came a run of good seasons
and high prices, and the bank made a heap of money
by getting rid of securities at prices far in excess
of their wntten-down book values. Out of its gorgeous
profits it started to write down its premises account;
and to-day, though its office nroperties must be
worth close on a million, they stand in the books at
only £40,212, and next year the item will probably
disappear altogether. Even now this means that the
bank has an undisclosed reserve of certainly over
£750,000. Little things like that have naturally
attracted custom —-so much custom that the ’Asia
could afford to turn away any that it had its
doubts about.
Meantime it saw that something would fall pre-
sently, and it kept itself in a position to help those
of its customers who were liable to be hurt if they
didn’t get assistance. Something did fall lately, and
during the year the bank has been able to increase
its advances by no less than £1,332,901 —and the
bank that can' stand by its customers like that in
had times is the bank to make friends, and inci-
dentally profits. The drain on the funds has of
course been considerable. This shows that and a
few other things:—
Oct., 1911. Oct., 1912.
Notes .. £227.860 £184,056 Dec. £43,804
Bills, etc... 2,753,640 2,722,182 Dec. 31,458
Deposits . . 17,414,474 17,906,493 Inc. 492,019

Advances .. 15,892,891 17,225,792 Inc. 1,332,901

That draw of £758,404 on the liquid assets left the
bank with 35 p.c. of the public’s money represented
by cash and as good as cash. It isn’t a dangerously
low figure; yet the management probably saw that
deposits weren’t likely to grow much, while some
customers were still liable to want help, so an issue
of £400,000 of capital was lately decided on.
The earnings, calculated on capital and visible
reserves combined, are extraordinary:—
Apl., 1908 £l3 4 10 p.c. Oct., 1910 £ll 15 3 p.c.
Oct., 1908 11 16 9 „ Apl., 1911 11 11 8 „
Apl., 1909 10 15 10 „ Oct., 1911 11 16 11 „
Oct., 1909 10 16 9 „ Apl , 1912 11 16 2 „
Apl., 1910 11 12 10 „ Oct., 1912 12 1 9 „
When a bank which doesn’t go in for notoriously
risky business can show a return of that size, it is
as good proof as one can want that the assets are
not overvalued. In fact, it is a pretty large hint
that assets are under-valued and that there is a
considerable internal or contingency reserve stuffed
away somewhere.

Besides that dividend of 5 p.c., payable on May 15,
a bonus of 5 p.c. will, according to present inten-
tions, be payable on July 1. Simultaneously the
first payment of 10s. per share will be due on anew
issue of 200,000 shares at par (£1). The£3so,ooo
of reserves transferred in 1911 brought up the capi-
tal to £700,000. The cash bonus (7£ p.c.) paid
last year was accompanied by the issue of a further
£IOO,OOO of capital at par. That made 800,000
shares; so in July existing holders will be entitled
to one new share for every four old ones, and the
cash bonus will provide 4s. out of every 10s. pay-
able as the first moiety on the new shares. The
second moiety will not be due till January 1, 1914;
nevertheless the new shares will rank equal with the
old ones with respect to the dividend which the
directors have hopes of paying at the end of the
current year. They “anticipate being able to pay
regular moderate dividends,” they say. On past re-
cords, The Bulletin can t accuse them of being un-
duly optimistic,. Asa matter of fact, the past year’s
profit was a shade over 27 p.c. on the actually paid
capital ( £397,500). Still the co. deserves to make
a very fine return on its capital because of the large
sums which it has consistently laid by. In the last
six years in which 8 p.c. was paid to shareholders,
the latter got £138,000, while no less than £160,303
was either put to reserves or written off special
accounts. How much is written off as a general
thing the long-headed management doesn’t mention.
During the last two years the book value of the
shipping asset has moved on from £191,148 to
£211,140, or an increase of less than £20,000;
and the’ Montoro alone cost at least sixpence more
than that. Certainly the Guthrie was sold; but
probably the ancient Guthrie stood at about 4d. in
the books, and what it fetched has been either added
to the disclosed profits or dropped into a stocking
somewhere to prawvide for eonteengencies.
But though the cp. has put a lot of money into
the Java-Singapore shipping business, and has added
the Matarain and Montoro to its fleet for those ser-
vices during the, last Si years, the bulk of its funds
is still apparently in the island trade: —

Liabilities to the public ( £209,508) are no doubt
mainly trading debts. On the other side book debts
represent £340,713. They don’t go in much for
weekly bills and settlements in the islands. Of the
£137,306 shown as cash and investments, £116,930
is represented by “shares and interests in shipping,
insurance and other cos.” The insurance interest is
no doubt in the Queensland, which started as a
branch of 8., P. and Cos., and has made a remarkable
success of things. This paper doesn’t suppose the
co. has written up its original holding; and as the
shares which fcost 10s. in cash are now worth 455.,
there is a pleasant little contingency fund hidden
there, too. Altogether the concern reeks of money.
It did look a while ago as if it was sinking a lot of
capital in big ships for the Java-Singapore trade
which couldn’t give anything like the return the co.
was used to getting from the Pacific Islands trade;
but 8., P. and Cos. arid the Dutch line buried their
hatchet, and with bulging cargoes and a good pas-
senger traffic both ways, that line must be very pro-
fitable nowadays.

Elder, Smith and Cos. have in one form or another
been in business in S. Aus. for 70 years. The co.
is more than an agent; to thousands of country
people it is banker and business guide as well. With
branches all over S. Aus. and in many places in
western N. S. Wales, it has so woven itself into the
commercial lives of so many peoole that, naturallv
enough, it was repeatedly asked by clients to take
charge when the clients became too dead , ,

£525,269. As with the Melbourne and Sydney
trustee cos., the charges run from 1J p.c to 21 no

There are few cos. that do more for their monev than

co. has a paid capital of £20,000, with £BO 000

buy a table and a chair or so ; the balance is earn
ing interest in a public security. The profit from
the business itself is, therefore, all profit; none of
it is reallv interest on capital. Last vear the net

over 34 p.c. and the business provided 54 p.c.)—a

and remembering that most trustee cos. have to wait
for their fr-'ends to die before they can show much
profit. Nevertheless, the co. pays no dividend ■ it
knows, of course, that the trustee takes some risks
though they are not very formidable ones if the man-
agement is good, and it is going to accumulate some
reserves in order to make itself safe. They are a
prudent crowd, and just the solid sort to make a
good thing out of this class of business.

By the grace of Robert Philp, Townsville is the port
for some few hundred thousand square miles of coun-
try lying along the railway line that crawls out
through Charters Towers and Hughenden, past CIOII-
- and on almost to the N.T. border; also, the
harvest of the rich Ayr sugar lands come that wav.
And because Cloncurry has been brisk and the sugar
business has boomed (though it ought to have wilted
and died when T. Tanna went), Townsville has been
growing; also, it has been leaving the gas burning
with a lordly indifference about the bill. Hence the
gain in profit during the last two or three years;
hence, also, the call for new mains, plant, etc. If
the co. had had a more reasonable thirst for divi-
dends, it might have met these latter requirements
out of reserves; but the Northern habit is to collar
money when it is there. Fresh capital has, there-
fore, to be got in ; £25,000 was authorised a fpw
months ago, and all but 2110 shares were taken by
existing shareholders at par. The money will be
used to build new works, presumably to more or
less replace the old works. So far as the works
are to be displaced, they should have been written
down out of past profits ;. and this paper has doubts
whether that has been done to a sufficient extent.
If it hasn’t been done, one lot of works will pre-
sently be struggling to pay dividend on two lots of
capital. The position, nevertheless, isn’t plainly
bad, because depreciation has by no means been
wholly neglected. What, however, is pretty obvious
is that, in view of the big increase in capital—it was
only £15,000 in 1907 and will be £75,000 when
the last payments are made on the present issue—
the past percentage earnings can’t be relied on as a
guide to the future.

The profit is shown after various deductions have
been made on account of depreciation of plant; and
though the two dividends of 2s. each naid during the
last term were not earned, the figures would still be
satisfactory if they could be maintained for another
quarter-century. But there isn’t any hope of that.
The ore in sight is 220,800 tons, and, though a lot
of prospecting has been done, the ore developed in the
past 12 months has been only 31,873 tons, while
the extraction amounted to 94,000 tons. Even if
another 31,873 tons a year can be found, the mill
will have overtaken the supplies in 34 years. It is
very doubtful, though, whether much more ore will
be discovered. The bottom working level is at
1715 ft. ; there is a level at 1815 ft., but no payable
ore has been found there or underneath. Six months
ago the co. had hopes of a western body found on
the Central (Sulphide Corporation’s) boundary; but
exploration has been “for the most part unsuccess-
ful.” Further, costs are high, and as the co. will
have to scratch harder to get out the ore, they are
likely to go still higher. A considerable quantity
of the stuff is in the upper levels, and even if this
can be got out at all the job will be very expensive
owing to the ricketty state of these workings.
There is no reason to expect any success below the
1815 ft. There are lots of reasons for expecting the
opposite.
The net profit last half-year was a shade over 7s
per ton. Assuming that another 300,000 tons can be
got out at the same profit, there will be £105,000
in all available. Add to that the surplus of
liquid assets, £82,000; total, £187,000 = 375. 4d.
per share. The co. has no stored by-products like
most other Broken Hill mines. The richness of
those by-products was really the co.’s misfortune.
Its extraction of silver and lead in the lead mill
was always poor, and the zinc contents were high:
wherefore it was to Block 10 that the treatment
cos. turned for tailings in the early davs of zinc
extraction, when the processes were raw and the
prices poor. Nothing, therefore, is to be added to the
£187,000 on account of stored bv-oroducts. And
whether even £187,000 can be realised denends on
the prices of metals. During the last half-year the

for means of transport of the raw material, half-

Messrs. Mephan Ferguson Proprietary, Limited, of
Footscray, Victoria, have secured the contract for
the supply of steel pipes required in connection with
the duplication of the Bombay Water Supply. The
contract amounts to over £226,000, and the pipes
to be used are of the locking bar type, of which Mr.
Mephan Ferguson is the inventor and patentee.
Engineers will be interested to note that this Aus-
tralian invention has been adopted in preference to
other classes of piping, after critical and searching
enquiries as to the merits of the various types of
water pipes made in the world. It is worthy of
note that all machinery required to carry out this
work has been designed and manufactured at the
firm’s Footscray Engineering Works, and will be
shipped to Bombay, where the pipes will be manu-
factured.*
Messrs. Babcock & Wilcox, Ltd., have received
the order for the supply of all the steam generating
plant and accessories for the electrification of the
Victorian Government Railways. The acceptance of
this firm’s tender was strongly recommended by Mr.
Merz, the English expert. The contract amounts to
£258,000.*

In view of the higher level of prices for practically
all commodities —a rise due largely, no doubt, to
the greater abundance of gold and the consequent
cheapening’ of that metal, which is our one medium
of exchange—-the £ll for lead of a few years back
needn t be looked for nowadays. Still, some weak-
ening is highly probable, wherefore the figures
arrived at above, on the basis of last half-year’s re-
sults, may very likely not be realised. Besides the
money that may be got out of the mine and the
liquid assets that have been already accumulated,
there is the plant, of course ; but the scrap value of
mining plant at Broken Hill is not large. And it
is pretty certain that, in a final struggle to keep
alive, the co. will spend a few thousands on fruitless
prospecting. If, then, shareholders get back 375.
4d. per-share they will be lucky.
No other field of recent years ever had so
many enemies who called themselves friends as Ard-
lethan (N.S.W.). It was quite possible for it to pay
fair dividends on a moderate capitalisation to pro-
bably half a dozen cos. The first float, the Car-
pathia, was moderate. After that, they were mostly
outrageous. The rich stuff got on the surface, ab-
solutely no proof of richness at depth, was used to
bait the cruellest hooks that have been thrown to
the public for years past. For 6hows that had no
more connection with Ardlethan than Woolloo-
mooloo has with Darling Point, tens of thousands
were asked and paid to promoters and vendors who
had done nothing beyond hurriedly driving in a few
pegs. The Ardlethan group is in granite or por-
phyry. Scores of the other claims are in schist or
quartzite country. In the most unexpected places
anywhere within 20 miles of Ardlethan, you can
drop on to camps—a tent with a bit of a yard for
the horse. The man inside is a “prospector.” He
doesn't know one thing about prospecting, and he
generally admits it; but he’s “in with a good man
in the city.” The “good man in the city” is busy
looking for fools ; and the crop is good. Nineteen-
twentieths of these claims will never be worked.
There is nothing to work in them.
The daily papers have been terribly to blame.
Following their usual policy, they have allowed any-
one to fill them up with yarns about the field. The
“local correspondents,” of course, knew little of
mining and nothing of tin-mining. They have been
sending along the local gossip—and the local gossip
came always from somebody with something to sell.
And, thanks to the puffing, the sales have been made
all right. A local railway stationmaster has gone
for al2 months’ trip to Europe. A local police-
man has made thousands —the number of thousands
swells as you go along. What sort of a wicket the
local hotelkeeper is on can be imagined. There is
only one of him—with a “coffee palace” to keep
him company.
They have missed no chances, these boomsters.
They have actually had the gall to get photographs
of the wheat stacks printed and placarded as pic-
tures of ore stacks. Certainly, the ore stacks are
growing; and some of the people who are helping
them to grow may be very sorry when they are
shifted. While the stacks are there they can talk
of 30 or 40 p.c. ore. When the stuff is sampled
they will have to shut up. One investigator, who
was wandering round a few days ago, saw a man
busy filling up bags with strippings. An hour later,
the supply of bags having evidently run out, the un-
used strippings were being thrown on to the mullock
heap.
Ardlethan is 3 p.c. rich surface ore, in short
shoots, with, perhaps, a few permanent mines under-
neath ; the other 97 p.c. consists of equal parts of
bovine ignorance and rank swindle.
* * * *
Carpathia’s new manager—Ellis, formerly
of the Conrad —sends in his first report. One re-
mark he makes about the ore body in the No. 4
shaft has been jumped at by the boomsters. “I have
proved this line of lode for fully 100 ft., showing nice
ore, and running in a northerly direction.” “This,”
says one daily paper fluter, “shows that the ore the
No. 4 shaft is on is not a pipe.” Asa matter of
fact, the No. 4 shaft, in the manager’s own words,
“shows an ore body 6Aft. long by 2Aft. in width.”
The lode is on the contact of the porphyry and the
schist, and pipes of ore along its course may be ex-
pected. The fact that ore has been got 100 ft. away
from the shaft needn’t, therefore, surprise anybody.
But it certainly isn’t proof that the ore doesn’t
occur in pipes.
* » * *
The Sydney co. which owns that dread-
ful stringer, Mount David (N.S.W.), has acquired
the Little Marvel mine, and is sinking anew shaft
to a depth of 200 ft. At that level a crosscut will
be run in to the contact of tiie felsite with the
slate, where, of course, it is hoped to find a payable
reef. r ihe Little Marvel gave some good crusnings a
tew years since from old workings, with which it is
intended to connect the new shaft presently, more
for purposes of ventilation than anything else.
Rich quartz discovered in decomposed
sandstone near the surface of the Hawkins’ Hill Re-
ward lease (N.S.W.) has caused a good deal of ex-
citement. But people who get excited about a nar-
row streak, as much as 4in. wide in places, have no
of proportion. The discovery was made near
the Exhibition shaft. Frenchman's reef is also being
opened by the Hawkins’ Hill Reward Cos. A crush-
ing of 80 tons returned 3loz. of gold—which is a
different and, on the whole, a much better story.
A plat has been cut at the 190 ft. level in the Exhi-
bition shaft, and a crosscut will be run in to prove
three different reefs, including the rich vein be-
forementioned.
* * * *
March and April developments added fur-
ther lustre to Meekatharra (W.A.). Higher values
than had ever previously been met with were exposed
at 627 ft. in the Fenian. Ingliston Consols reported
the intersection of a good payable lode at 750 ft. ;
and the Ingliston Extended, which has lately had
more than its share of water and other troubles,
managed to drain the 300 ft. level and sink a winze
27 ft. in ore worth many ounces per ton. Commo-
dore is close to 400 ft., where good values are ex-
pected, and the Kyarra is getting down as fast as
possible to 300 ft. Recent developments in the
higher levels have been quite up to expectations.
And, finally, the March yield fronS the field was a
record.
The substantial rise in Golden ’Shoe shares
is due to improved values in the No. 3 lode at the 13th
and 14th levels. At the former point the ore for
748 ft. south of the main crosscut assayed from 255.
to 325. per ton for the full width of the drive. Then
from 748 ft. to 785 ft. values improved to 85s. per
ton. At 1400 ft. the lode assayed up to 255. per ton
until the drive reached 772 ft. south ; from there to
895 ft. the grade varied from 325. to 425.' Some
weeks ago the manager advised the Board by cable
that from 885 ft. to 1000 ft. the average was 89s. per
ton for 72in., and that the ore in the face assayed
191 s. At the 1500 ft. level the same lode is reported
to have been opened up for 869 ft. south of the main
crosscut, in ore ranging from 255. 6d. to 345. No. 2
lode for the last 110 ft. driven at this level is re-
puted to be worth from 30s. to 80s. In addition to
these developments are those recorded as a result of
work done some time ago on the No. 4 lode from the
adjoining Great Boulder workings. At 2480 ft.,
where the crosscut passed through a portion of a rich
sect ! on of the lode, assavs averaged 1065., but in-
creased to 4765. in the first 10ft. south and to 12755.
m the next sft. At the 2630 ft. level the lode
assayed 106 s. in the crosscut. From 90ft. to 100 ft.
south, values rose to 4255., though they tumbled to
115 s. in the next 13ft. Where tapped at the 2800 ft.
level the lode was 6ft. wide and assayed 104 s. per
ton. These three levels have already been connected
by winzes, and the main shaft is down 2650 ft. All
this makes very pleasant reading; but with heavy
liabilities still to face, and the prospect of a long
wait before dividends are resumed, shares look dear
at present prices.
* * * *
Oroya Links has taken a short sampling
option on its next-door neighbor, North Boulder. At
one time this show was worked by an English co.,
which, more by good luck than good management,
unearthed a lot of gold and pa : d £27,500 in divi-
dends. Then the main shoot dipped into the Asso-
ciated. A good deal of money was afterwards sunk
in testing other parts of the property, but without
much success, and ultimately the whole outfit was
sold to a local syndicate, which knew a bit more
than the co. about the mine and about where to look
for values. This syndicate has since laid out a fair
sum in plant and development and has d : stributed
£4500 among its members out of gold won. A
hurried sampling of the upper levels will not en-
lighten the Oroya Links much as to the real value
of the property. A much longer option is needed.
* * * *
Tributes are off at Burbank’s Birthday
Gift (W.A.). Six years ago when things looked a
bit blue, the co. elected to stand aside and let others
have a go. Since then over £BO.OOO has been un-
earthed by parties of working miners, nearly one-
half of it during the past year. Old Professor
Nicholas, who made money for sharehold n rs in the
old days, is again in charge, but from all accounts
there isn’t much left for him to take out. During
the professor’s previous management the mine pro-
duced £537.000 and paid £99.500 in divs. 8.,
M. and Cos. then came along and dug out £70.000
for the co., but no further dividends were earned.
* * » *
After an inglorious sort of career, Mur-
chison Associated has decided to spend no more
money in W.A., and proposes to try to get back the
heavy losses incurred there by concentrating atten-
tion on its rubber estates in Ceylon. To do this,
however, another £25,000 is said to be wanted, and
an attempt is about to be made to get it out of
shareholders by means of another reconstruction and
a 6d. assessment.
General-manager Magnus, of Mount Mor-
gan, draws less from Many Peaks than his prede-
cessor did. The output for last quarter was 14,630
tons ; gold contents of this flux were 14dwt., and the
copper 1.4 p.c. The output for the corresponding
quarter of 1912 was 22,220 tons. Gold and cooper
contents are about 7s. per ton poorer now than they
used to be. The other shows in the neighborhood
are Glassford Creek and Mount Hector. The former
is still under exemption—it is about the most per-
sistent applicant for exemption in Bananaland. One
lease has had exemption 19 times; but something
should happen soon, as the Minister minuted the last
application “Further and final six months.” Some-
body had a look at Mount Hector the other day, but
that has happened many times before. The Eleanora,
a copper show near Marmor (where Mount Morgan
draws its limestone from), still sends its small
parcels to Mount Morgan for treatment. There is
copper where there is ore; but the ore is mighty thin
at times.
* * * *
Stanthorpe Tin. Ltd., a sluicing proposi-
tion iust over the Queensland border, has had its
second clean-up. and 33$cwt. of oxide was the har-
vest of 70 hours’ work. On the basis of these two
runs the management reckons it is entitled to do
some figuring. There should be 336 working
hours per month and a total revenue of £1250.
Expenses are put at £3OO. leaving £950 profit per
month for plant duplication, dividends and sundries
—on a paid capital of £II,OOO, in 1100 shares of
£lO each. It is a cheerful prospect; but it can’t
be realised until the co. gets a bigger water supply
than it has at present. And if the co. is certain of
its ground and is dead-sure it can get a water-
supply, it had better hurry along another plant.
There isn’t much sense in leaving tin in the ground
when the market is ready to snap up oxide at £125
or so a ton on the spot. There have been times
within the last 10 years when the tin scratcher has
been glad to take £6O, and those times (or some-
thing like them) may come again. In. fact, for at
least 30 years prior to 1899 there wasn’t a single.
12 months in wnich black tin was worth more than
£65 on the average, and in 1896 metallic tin was
down to £59, or (say) £32 10s. for oxide. These
figures are a dreadful warning to the co. which has
tin in the ground to lose no time in rooting it out.
Low-grade Scottish Gympie (Q.) has
paid £552,367 in dividends since 1895, when it was
purchased from the Eastern Monkland. At the annual
picnic a few days since, the chairman stated that
since the start, 18 years ago, 1,068,954 tons had
been crushed for 448,3910 z., worth £ 1,682,349. The
mine has averaged 31s. 4d. per ton, and at the pre-
sent time working costs and the expenses of manage-
ment run to 17s. 3d. per ton. Still, one mustn’t for-
get that low costs are to some extent due to an un-
stinted expenditure. Mining and treatment plants
have mopped up £97,179. The latest move is to
purchase a tube- mill and cyanide plant, presumably
to treat some of the residues and tailings from the
million tons of stone crushed.
Mt. Oxide (Q.) developments continue to
give every promise that the deal with the big Lon-
don people will be completed. Acting under ad-
vice they went into the business on a six months’
option. They advanced a sum of money, said to be
£20,000, to carry out certain development work,
and if that work was satisfactory they undertook to
pay in all £160,000 for 275,000 shares. The co.
had enough confidence in the mine to be satisfied
that the development would give good results, and
so it is turning out.
The high price of tin is drawing a good
deal of attention to the Annan River fields (N.Q.).
An English syndicate is now interesting itself in a
bucket dredging area in the lower reaches of the An-
nan River. If the values are sufficiently good, it
should be an ideal field, as there is no doubt what-
ever about quantities of dirt and water. Surface
prospecting on beaches has given from lib. to 21b.
to the yard.
One looks for black edges to Cathcart
(Vic.) reports nowadays. Bryant, manager of the
one-time aristocrat of Ararat, fears that most of the
3000 fathoms off the rises is “under-payable.” The
one crumb of comfort he offers is that work north-
east and south-west may put him on the old run of
gold. The actual length of “under-payable gutter”
should be set out, so that one might know how much
to write off the value of the claim. What has upset
the Cathcart applecart is the functioning of the deep
gutter with the channel carrying the payable ground.
This has happened at a point opposite No. 3 shaft.
And it may have meant sweep'ng the gold into the
Central Cathcart section of the lead, enriching that
patch, which the Cathcart aristocrats were too lofty
once upon a time to give a couple of “ponies” for.
Since Golden Pyke, Bendigo, got on to
the dividend list, great things have been expected
of its neighbors, Golden Age and Princess Dagmar;
but the only pirates to annex anything out of either
co. so far have been those who float around the Bee-
h've. At the Golden Age a little ginger in the man-
agement would help ; if that isn’t forthcoming, Prin-
cess Dagmar must beat it in yields in the near
future. This latter company some years ago went
out west from its main shaft at 781 ft., and hit
quartz. As only Ift. of stone was got, and that of no
value, the formation was neglected. Hard times
have driven the Dagmar and other mines to look up
these neglected makes of quartz near the surface.
The experience of Golden Pyke on the same line
now shows that the Dagmar ought to have done a
great deal more poking about at this point. In a
drive of 858 ft. from the Golden Age boundary, Ift.
of good stone is now being carried, with the shoot
widening over the back of the level. The Dagmar
looks to better times when it can handle its stone
better ; and as a big block of this scrip is held out-
side Bendigo, many eyes would be turned towards
the field if long-deferred hope now matures at this
old claim.
Argo Dredging, at Sandy Creek (Vic.),
despite the Svme interest, has not so far been a bon-
anza. Last, half-year the plant churned up 203,000
cubic yards of drift for an average return of only
3id. per cubic yard, and even in the Bright dis-
trict there wouldn’t have been much in that. Still,
the co. has wiped off its overdraft. Now the dredge
has its tail-race over a fresh paddock in a narrower
field of wash, so it is believed that better results
will be got.
* * * *
Cock’s Pioneer (Vic.) passes into the
hands of Robinson, Clark and Cos. and the Baillieus,
who undertake a float. Present capitalisation is
40,000 shares paid to 21s. 9d. In the new co. of
150.000 shares the old co. is to have 33,333 shares
of 20s. ; the underwriters get 50,000 shares at par
and the call over another 50,000 shares at 265., the
call to be open for two years. As the nominal capital
of the new co. is to be £150.000, there remain
nearly 17.000 shares —presumably to serve as com.
to underwriters and agents. Hector Macdonald had
a lot to do with pushing the deal through, and may-
be he gets a bit. Expert reports on the claim were
full of hope ; so instead of going along with a small
plant and few chances, it will have the big plant it
needs. Also, it will have the usual swell accessories
that it could very well do without.
Mount Lyell is at last dragging out ore
from the North Lvell 850 ft. level, and nearly has
the 1000 ft. level cleaned up for the miner. Then,
with the water out of the 1100 ft., the co. will get
go ; ng on the old scale; but the Mines Department
may require a lot done to prevent another disaster,
and the co. itself wants to make itself quite secure
against a second holocaust. The wait for dividends
may, therefore, be longer than the over-sold market
counts on.
* * * *
Briseis (Tas.) has acquired an option
over 255 acres up Mitta Mitta wav (Vic.). Where-
upon all and sundry are notified that a belt of
metamorphic schist, extending 35 miles from Tallan-
doon to Glen Wills, embraces the happy home of the
future Ardlethan of Victoria. Geology Dunn, even
in the face of the awful smash-up of the Glen-Wills
rush, always had hope of the Tallandoon region, but
his idea is that the irregularity of the shoots makes
it a small man’s field. Lindesav Clark mav be
trusted to soon pull out if this is so; for, although
he has lots of stuff at his back, he isn’t fond o-f
chucking it about.
Yields from the Tasmania at Beaconsfield
(Tas.), unless the next level helps out the position,
will stamp the claim as a pumping station more
than anything else. Frechville, the big expert, and
John Taylor, together with the late Billie Jones and
the Hoffnungs, pinned their faith to the gold living
down in the Tasmania, in view of the big returns
got by the old Launceston co. up to the point where
J. Bull bought the claim. Results since then show
that the best of mining engineers can’t look 100 ft.
into the ground. To lift the clouds a little float is
being put out in London —-the Delamere and Tas-
mania Development Cos., Ltd. This innocent is *to
pick up a block of 4428 acres, owned about equally
by the Tasmania Cos. and the executors of the dead
George Lambert. The land is to be transferred to
the new co. for (roughly) £3 10s. per acre, or
£16,650 in all. Asa start, the new co. is to spend
up to £BOOO in clearing roads and in demonstration
orchard work. Some Tamar fruit land is selling for
four times the money, and the tendency is still up-
wards, so the prospects are by no means vile.
The Radium Hill Cos. sends a parcel of
radium to England through the Bank of North
Queensland, so apparently buyers are not rushing
things. A parcel "from Woolwich, tested at Sydney
University, gave an indefinite result, for after the
test was made it was stated that the radium re-
quired further time to mature. The stuff being the
highest priced on earth, buyers may be excused for
being particular about purity. The whole process
is painfully slow. Compared with the reserves in
the mine (Olary, S.A.), the output and milling of
16 tons of ore per fortnight seems absurd. ' r he
cheeriest news is that a parallel 6in. vein, contain
ing a better percentage of carnotite or uranium, has
been discovered.
Investor: Costs, extraction and value of
product are all so uncertain that the radium business
is still a gamble, not an investment.....!. Mcl.:
There has been a hitch over the Mount Oxide’s Lon-
don business; but this paper believes it will go
through all right.

THE PRAYER OF THE PROMOTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We do not want a boom.— Too-much-protesting
Ardlethan director.
Best for our true development
Is water drawn from main or pump;
Champagne’s a curse by devils sent,
And our most dear desire’s a slump,
For, oh! how like to gods grow we
If cradled in adversity 1
We sink in iron and porphyry,
In quartz vein and in granite rock,
And hope the ore will not be free,
For easy money is a shock;
For low-grade stuff we make our prayer
And hope that arsenic will be there.
Twenty per cent, tin goes not down ;
But poverty is best for us —
Each tin-shoot with an iron crown
Shall soon be very pyritous.
O’er the rich ores black hell may loom,
So, Lord! we pray, send not a boom.
The quartzy shows in granite may
Cut out in fifty feet or so,
But better, Lord! to walk Thy way
And poor and righteous daily grow.
Send us a slump chock-full of doom —
But, O Lord! keep from us a boom !
.N.S.W.
K. B.
