WOMAN’S
LETTER
Sydney, May 5, 1913,
My Dear Arini, —
Hospital Saturday fund, in spite of being
nearly squeezed to death by 20,000 cadets on
the march, climbed up to the respectable
total of £BOOO odd. The Bank of N.S.W.
clerking staff deserve a bouquet for obliter-
ating their Saturday half-holiday for the
Cause. Instead of skipping round a tennis
court, Algernon Beau-Suburbs, nobly stuck
to his desk and his office coat. He toiled to
chase the uttermost farthing to its bleak
home in a bank vault, till the midnight cat
meowed on the city roof. As Algernon of
the New South not only gives his services on
Hospital Saturday, but gives them with a
cheery grin, he is a mighty good citizen.
Mrs. Jack Cormack fed a section of the
hungry box-ratttlers on Hospital Saturday,
from 11 to 12 noon. A small niche was
walled off in the N.S.W. Tourist office for
this worthy cause. Mrs. C. D. Patterson
lent her aid. Violet Lorraine, a smart young
Puss, in a fur toque and smoke-grey gown, was
located in the stand outside the Tourist lair.
The crowd, which expected to see Lily Bray-
ton on this patch of pavement, walked over
its neighbor’s corns with enthusiasm. In
its struggle to poke its inquiring individual
nose into the small calico reserve that was
to have sheltered the Lily’s head, it became
too demoralised to resist the insistent cry,
“Give ! Give !”
The daughters of the horse-leech were mendi-
cants of no account, compared to a Sydney
girl when she fights for a copper on Hospital
Saturday. Cleopatra Asche had sent along
a ten-pound note instead of her presence.
But the man in the street, not Knowing this,
fell into the arms of a horde of collecting
angels, who wheedled him out of everything
but his wearing annarel, while he searched
for the Brayton millinery.
Mrs. R. B. Sheridan and the muscular Ivy
Schilling, guarded by a vast bulldog, with a
pensive smile, camped at the Australia stand
during last Saturday’s scramble for coin.
The Dog, as a polite negation, does good ser-
vice on Hospital Day, and a St. Bernard,
about the size of a small pony, lay in wait for
cash near the Commercial Travellers’ Club.
„ lightning sketch appearance of the
Gov.-General in Sydney on Saturday, was
garnished with the cheers of the multitude
and the inevitable jog-trot of the under-
taker’s horse. Aldermen, as part of the
city’s welcome to the rich exile from Yarra’s
far shore, were seated, in chariots that
looked suspiciously like mourning coaches.
The serious attitude of the horses suggested
that, if they don’t belong to the cemetery
trade, they ought to. His small Ex., pink
cheeked, with the suppressed excitement of a
schoolboy who has crept over the wall into
a forbidden apple orchard, motored through
the streets with the large Gaud Mayor
Cocks.
The Welcome Home bunting that wriggled
overhead was good in spots—like the pro-
cession. A queer conglomeration of small
toy flags, a motor or two, a hired taxi or so,
a few mourning coaches, and some uniforms,
pulled up in front of the Union Club. Half
a dozen members, with their hair nicely
greased, hung over the entrance as the G.G.,
almost buried beside the portly form of the
City Father, hurried out of a car and scam-
pered up to the Club citadel. Later on,
G.-G. Denman, clothed in the burnished radi-
ance of a cocked hat and gorgeous militarv
trappings, received the salute of 20,000
boys in khaki at the gates of the
Lown Hall. How to keep on bobbing
to the vociferous welcome of the crowd, with-
out nodding the glory of the cocked hat into
the gutter, was the G.-G.’s most intricate
military evolution on Cadet Daw The
lengthy Polo Burnham, also decked in his
Sunday best; Polo Nutting and A.D.C.
Barttelot shadowed the Denman flight to and
from Sydney.
iHi’. Arthur Guise is the G.-G.’s new secre-
tary, now nearing Australia in the Morea.
The boys in khaki showed various grades
of drill in their tramp through the city, pre-
sumably according to the length of time
theyve been under healthy discipline. On
the pavements a panting, struggling crowd
tried to pick out its own mother’s darling,
as the long khaki stream of Boy trickled
slowly through the streets. A riotous baby,
trying to chew off a floral decoration in an
adjacent hat, got a fine maternal reproof.
‘My word, I’ll be glad when you’re old
enough for compulsory.” From infantine
groups perched on window sills, shrill treble
cheers greeted Billy and Tommy as they
dug their heels into macadam.
“A Stranger” writes :
Re Kurnell celebration, it seemed to me, as a pil-
grim who has brought his camel and h>'s tent into
your city, that the absence of school children was
a conspicuous blunder on the part of the authorities.
The speech of the Minister for Education teemed
with the possibilities that history opened up to the
Young Idea. Here was the very opportunity. Two
or three schools, at least, mwht have contributed
their senior pupils to the Cook memorial dav. Chief
Justice Cullen’s short address was the most inspirit-
ing thing I have heard for many a day. It ought to
have kindled the fire of patriotism in the breast of
a red-ragger. I left Kurnell under the impression
that the whole affair was purely a society function
arranged for the purpose of giving the park trustees’
wives and their friends an opportunity to hobnob
with the Governor, the Admiral and other person-
ages.
The Automobile Club showed its respect-
ful admiration for female nerve by handing
Hypatia Monk, a special trophy the other
day. Though the daring Hyoatia had been
politely wet-blanketed when she first men-
tioned her desire to take part in the inter-
state motor race, club members stepped off
their exclusive, rubber-tyred track to heartily
congratulate the only lady motorist on not
getting in front of male competitors. That’s
what the souvenir amounted to, I imagine.
The Fine Arts Society, in Bligh-street, has
taken £l6OO in 16 months. This is a toler-
able record for an infant which hopes to cut
more cash when it feels its feet. The strenu-
ous Julian Ashton is now boss manager. He
does it in his spare time, and has already
managed to extract extensive ' promises of
support out of 20 or 30 leading citizens,
even as old Plantagenet kings used to draw
the teeth of leading Abrahams and Isaacs.
The “Fine Arts” was founded by a num-
ber of artists, backed up by hard cash from
financial admirers. It aims at the useful
work done by the centenarian Fine Arts
Society in London. It charges the person
who sends his brush work 20 per cent, on
sales—a substantial drop from the average
dealer’s percentage. But 20 per cent, has
left a small dribble of loss out of £l6OO
worth of commerce. As the Society has no
intention of going out of business, it means
to broaden its field of operations. Hand-
made lingerie, elegant trifles in point-lace,
old silver, bronzes, opera-coats just landed
from China, and some hand-painted crockery-
ware that would turn the head of a collector,
are now on view. William Cullen, Chief
Justice, and his gentle dame, were amongst
the first to respond to the Ashton appeal for
help.
“The Women’s Handicrafts” will have to
shake itself together and kick itself violently
on the shins, and wake up generally, if it
wants to remain above ground. Signs of
its funeral are not many billions of miles
distant, and it looks as if it might expire
almost any aeon. In its upper chamber (op-
posite Paling’s, in George-street), the Soci-
ety shows internal evidence of a lingering
perish. Its standard of work is insignifi-
cant. Some of its exhibits, on sale at fairly
large prices, ought to be chopped up to boil
the kettle, or relegated to a second-hand
curiosity shop. It has some good stuff,
notably needlecraft and leather work, jumbled
up, the space being limited, with rubbish.
The Women’s Handicrafts suffers, from the
lack of a hanging committee, which would
not only throw out inferior stuff, but threa-
ten to hang the perpetrators of some of the
livery-looking “art” it finds so hard to sell.
An ingenious bed-cabinet model is .its star
exhibit just now. It is the invention of a
Victorian woman, Mrs. Anderson. As a
combination of bed, wardrobe, settee, and
office bureau, it is the last word in saving
space. By pressing the wrong button, I
imagine, it would be. quite possible to sud-
denly hang yourself in the wardrobe till a
good hard kick turned the dress hooks into
the hinges of a writing table, and you found
yourself writing letters to the editor re the
uncertain habits of modern furniture.
Syd Ure Smith, black and white illustra-
tor, hurried into a private hospital last
week. An appendix that he considers super-
fluous luggage was the cause.
Lieutenant Seton, R.N., who flag-lieuten-
anted in so agreeable a manner at blue-
water frivols, is convalescing from a recent
operation. His Best Girl, Miss Joan Deck,
is the daughter of a medico of that name.
The Deck family run to medical and mis-
sionary enthusiasm, and have some mun-
dane interests in tropic isles and copra. The
Seton homestead is in the Land of Kilts
and brose and haggis.
Dr. Thorold Quaife, now in the quaky
region of Panama, writes a Sydney chum, re
that hive of colored industry: “The hotel
I’m located at is a huge wooden structure.
All its doors and windows are covered with
wire. Colored servants and streams of
tourists are its leading features. It over-
looks the town of Panama—a place of nar-
row streets. The resident population is
mostly poor and oarti-colored, dresses itself
in gorgeous hues and lives in the open.
There are some prettv squares grown with
palms, and some quaint old Spanish churches,
South American bands (very fine) and
a good cafe contribute to the life of the
place. On Sunday nights the cafe .is
thronged with visitors in gay Parisian
dresses. The band discourses grand opera
inside, while outside in the streets one
hears the honk of motors and the clang of
bicycle bells that are hung on the queer old
carriages. And just across the way, per-
haps, naked children play in the gutter.
Panama is a place of weird contrasts.”
Australian-born Justine Kong Sing sends
a line from Amsterdam. Sydney’s youthful
artist Justine has been pushing her easel
alongside Dutch caps, and sketching canals
in their wettest streaks. With Hetty
Dymoek she shoulders her paint-box for
Brussels. Later on, the N.S.W. girls loiter
under the shadow of Bruges’ belfry “old and
brown.”
Artists Griiner and Harley Griffiths are
shivering on Canberra plains. With heroic
fortitude they have planked down their
easels in the teeth of the wintry blast that
howls round the ears of the rabbit popula-
tion. and threatens to blow off its million
tails. Griffiths is going to cover a large
canvas with his impressions of the local
burrows, and other outstanding features of
the Federal city. Bunny, meanwhile, is
said to have retreated to his uttermost wind-
proof hole, while Griiner dashes off a few
sketches of the purple hills.
Journalist Alice Schalek, the bright-eyed
Viennese traveller, is picking up solid slabs
of information on Australasian affairs, for
one or two Continental papers. The Frank-
furter Zeitung, to which she is a regular
contributor, is apparently the voice of a con-
servative political party which admits no
radical sentiments in its Tory columns. The
haughty, high-class Austrian’s brain-pan is
over-polished. Result, a carping, hyper-
sensitive critic. He takes his newspaper,
not as a hasty consensus of local news, but
as a serious literary effort. “If I did not
re-write most carefully each article, choos-
ing the most exact word to express what I
mean—then i should get letters to reprove
me, from the public. They care not for the
football match, as in your country,” says
the informative Alice Schalek. “They want
information —oh, yes—but on the affairs and
politics of other countries.” Briefly, Alice
Schalek has to be a mixture of journalist
and Macaulay, junior, rounding up her sen-
tences to suit the fastidious ultra-cultured
Austrian taste. Like the Germans, her
readers love their literature long, not
chopped up short in tabloids as in Aus-
tralia. In N.S.W. the scenic attractions of
the Blue Mountains, and the glory of Syd-
ney’s water-front, have made a deep imprint
on the Austrian lady’s notebook. She is an
ardent patriot. Any reference to the Monte-
negrins and Skutari, in her presence, should
be made bv large, healthy persons, able to
defend their oninions.
The John Robertson memorial fund stamps
on the public imagination with the measured
step of an aged tortoise. Politicians are
soon forgotten, while performing fleas and
acrobats are long remembered. John Robert-
son fought for the right of the poor man to
free-select the soil. The fund, which has
only crawled up to the vicinity of
£340, is intended for the benefit of five
or six afflicted grand-children of the late
John. They are deaf and dumb. The
chance to help the good cause will not cease
till May 31. So the up-country selector
has still time to rush to the aid of the
descendants of the man who was his first
friend. Francis Suttor, M.L.C., and Aider-
man Clarke are hon. treasurers.
Pretty Kathleen Broughton, an Armidale
girl, was married to Norman Dangar the
other afternoon. The wedding was shorn of
the usual fashionable frills, owing to the
recent death of wealthy A. A. Dangar, the
groom’s father. Muriel Broughton, the onlv
bridesmaiden, attended to the correct flutter
of the bridal veil before the small nrocession
moved up St. Philip’s ancient aisle for the
great “I will.” R. M. Bell best-manned the
ceremony.
Tradition used to say, in its old, toothless
fashion, that Bathurst was mostly peopled
with Lees, Suttors, Bassetts and McPhil-
lamys, also fat stock and lean boundary-
riders. The town has grown up into a city
since then, and keeps a bishop and many
other luxuries. But the Suttor family shows
no signs of being wiped off the local map by
the influx of new blood. And the McPhil-
lamys still ride or drive the best horses in
the district, when they don’t fill the scenery
with whiffs of petrol. Kitty McPliillamy,
one of the finest amateur horsewomen in
N.S.W., a daughter of Bob McPhillamy, was
married to Claude Suttor in Bathurst, last
week. The groom belongs to the Brucedale
clan. Liberal helpings from both primeval
families munched the wedding cake.
Zara Wolinski and her brother. Artist .Toe,
were still on the trail of a London studio
when the last mail left the Bull Archipelago.
Mrs. G. P. Bowman, who passed out at
Elizabeth Bay the other day, was a native
of Singleton, which grows Oldest Inhabitants
in prolific crops. The veteran, who had
reached her 80th milestone, was a daughter
of a pioneer named Thomas McDouall.
Mrs. Acheson unnacked her cabin trunk
a week or two ago, and concelled her nassage
in the Orsova. She is a member of the
well-known Plunkett family, and has a stack
of affairs to wind up before she strikes new
roots in England. Her only chick, the
youthful goes with her Staff-
Surgeon. R.N., to Britain’s misty shores,
as previously arranged, on May 10.
Miss Marjorie Nott, a daughter of the
Bowral Randolph Notts, was married to Mr.
Ted Giblin the other evening. Strathfield, a
suburb which resounds with the tinkle of
tea-cups on first and fourteenth Thursdays,
and is primed with “At Homes” on every
day but washing day. was the scene of the
Giblin-Nott wedding bells. The good-
looking Marcia Ross, with Phyllis Ford and
Janet Fell, were the bridesmaidens who
waited on the usual Limerick lace bridal
finery.

“Captain Cook’s childhood” is the name of
the latest historic bone. It is in the act of
being picked bv Larson Ord Todd and James
H. Watson. The parson is a relative several
times removed of the Dame Walker who is
alleged to have spanked or coaxed the alpha

Bert Bailey has put on the outer shell

Steele Rudd’s best book, “On Our Selec-
tion,” has been better dramatised than any
other Australian story. Therefore, the
Palace Theatre is redolent of the smell of
gum leaves just now. “Standing room
only” is its prevailing complaint, a gilt-
edged proof that the public swallows re-
peated doses of a dashing Australian play,
in which the situations are strong and racy
of the soil. The massive chin of George
Treloar pushes its owner along a career of
unsuccessful crime as a stage villain, and
he gets home earlier than the rest of the
company in consequence. As Jim Carey
his murder is arranged for in the second act.
Sarah Rudd, as played by clever Laura
Roberts, in rags ( Act 1), or in large red
hair bows and a flamboyant party dress
(Act 4) is Sarah of the author’s spirited
sketch. She makes the genuine Cornstalk
feel _ homesick for corned beef, carrots, and
a vista of gum trees silhouetted against a
sapphire sky.
The man whose, chief job is to groom the
Wild Cat tells me’this: —
. Every time I go to Jenolan I come back sadder,
and I’m so sad this time that 1 don't think I’ll go
any more. When the tourist blows into the N S.W.
Government Bureau the first thing he is told he
ought to do, after inspecting the Harbor, is to rail
up to the Blue Mountains and take a motor to the
Caves. It is good advice, for the Blue Mountains
can show about as much gorgeous color as any other
disarranged landscape in the world, and the 30-odd
miles from Mount Victoria are 30-odd miles of pure
delight. But then you strike the State-run Caves
House —the only place of accommodation—and the
delight begins to fray at the edges and sag in the
middle. It is about the most comfortless shack this
side of Booligal; and beyond Booligal they generally
give you more attention. It is cold at jenolan, as
a rule; but they are liable to drop you into a bed-
room carpeted with icy linoleum and nothing else.
There’ll be gas laid on, but it won’t be usable. The
windows will be curtainless. There’ll probably be
a cha ; r in the room, but it will have lost one leg
and have gout in another. And if you don’t eat
the candles you will as likely as not leave hungry.
All the while the gibe of the man who told you that
“Private Enterprise can do it better ” is blistering
your ear, and making you want to kick somebody
right up to the Dog-star. And things are not much
better in the Caves. Where there isn't muddle there
is mostly ignorance. You are instructed to stick to
the guide with whom you started the round of in-
spection But if you do you’ll presently find that,
the _ parties being limited, you’ll be sent where you
don't want to go, and then you’ll discover that you
could have gone where you wanted to go if you had
thrown the official advice into a cavern and changed
over to some other guide. And those guides! There
are one or two who know' something of their job,
but there are other dreadful humorists who butcher
every rule of grammar for two hours twice a day.
and can tell you nothing more valuable than that
if you look straight ahead you will see a “remark-
able representation” of a sheep’s pluck. The “re-
markable representations” of animals’ innards and
German sausages become' wearisome after the 303rd
infliction. One can’t n°rhaps expect that scientists
will take on the guides’ job, though manv a scien-
tist gets poorer pay; but surely the visitor snared
by the Tourist Bureau might expect something more
solid than the constant drip of information that if
he looks ahead of him he will see a remarkable re-
presentation of a pork ebon. The only comfort is
that as often as not it looks just as much like a
bishop.

“ Chandler of Corraling'a,”
by R. J. CASSIDY.
Here, for the first time in the annals of Australian literature, the thrilling, pulsating excite-
ment of a big shearing-shed has been incorporated into a stirring story. The manner in which
Jack Chandler, a “gun" shearer, rung “Mulgawirra,” and took his own and his rival’s cheque, is,
as The Bulletin says, “graphically and tensely told.” The roar of machinery, the whirring of
the wheels, the flash of the flying cutters and the stress and strain of the terrible ordeal are all there.
And running through this remorseless saturnalia of enduring human toil is a woman’s love, and a
strong man’s way of winning it. It is a strenuous and a striking story—one never told before,
and one not easily forgotten.
“BILL’S IDLES
9 9
9
by A. Q. STEPHENS
(“ The Bookfellow ”).
Fully Illustrated. PRICE, 1/-; POST FREE, 1/1.
In “BILL’S IDEES” the N.S.W. Bookstall Co., Ltd., has the pleasure of introducing Mr.
A. G. Stephens as a new Australian humorist. Mr. Stephens’s reputation as the doyen of
Australasian is widespread, and he has several books of prose and verse to his credit.
“Bill’s Idees” is the first book in which he has given his natural humor free play—as we venture
to think, with the happiest results.
Many years ago the original of “Bill” was a larrikin in Sydney—the leader of a “push.” He
reformed himself, and for a long time has been a highly-respected citizen ; as he says, improving
his mind, cultivating his good instincts, and trying to live up to his ideal. Bill’s ideal is “a
quiet blolce with a quiet wife and a quiet dog with a quiet name.” Bill had had to struggle to
reach his ideal, because he had a lot of bad instincts to get rid of, and because he never had much
chance in the way of education. Still, with the help of his favorite author, Vere Foster, he is
getting along nicely.
Bill’s story is told in his own words. He is interesting when he looks back upon his
unregenerate past, and is no less interesting when he contemplates his reformed present. Hard
life has taught him a philosophy of his own, worth considering. His native humor is of the kind
called “pawky,” and he enjoys a joke against himself. Bill isn’t a fool and he isn’t a clown;
perhaps his readers will seldom laugh right out. But they will get a few broad grins and a
whole heap of chuckles.
Bill's “missus” is several chapters to herself.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, May 5, 1913.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The hop season promises well. Cards for
the St. Kilda Bachelors’ Ball are out, the
festive creatures having arranged to give the
girls a treat at St. Kilda Town Hall on
June 13. Twelve nights later the Vic. Art-
ists’ Society will hold the annual mask <md
costume frivol at Melbourne Town Hall.
Last year the range in bizarre disguises was
restricted to a certain historic period. This
time any properly picturesque age may be
represented. Prehistoric outfits will be
barred, of course ; but early Roman legs and
ancient Egyptian ditto will meet require-
ments.
The Australian Art Association, which is
practically the cream skimmed from the poor
old V.A.S. milk, is now having its first ex-
hibition of pictures at the Athenaeum Gal-
lery. The A.A.A. is a combination of Har-
court, Mather, McClintock, McCubbin,
Meldrum, Officer, Patterson and Withers.
Their show was to be opened yesterday
(Wednesday) by Lady Denman, and will
remain hanging until May 30.
The Fred Alcock family lately wrenched
themselves from their billiard tables, and
steamed off in the Malwa for a scamper
abroad. England and Europe are to be
“done” within seven months, while America
will be glanced at on the way home.
The Melbourne Shakspeare Society cele-
brated poor, dear William’s birthday recently
at the Masonic Hall. The menu for the
evening was a cold hash of “Twelfth Night”
at 2s. a brain. The job took so long that presi-
dent Archibald Strong, at a late hour,
stopped the impatient shifting of chair legs
by the suffering survivors with the intima-
tion that the proceedings would conclude
with the Clown’s song after the third act.
This cut out poor Sebastian’s only scene and
all the fat of the minor roles. It was hard
luck for the people who intended to do the
jobs, but the audience would have risen and
tramped out of the place if some concession
of the sort had not been made. Hired cos-
tumes were eked out with flannel dusters and
other curious remnants. Reciter Charlotte
Hemming, as directress, was assisted by
Frank Russell, secretary of the society. He
also wrestled with the part of Malvolio.
Charlotte stood in the wings holding (1) a
household edition of “the Works,” (2) a
candle, (3) the strings of the curtain, and
(4) her breath. The curtain jibbed badly
after the riotous revels of Sir Toby (in brown
paper boots and very husky), and Sir Andrew
Aguecheek (verv nervous about the stock-
ings). Andrew kept on capering in hopes
that the curtain wmuld be dragged down;
but the curtain remained aloft, and the
panic-stricken actor finally had to leap out
of sight behind a piece of scenery. Viola
wore bunchv petticoats and strutted. She
seemed to be intent on diverting attention
from her grey stockings. The Duke suffered
somewhat from lockjaw; there also appeared
to be something wrong with his back. As
the audience was scuttling towards the doors
the bouquets were hurriedly bundled together
and the coy she-mummers pushed forcibly on
to the stage to take delivery of their re-
wards. Malvolio helped in the scramble.
The rest of the company refused to appear
and bow to the back hair and coat-tails of
the dwindling crowd.
Joseph McCabe is telling scientific secrets
every night, at the Athenaeum Hall. Bald
heads, partly bald heads, corrugated fore-
heads and flashing spectacles occupy most of
the seats. Everybody present is determined
to improve his or her mind —or the mind of
some docile young person in the next chair.
Joseph, a wiry little man with a stubborn
jaw, stands beside a large sheet that looks
as if it had been used in at least one spring
cleaning. Then the lights go out, and the
pleasant voice of Joseph is heard retailing
immense facts about the millions of years
that have floated past like clouds. Listening
to him makes you feel as young as yesterday
morning. I’m sure science must be good for
the complexion.
Rumor asserts that Louis Esson, poet and
dramatist, is booked for matrimony with a
nice Melbourne girl. Rumor is sometimes a
bit previous ; but she is generally worth lis-
tening to with at least one ear.
The University Graduates’ Association in-
duced Canadian Minister G. E. Foster to lec-
ture .on “Ourselves and the Empire,” at the
Masonic Hall, last Friday evening. Invita-
tion cards were fired at citizens through the
post, and the place was crammed to the roof
with Imperialists, all of them bursting with
loyalty. John Madden, who should have
chaired the . show —or blow —was not well
enough to attend; so Vice-Chancellor Mac-
farland did the welcoming act. The plat-
form was furnished with pink screens and
graduates. Alfred Deakin, Norton Grim-
wade, and A.N.A. President Phillips were
also on view. The lecturer proved to be a
narrow, grey man with a broad, red view, and
when he got fairly going he set fire to the
house. Some of the womenfolk were dressed
as if for morning shopping; others were at-'
tired for an evening function. But, thoueh
the dressing was uncertain, there was no
mistake about the enthusiasm. The Empire
is not going to fall yet awhile.
Mrs. Ted Bage has tired of running a home
for an absent family, and is preparing to
steam off to Japan, at the end of this month.
As her son is sampling the Antarctic climate
with Mawson, and the eldest girl is sunning
herself at Brisbane, daughter Ethel, who has
only been back about ten minutes or so after
an extended trip, has been instructed to re-
pack her trunks and accompany mother. The
Japanese trip has become a fad with local
womenfolk. One lately confessed that her
curiosity had oeen aroused by hints thrown
out by a returned husband. She is now wan-
dering through Japan seeing some of the
sights for herself, and wondering about the
other things that she can’t see.
The Barttelots, who boarded with the
Hunter-Landales, in the Deniliquin district,
during the local horse revels, have been going
in for lots of exercise. Long, breezy hours
spent tramping over the links, or leaping on
the tennis courts, were mere preliminaries to
the nightly dances. This week-end the two
are expected back at their suburban cot, for
the Help is due to resume his official job at
the vice-regal establishment. Heaven alone
knows what would happen to the bleeding
country if a Help didn’t come on duty at the
exact minute.
Amongst the Salonites, at Mrs. Bill Wil-
son’s musical tea, was Mrs. Taverner, an old
member or Salonitic. She was towed in and
out of the place by the adopted infant, evi-
dently an impetuous child with the strength
of an ocean tug. The Taverners have defi-
nitely decided to return to London in June.
“I could be happy in Victoria, but John has
got used to the people there and misses them,
so we are going back,” remarked John’s
thoughtful wife. At the Taverner elbow sat
Mrs. McEw-an, who is looking after brother
Fisher, from Maoriland. Their trouble has
been to find time and room for the hospitali-
ties and refreshments that have been thrust
upon them. Both brother and sister were
bidden to lunch this week with the Denmans.
They will indulge in a return spread at the
Oriental before making back.
Amongst the approaching marriages is
that of a dashing widower, something oyer
three score and ten, with an attractive
widow- who has long been regarded as a
family friend. His children are said to be
somewhat upset over Father’s decision. The
role —as actors would say—of every man’s
children is to be mostly angry with Dad and
quite pleased with themselves.
Madge Temple, the vaudeville fashion
plater, gave a tea, this week, to. a selected
company at the Vienna. Madge, oh the boards,
is Mrs. Dereweski, and her husband, who
travels with her, manufactures the touching
little songs she carols. He lived next door to
Paderewski, sen., in Poland, and the illus-
trious pianist’s son and he used to play to-
gether as boys. Any man who has played
with Pad. should be able to write Madge’s
songs.
The Norton Grimwades’ festivity was a
public manifestation of delight at recovering
son Fred, after a two-years’ absence in Eng-
land. Nephew Cedric Battle (who has since
left for London, intent on becoming as “pol-
ished” as his returned cousin) also took a
modest helping of limelight. Homeden is
rather celebrated for its revels, for the
Hodges used to make the board groan there,
and the present owners keep up the toothsome
tradition. Mrs. Norman floated round in
chiffon and lace. A tall red osprey and a
sunburst advertised themselves in her hair.
Mrs. Alf Treacy sported a tunic of gold tis-
sue over melon pink. Bessie Tobin looked
smart in a lemon frock touched with black.
Herbert Sargood chaperoned a blue-clad
wife, and sister-in-law Young; but Norman
Brookes just brought himself. Sydney Dal-
rymple was in care of his sister. Mrs.
Teece (once Freda Grimw-ade) was a study
in lavender. Miss Keats and Lesly Madden
bravely inhabited pannier frocks. The tall
Clarke sisters were amongst the steppers.
The event of the event was supper, which
was served in the dining-room. All the
Grimwadean solid silver glittered on one
long board. The envious looks of the female
guests must have been intensely gratifying
to the energetic hostess. In such cases,
looks are much more than words.
The Warraw-ee Club “At Home” was made
up to suit all tastes. The large room up-
stairs was reserved for dancers, and a musi-
cal programme supplied by Brooks-Smith,
Lottie Williams and others was thrown in,
while card-players glued their attention to
Bridge in a special reserve. President
Staughton, in an inky robe glittering with
jet, triumphantly announced to friends that
the hospitality committee had decided to
entertain Mrs. Taverner on the 16th. Victor
Busst’s mamma stuck (I don’t mean that
she adhered) to her expensive ermine wrap-
pings. Mrs. Secretary Phillips staggered
under a load of art jet. Amongst the dan-
cers were Dr. Grey-Gross and small Anne
Stutt. Nellie Holson w-as also on the scene.
She has lost her limelight ambitions since
her spell of sickness, and has decided to cul-
tivate a voice. (Some people do this as
casually as the suburbanite starts out to
cultivate cabbages.) Nellie was Lily Bray-
ton’s understudy during the Melbourne sea-
son ; but, when the co. went on tour, she fell
ill. and had to retire.
The King’s Warehouse, which uprears
itself in the vicinity of the Australian
Wharf, was invaded on Thursday afternoon
by a curious company anxious to see the
stamping ceremony of the first Federal note
issue. Secretary Collins and Printer Harri-
son soon got busy and showed the guests
how to make paper money. One machine on
the premises turns out tenners at the rate
of £4O per crash. Other machines add the
beautiful carroty-blue color, whilst a ready-
to wear aspect is imparted to 50 notes at a
time by a cute stamper which saves the hand-
trimming process undergone bv the Yankee
greenback. The G.-G. came on from Maori-
land Fisher’s luncheon. His small family
followed him lovingly with wide-open eves.
Sir Alf Vincent (no relation of the black-
and-white artist) made one of the party,
and wore his well-groomed whiskers with dis-
tinction. Help Burnham posed wearily in
the background. Prime Minister Fisher,
showing signs of weariness, jerked out a
few words of welcome. Then little Judith
Denman, who had been chosen to number the
first ten-shilling note, got to work at the
job. His Ex. and small son Thomas added
two more to the issue. The presentation of
souvenirs excited the watching crowd, and the
Treasury had to close down in haste lest the
gold reserve should be imperilled. Westra-
lian Premier Scaddan looked in on his way
home and chatted awhile with the Gaud
Mayor. Plump Mrs. Mills came along with
the members of the Dominions Commission,
and the Haggard laugh (a unique sort of
cackle) was heard above the clatter of
tongues and machinery. Mrs. Andrew
Fisher, despite a recent long train journey,
showed a fresh exterior. Mrs. Pearce was
close at hand, and Mrs. Hughes, greatly
improved in health, hovered around grace-
fully. The Findley couple, the Josiah
Thomas pair, and a number of banking chiefs
were the other noticeable people.
The Gaud Mayoress Vas issued invitations
to meet the departing Denman lady over a
cup of tea, in the Town Hall, on Tuesday
afternoon, May 13.
Lovie Mueller Cadorin (she is now
threatening us with a couple of concerts)
displayed the collection of Greek and Byzan-
tine wraps and gowns, at the Centreway, on
Friday. A living model paraded round, decked
in a succession of interesting duds designed
by artist Fortuny, and Mrs. Cadorin herself
fluttered about in a sort of inspired green
and gold nightgown. The Denman lady,
who came early to avoid the rush, was
anxious to bid for the lot, but in the end
was pacified with two garments and a
promise of all the surplus. Her Ex. takes
an interest in Lovie, who is well acquainted
with an aunt of the G.-G. lady, who resides
in Florence. Chamberlain Nevill looked in
on the gay scene, and the sculptor husband
hovered on the edge of the crowd. Even
Alex. Landale’s widow- was enticed from her
retreat by the unusual finery. Mrs. Pins-
chof, with a trousseau problem looming
ahead of her, looked at everything with a
business eye, and handsome Mrs. Crowley
figured amongst the enthusiasts.
Dr. Hordern, who plays first-class cricket
w-hen not otherwise engaged, is now- honey-
mooning in Melbourne. Menzies’ is shel-
tering him and his bride during their stay.
An interesting three-brush art party opened
last week in the Centreway, where the walls
are carefully covered with the work of May
Roxburgh, Dora Wilson and Percy Leason.
All three hail frojn the National Gallery.
Friends and relations do their duty nobly
by wandering in with a vague smile and all
the adjectives left over from last month’s
canvas-gazing.
Henrietta Walker (she used to be Hen-
rietta McGowan) still follows the inky way.
This time her busy nib is represented by a
neat blue volume entitled “Rafia Work.” It
tells you exactly, in plain language, how to
make rafia into hats, buttons, hammocks
and floor-coverings. Apparently, the sub-
stance is becoming one of the indispensable
household commodities.
Pianist Edward Goll and his wife are stay-
ing with the lady’s sister at Balwyn. Ed-
w-ard’s marriage was not exactly a May and
December affair; it was, say, May and
August. And Mrs. August is a very w-in-
some; person. Artists usually need someone
to lPok after them, and the bushy-haired
pianist seems to be amply provided for.
The Cliveden flatteries are echoing unusual
yelps, shouts and cacklings. The uproar has
been traced to the Madden corner, where
daughter Lesly is rehearsing a theatrical
show. Rehearsals happen on the landings
and in any convenient corner, and the time
is drawing near w-hen society, blessed by all
available vice-regality, will stifle its yawns
at the finished performance.
The Butt-Rumford family is migrating
this week to Cliveden, where several acres
of floor space have been reserved.
The Nyulasy medicine man has steamed
off to Maoriland, accompanied by a couple of
sisters, to acquire a new brother-in-law.
Marguerite, the elder sister, is the bride-
elect, and she is to marry Mr. Cowper, a re-
tired banker who is well-known at the best
male clubs. Marguerite kept her matri-
monial intentions a dark secret. In fact,
the Toorak W omen s League, whereof she
was treasurers, will have to send its Wh
ding present on by post. The function h t
be dressed in tailor-mades. 10

Two courageous young persons have added
their names to the list of Melbourne busi
ness women Ivy Stuckey is one, and she
is collaborating with another small venturer
by the name of Jacobs. They intend to run
a toilet salon until lately propelled by two
other girls Charming little Ivy, who shines
as a social entertainer in a strictly amateur
way, is closely related to the author of a
new order of tlakey food, which is flaked in
Austraha, and is beginning to proclaim itself
loudly from the hoardings.
The explosive Hakes have scored their
return voyage from England off the list of
social events, and have directed that their
choice collection of antiques be shinned from
Toorak to Italy, . where they 1 P (mean-
ing the Hakes primarily) intend to live
for a space. Mrs. Hake was one of the
John Simson clan, a fact which spells end
less wealth. By the way, sister Mrs Scobie
Mackmnon, together with Scobie and their
pigeon pair, are about to move into Beaulieu
—the Mars Buckley house of old. The
place is now glorified out of recognition.
The Taverner couple show no desire to
wrestle with the domestic problem in this
part of the world. Nowadays they feel lost
out of the smoke and noise of London, and
are inclined to pine for the friends
they have made thereabouts; so it is
possible they may presently get back
to _ the ant-hill by the Thames. Mean-
while, the Oriental supplies them with
board and lodging. John has put on flesh
during his years of pleasant exile; he has
also acquired a bucolic flush. His dame, on
the contrary, shows a slimmer figure line
than of yore, with a more subdued color
scheme. The pair have added an adopted
girl-child to their responsibilities, thus con-
verting the domestic duet into a trio.
A young man has disappeared from his
usual haunts. A relative who fetched him
out here and put him in the way of earning a
crust, grew tired of his expensive tastes, for
which the relative had to pay. Not so long
ago the nephew’s name was coupled with
that of a local heiress, but nothing has come
of it up to date. Possibly the sorrow is
being drowned in beef-tea or something. I
suggest this victual because I don’t like it
myself, and anyone who wants it can have
my share of all the beef-tea on earth.
Seems that Clara the Gigantic is bringing
the same luggage as before to Australia, or
even more so. Husband Kennerley Rumford
dees not come under the heading of luggage,
of course. The three children, two gover-
nesses, a tutor, three maids, and a valet, who
tiavel with the Butt pair, are the burden of
responsibilities. In addition to the family
party there is to be a trio of surmorting
artists on the tour —Frank Carter, the Syd-
ney violinist: Harold Craxton, accompanist;
end Willie Murdoch, the pianist, who has
lalely made good in Melbourne. Clara, Ken-
nerley and the other 10 souls are due in Syd-
ney front Vancouver on the 10th. The con-
cert tour starts in the brand-new Tait Con-
cert Hall, in Melbourne, a week later.
From across the Straits :
Australia’s broadest-minded bishop, Dr. Mercer,
who throws off his apron and gaiters in a few
months’ time, is said to be about to commit matri-
mony (his second offence) with one of the “poor
widows” who are subject to, the cruel Federal land
tax. The relict of a former member of Parliament,
who was also a railway contractor, is named as the
disintegrator of the Tasmanian bishopric; and the
alleged intention of the couple is to sojourn a while
in the south of France, where the atmosphere is
supposed to be congenial for the making of a few
books, which his Lordship has promised himself to
write.
Maybe an Art and Law Guild will event-
uate from the intrusion of Gretchen Lesch-
kau among the lawyers of Temple Court.
The little dark-eyed paintress, who includes
a National Gallery prize among her creden-
tials, has ooened a studio in the vast build-
ing (No. 25). Where she wflll take classes in
drawing, painting and applied arts. Gov.
Carmichael of yore remarked that he had
never been quite clear about the meaning of
“applied art,” though he knew misapplied
art when he saw it. But gentle Gretchen
gives lessons in the thing which puzzled his
Ex. The studio is furnished with an interest-
ing collection of her own achievements in oil
and watercolor.

A RIME OF STOUT AND STOUSH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He dearly loves a fight. He can use the gloves,
as the interrupters at his meetings have occasion to
know. — Argus eulogy of a Liberal candidate
USH/’JS).
The candidate got up, a man of worth and probity,
And spoke his mind: “I am agin’ the Gover’ment,”
said he,
And, when a rude soul asked him “Why ?” he
lifted up his mit
And smote the forward one a really nasty blow with
it.
“I plead,” he said (and how the long, loud, wild ap-
plause did crash!)
“For freedom, and the right to stoush, for liberty
and lash!”
A plain, hard-working man then rose and asked:
“Is it a fact ?”
The candidate let out and smote the blighter in the
act;
And yet another bloke began: “Do you believe —— ?”
No more
Was heard from him, for Fat’s knight-errant
punched him through the door.
Early and late the candidate left-hooked, and crossed
again,
Until the staggered floor was strewn with senseless
working men.
With right and left he kept the interjectors on the
jump.
“ What of our bleeding country?’’ [Biff!] “What
price Australia?” [Bump!]
And glad old ladies, mad with joy, leapt up and
down, and squealed
As his clenched fists shut up each mouth and stricken
voters reeled.
Right-thinkers, parsons, thin old maids—all sorts of
earnest folk
Emitted yelps of sheer delight at each new head he
broke.
He pleaded for plain justice—-and, at every other
word,
He drove his knuckles here and there among the
doubting herd ;
They howled for peace and reason, but he punched
their noses flat
While Slowgress cheered and howled with joy, and
flung aloft its hat.
“I stand [bash, bangl] for Slowgress [crack ! ] —to
hold what we/have got;
And [biff, bang, bump, crash, batter, thump!]
you’re with me, are you not?”
FRANK HENTY.
Yarraby, Glen Iris, Victoria.]
POLITICAL
POINTS
A DREADFUL POSSIBILITY.
Whilst we are determined to prevent the trusts
obtaining a hold on Australia, surely there is some
method more practical, less drastic, and less unjust
than these Referendum proposals.
—Wade on the Referenda.
WADE : “Hit him with this. That is much
too drastic. Why, it might KILL him."
[FRANK JESSUP, 52 Renwick-street, Leichhardt.]
From Leonard James Watson, Orrong-
road„ Elsternwick, Victoria :
The amazing differences in State laws dealing with
friendly societies provide one of many reasons why
lodge members should shout an enthusiastic “Yes”
to the Referendum proposals on May 31. At the re-
cent H.A.C.B.S. conference, in Melbourne, one of the
delegates, brought forward a motion affirming the
necessity of Commonwealth legislation for the con-
trol of friendly societies, but he pointed out that
unless the “Corporations” Referendum was carried,
the thing couldn’t be done. In a two hours’ debate
it was shown that an overwhelming majority of the
delegates favored the proposal; but the chairman
finally ruled it out, on the ground that political
questions couldn’t, be voted on by the Order. With
Commonwealth legislation on the lines of the Ma
State’s Act, there would not be so much doubt as to
the solvency of some of the friendly societies, and
then there wouldn’t be need for such scathing reports
as those presented this year and last by the secre-
tary of the big Oddfellows’ Order.
Liberalism would have a smoother road
to pedal if there were more women voters
like the one encountered recently by two
Victorian lady canvassers. “You belong to
the League, of course,” cooed the spokes-
woman. “No, I don’t,” replied the cheerful
lady of the house, a property owner of many
years’ standing. “But you’re not going to
vote Labor, are you?” they duetted
anxiously. The voter folded her hands, and,
with the placid air of one who has put her
trust in Heaven and handed over the keys
of Destiny, replied: “ I'm going to do what-
ever the Argus tells me!" Votes for
Women ! Blessed be the Argus !
[Ernest Francis O’Fkrrall, The Bulletin Office,
Sydney.]
Victorians are a long-suffering people.
Recently the municipal council of Wonthaggi
circularised its fellow councils as to inter-
viewing the Government to ask that the
public might be allowed to buy its own Won-
thaggi coal. The proposal was cordially re-
ceived, and a large deputation spoke with
John Weary. John said that he was very
sorry, but the present output of Gipps-
land coal was barely sufficient fur the
railways, so there was none left for tne
public. That looked conclusive —or did
until certain persons pointed out
to the tired statesman that bores put
down prove that there is a large body of
workable coal in the vicinity of Wonthaggi,
and that the output from the otate mine
could be largely increased by the tremend-
ously uncomplicated process of merely dig-
ging out some of it. At this point it was
discovered that John was only talking in his
sleep, and the deputation withdrew softly,
carrying its boots in its hand, for fear of
shaking him un too much.
The perishment of N.S.W.’s once promis-
ing ship-building industry under Foreign-
trade conditions, was emphasised one recent
day in Sydney Telegraph, but the paper
wasn’t thereby moved to shove High Protec-
tion into the forefront of the Federal battle,
as David shoved Uriah after seeing Bath-
sheba washing herself in the second Book of
Samuel. During the five vears to 1880 the
vessels built at Sydney alone represented
16,551 tons. The five years to 1885 saw the
culmination- —24,949 tons. Then came the
lesser and the greater and the intermediate
slump—Bo46 tons, 4907 tons, 7434 tons,
8255 tons and 5114 tons, each figure reore-
senting a period of five years. And in 1911
came the really bitter slump, when ship con-
struction, including motor-boats, got down to
986 tons, or at the rate of 4930 tons for five
years. The increase of wages, without any
corresponding measure of Protection against
low-wage competitors outside, has almost
driven this once promising trade out of Syd-
ney. As for the outports of N.S.W., it was
practically driven out of them long ago. Some
of the places that used to build ships have
now almost forgotten what a ship is like. ,

Last Thursday Syd-
ney Telegraph, cham-
pioning Joe Cook against
the gibe of the Prime
Minister that the Fusion
leader had faced every
point of the political
compass, asked plaintive-
ly: “But has Mr. Cook
ever faced the Socialist
point?” And the answer
is: “ Yes, certainly.”
During 1891-4, in the
N.S.W. Legislature, Joe
Cook was leader of the

graph of the time used
to say the Labor party
was Socialistic. And the

know a bit. So what

[C. A. Jeffries. The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
Politician Catts (N.S.W.) got on the roof
one day last week and bristled his tail and
said things. Incidentally, he handed in his
mouse and his job and his resignation.
Catts had long been secretary of the Amal-
gamated Railway and Tramway Service
Association, and his general plaint was that
that organisation was a hard master, a bitter
nigger-driver, and a thankless boss. Exit
Catts.
It was bound to come. The Sydney
Telegraph, a Federal election being on
hand, has been, for the billionth time or
thereabouts, flabbergasted within an inch of
its life at the progress which Imported
Shoddy doctrines are making in Victoria. In
its own words: “The jmunger voters, and
the women voters in particular, seem to be
giving an acceptance to Freetrade views to
an extent that was not quite hoped for by
the propagandists.” That great Freetrade
revival has been just about to burst over
Melbourne since the day when Henry
Parkes wrote his first poem. And it is still
about to burst—when it gets rested and finds
its second wind.
[James Edmond, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
An heroic cablegram of last week set forth
that “200,000 men were drilling nightly in
Belfast and adjoining counties,” to be ready
to fight against Irish Home Rule, and among
them thev had 140,000 rifles. Maybe!
But in all Ulster there are less
than 1,600,000 people. Of these over
800,000 are Home Rulers. When from
the other less-than : 800,000 are deducted
women, boys, girls and the aged,
sick and insane, it must be a desperately
hard struggle to find 200,000 men to “drill
nightly.” It would probably be necessary to
stop all trams, trains, theatres, ferry-boats,
cabs and picture-shows, and close all shops,
and rake in even the police in order to get
half that number.

[C. A. Jeffries, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
Joe Cook goes round the country asking
plaintively, “Why is it our opponents will
not submit to the will of the people when
it has been so definitely expressed?” It is
a fine old Tory attitude ; and, like most Tory
attitudes, it is rotten. Wherefore doesn’t
Joe Cook ask himself why, Providence hav-
ing made a coal-miner of him (Providence
does everything), he tried to become a poli-
tician and a Cabinet Minister and a Pre-
mier? And why doesn’t Joseph ask his
friends in the rag-importing trade why, hav-
ing stocked only stay-laces to begin with,
they weren’t content to go on stocking no-
thing but stay-laces? Also, as a distin-
guished military and naval expert, he might
drop a line to Lord Roberts asking that little
man why he didn’t turn back after his first
reverse.' And, finally, as. a Methody “local
preacher,” he might throw a hymn-book at
himself for preaching a second sermon to a
sinner who hadn’t come along to the peni-
tent form at the first time of asking.
[S. H. Prior, The Bulletin Office, Sydney.]
Thus Charles Gregory Wade, ex-Premier
of N.S.W., to the electors of Leichhardt in
his own State (the writer quotes from
memory and tries to put the emphasis where
he thinks the great man put it) :
We have the experience of the United States of
America to guide us at this Juncture, and the
Federal scheme has not There been altered for 130
years. I would ask you whether America has ad-
vanced during the last 100 years. The undoubted
advance that America has made has Taken Place
under a Constitution THAT left local questions to
local people We should take the example of
America, etc.
And The Bulletin, for one. is willing to
“take the example,” especially in the matter
of High Protection. As regards the vote
which is to be taken presently re the amend-
ment of the Australian Constitution it is
worth mentioning that ten amendments of
the United States Constitution were carried
almost immediately after the said Constitu-
tion started. (It began in 1789.) In 1794-
1798 (it took four years to get an'amend-
ment through) the eleventh amendment was
carried. This eleventh job was finished nine
years after the establishment of the Consti-
tution. The fifteenth amendment of the
U.S. Constitution was definitely passed in
1870. So, after all, Australia won’t be
making an unprecedented wreck of its Fed-
eral arrangements even if the proposed
amendments are carried.
[James Edmond, The Bulletin Office. Sydney.]
F.S. : Argument has been rife in Mel
bourne, of late, on the question of strikes
and how to stop them. One “A 1 Rakim”
wrote to the Age suggesting the disfran-
chisement of strikers, and barrister Coldham
of all men, supported him. Of course such
a scheme wouldn’t work for a minute as
several authorities (H. M. Murphy, Vic-
toria’s Chief Factories Inspector, ' among
them) hastened to point out. Preference to
unionists and a system of levving on Union
funds is the likeliest remedy. The Law should
compel unions to keep publicly-audited ac-
counts, levy sufficient dues to form a consid-
erable and valuable fund, and make such
funds seizable on failure to keep an agree-
ment. The same with employers. The suf-
fering public would thus recoup itself some-
what ; the strikers would suffer through
having to submit to heavier levies to make
up the fund, and the vast majority of
workers, being then obviously in the unions
would be under some sort of control.
That weird political circum-
stance, Dr. Arthur, of N.S.W
rose up in the Sydney Tf.te-
graph last week to explain
that the Trusts in the Enina
States “have only been made
possible through the High
Protection tariff.” Now, if
High Protection is the cause
of Trusts, how do they exist
so numerously in Freetrade
Britain? An explanation 0 f
this fact would be interesting.
Also, Dr. Arthur might explain
how it is that the first great
United States Trust, the Stan-
dard Oil, grew up under abso-
lutely Freetrade conditions.
There was no Protective tariff
nor any other kind of tariff
on imported oil, unless the
goods came from a country
which imposed a duty on
United States oil. The Stan-
dard had no natural mono-
poly. Russia was an oil-pro-
ducing country before white
men went to America, and it
imposed no duty on Yankee
oil when that product came
on the market. Scotland used to export
petroleum to the U.S.A., and it also imposed
no duty on Yankee oil. Yet Standard Oil
was the first and greatest of all the Yankee
Trusts. The water-drinking doctor should
take another look at his facts.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Melbourne s various hoofball teams con-
cussed on Saturday in ideal football weather, and
some strenuous scuffles ensued. Though the season
is only two weeks old, ’Wood alone remains un-
beaten. It encountered Geelong on Saturday, and
routed it with difficulty by nine points, alter a game
that was anybody’s right up to the bell-ring. ’Wood
had the better system, but the Seasiders' marking
was excellent, and their long, accurate kicking
superb. The scores—-’Wood, io goals 17 behinds;
Geelong, 11 goals two behinds—tell their own tale,
lor some inscrutable reason the annual collision of
Sowth” and Carlton, on the latter’s ground, always
provides an exciting contest, and Saturday’s was no
exception. Neither team goes in for much system,
though South’s is the better, but in fast, clean, open
play, with fine, long kicking and good marking, both
excelled. By greater accuracy in goal shooting South
kept a few points ahead till, on starting the last
quarter, it had a goal and a half to the good. But
that last quarter belonged to the Old Blues, and they
steadily accumulated singles till the tale stood 61
all Then the bell rang on a beautiful draw—
Carlton, eight goals 13 behinds; South, nine goals
seven behinds. The Essendon-Richmond skirmish was
also exciting, the Yellow-and-Blacks having, with the
advent of a new secretary, decided to bury their
squabbles and battle as one man. They battled so
heftily that they were only four points behind ’Don
at lemon-time, and had overtaken them before the
close; but an extra-strenuous rush of the “Same
Old gave Don a couple of goals, and the game
ended: Richmond, 78 points; ’Don, 89. ’Varsity,
playing Melbourne, kept almost level till the last
interval, but failed to keep going in the final quar-
ter —the usual lack of training—-and lost by 72
points to Melbourne’s 99. ’Rov should have given
a good account of itself against St. Kilda, but seemed
to have forgotten all it ever knew about the game,
and could only accumulate a miserable 34 to Saints’
67.
* * * *
The chief Ass. scuffle—North v. ’Don—
furnished a surprise for some 5000 onlookers, and a
considerable shock to the premiers, who were held
comfortably throughout, and ultimately demolished
by 1 8 points to ’Don’s 62. Brunswick visited the
suburb once sacred to Thomas Adolphus Bent, and
defeated it with difficulty, mainlv through its utter
inability to direct the ball—’Wick, eight goals 20
behinds; Brighton, eight goals seven behinds. P’ran
paralysed Melb. City by 100 points to 45; ’Town
routed ’Cote by 60 to 37 ; and ’Scray smashed up
Port by 77 to 33. The repeated wallopings of
these disorganised teams get on the nerves; under
a proper scheme of district hoofball some of them
would annually descend to a lower grade, where they
might have a chance to break the monotony by
scoring an occasional win.
* * * *
N.S.W. Rugby League onened its compe-
tition in Sydney on Saturday, and, in spite of other
attractions, such as watching the march past of
20,000 cadets under arms, ogling Hospital girls, and
so forth, the cult of the muddv leather bubble
attracted somewhere about 40,000 people to the
various grounds. The star match of the day was
that between Eastern Suburbs and Newtown at
Sydney Cricket Ground. Eastern Suburbs was the
premier team last season, and was generally under-
stood to be in a frenzy of rage and despair because
Dally Messenger’s wife had persuaded him to retire
from the game, and he had sufficient sense to allow
himself to be persuaded. Still he left a very prom-
ising brother,, W. Messenger, to perpetuate the
family horfor and fame, and W. kicked the first goal
of the afternoon. The general opinion was that Newtown
had absolutely no hope, and would be chewed right
up, stamped flat, and generally thrown to the winds.
East opened with a charge which drove Newtown
back on to its line. But the line was cleared. East
came again, and this time burst through with great
violence, and W. Messenger kicked the goal. New-
town pulled itself together, the captain howled like
a wolf; and that mighty forward, Murray, right in
the van, began to accomplish things. He took the
bone of contention along at the toe. and was doing
splendidly when someone took it right off his toe
and reversed the position. Murray still had his toe,
but he didn’t have the ball. But this time the New-
town defence was magnificent, and one after another
the Eastern champions were hauled down and thrown
back across the line. Newtown let loose a counter
attack, and blew at a great pace right down the field
and over the line. The kick failed. Then East lost
i+s combination and went in for individualism, while
Newtown stuck to Socialism and discipline, and at
half-time led by 10 to seven. The second half was
solid bullocking from start to finish, and East, having
recovered its discipline, had the best of it. Towards
the end Newtown, now hovering in the rear,
straggled desperately to equalise, and succeeded in
reducing the margin to two. But time ran out,
the whVle blew and East was triumphant bv 17 to
15. Which in League Rugby is a mighty close thing.
Another straggle of some size was that between
North Sydney and Glebe at the former’s ground.
Glebe at first romped over the hillmen and ended
the first half with 11 points to two. The second
half saw North Svdnev make heroic efforts to wipe
out the margin, but the men of Glebe were too strong
and won with 19 to 15. Balmain managed to fust,
hold down Annandale w ; th seven points to five. The
onlv run-away victory of the day was that of South
Svdnev over the Western Suburbs team, wh'uh it
crushed bv 33 to 11. So Eastern Suburbs, Globe,
Balmain and South Svdneu are each two competition
points and the rest are nil.
* * * *
The Rugby Union also onened its compe-
tition on Saturday, and for an allegedly dying cause
it had a lot of onlookers. Evidently the old line
game still has its admirers. At University Oval,
Glebe and Western Suburbs collided in a grim, hard-
fought struggle utterly devoid of brilliance. It con-
sisted of all kicks and no limelight. Glebe won by
six points to three and a large streak of luck.
University gave St. George a torrid time at Hurst-
vine and finally rubbed its hat in the mud by 16
points to six. North Sydney put up a sparkling
game with Newtown, which also coruscated, and
victory rested with the North by 19 points to 14.
Balmain fell on South Sydney and walloped it by 29
points to 12; while Eastern Suburbs journeyed by
sea to Manly and jumped on the Village by 14 points
to 11.
* f * * *
The Australian Rulers also opened their
competition on Saturday, but, by some accident,
bungled the time-table of their star match, that
between Sydney and South Svdne.v. It was 3.40
p.m. before the sides took the field, and a good
game was spoiled by beihg played out in the dark
when most of the audience were on the way to tea
or the nearest bars. There were no startling results,
and the games ended practically true to last year’s
form, East Sydney downing Newtown by 83 points
to 29 ; while Sydney scrunched South Sydney by 81
points to 16, and Paddington overthrew North
Shore by 99 points to 60.
* * * *
The Soccer crowd, which has about three
competitions to get through during the season,
started the Rawson Cup Armageddon on Saturday.
Balmain, the champion club, raged against New-
town, overthrew it and also tell upon it at Epping
racecourse by one goal to nil. Mitchell, the aoal-
keeper of the defeated team, covered himself with
glory, though he could not avert the disaster. On
the same ground Sydney defeated Annandale by five
goals to two —a big margin at Soccer. Granville
scrunched Canterbury away out at Clyde, 13 miles
from Sydney, by three to nil, and 'Gle.be banged
Northern Suburbs by seven goals to nil. Which was
an awful doing.
* * * *
“Touchline” writes: I rise to remark that
N.S.W. Rugby Union is far from being the dead
horse which rival football bodies fondly imagine it
is. It is a case of the wish and the thought! The
Union’s international scheme makes it appear that
the looked-for demise is still several years away.
Joining hands with the Maoriland Rugby Union, the
N.S.W. body will make an effort to bring an Eng-
lish XV to Australia in 1915 and a South African
mob in 1916. Also, 1915 will probably see an Aus-
tral-Maoriland XV visit the United States. Mean-
while the inter-Sfhte and club programme is quite
big enough to keep the wreck afloat the meanwhile.
The set-back will Drobably prove to be a blessing
very well disguised.
* » * *
English papers note, as signs of the inter-
national spread of Soccer football, that an English
team has toured Portugal (of all places), and that
a 20,000 gate has been reached in Berlin. But even
20,000 is small pumpkins compared with the 111,000
at the Crystal Palace last week. Still, the Crystal
Palace isn’t Berlin.
* * * *
In all branches of Labor the workers’ dis-
satisfaction with the “speeding-up” system is finding
active expression. Last week a deputation of race-
horses waited upon the A.J.C. stewards, to protest
against the practice frequently adopted by the
“bosses” in classic events of starting animals as
pace-makers, with a view to busting the field along.
Dismal Willie, an aaed grey,l said, in introducing
the deputation, that the time was now ripe for abol-
ishing the pernicious time-limit, which, in the not-
able case of the Champion Stakes, established at
Flemington by the V.R.C., had been responsible for
many cases of leg-weariness. Slowladdie reminded
the chairman that many wealthy employers, de-
scribed as “keen sports” (neighs of derision) gave
orders for a stable compan.on of the favorite,
usually a young, impetuous beast —(neighs of
“Shame”) —to be driven along from the start, in
order to find out weak spots in the non-stayers.
Sugarloaf, who claimed to be always one of the best
in a field of non-triers, remarked that the result of
starting a pacemaker was to “speed-up” the others.
Hoarse Chestnut complained that it encouraged
sweating all round, and many other neighers warmly
supported his contention. The chairman promised
the deputation that something should be done in
the matter. He didn’t think the stewards would be
able to suppress sprinting altogether, but he thought
that the go-slow habit could be widely extended
by offering substantial prizes to the three last horses
in all handicaps and w.f.a. races. The , gathering
then kicked up its heels and withdrew.
SIDE ELEVATION OF T. O. CURRAN
as he appeared on Saturday last.
A—Superfluous tissue. B—Fat. C—Plain
bingey. D—Boil or carbuncle. E—Porous
plaster.
“Adelaide”: A word about “Brickbat’s”
denunciation of the alleged keeping down and out
of juniors in Adelaide cricket (B. 24/4/’l3). At
first sight, it is laughably incorrect, with two men
just from school in the State XI, and East Torrens
placed easily top of all the clubs by the help in great
part of men whose names Bulletin readers
outside S.A. wouldn’t know if they were set
down here. I asked the opinion of three
prominent players, all young themselves, about
“Brickbat.” “Absurd!” said two of them.
“In our clubs, we have every chance given us; and
there are two other clubs with hardly any but
juniors.” The third authority thought it might be
true concerning two other clubs, which he named.
So “Brickbat’s” mud-slinging is perhaps one-third
true. And he may remember what Tennyson wrote
of that kind of mis-statement.
Bill Lang accomplished his resurrection
stunt to the accompaniment of wild applause at
Sydney Stadium on Saturday night, when, after 20
rounds, he was awarded the verd:ct over Petty Officer
Curran. Still, it would be well for Lang to refrain
from any tendency to swelled head over the victory,
for Curran was in anything but good condition, while
Lang was about the most perfectly trained man who
ever entered a ring. He had worked hard, solidly
and conscientiously at his preparation, and, verily,
he had his reward, and also, verily, he deserved it.
It is said that Curran was heavier than on the day
when he stepped off the steamer's deck. Be that as
it may, his fatness was calculated to stagger
humanity. His stomach ballooned over his waist-
band. Across the small of his back were the traces
of a belladonna plaster, showing that the sometme
defender of Ladysmith had been suffering from rheu-
matism. How on earth he lasted the 20 rounds in
his hog-stout condition is one of the mysteries of
this world. It is said that he never trains very hard,
and has a firm belief that meat is reserve power,
which he can let loose anywhere between the 18th
and 20th round. Everybody waited on Saturday
night to see the “letting loose,” but it never came
off’ ami Curran didn’t sparkle a cent. His seconds
said he had ricked his side ; someone else in author-
ity said he had damaged his hand ; but many believe
his bingey had fallen on him and killed hm. Lang,
on the other hand, had only the bingey requisite and
necessary to carry on the work of assimilation, it
was flat as a board, if not actually concave, and
Lang's care in keepihg it out of the danger zone is to
be highly commended. Here endeth the chapter on
bingeys.
The actual fight started splendidly. In the begin-
ning Curran rushed into the van of the battle with
a splendid burst of energy and ferocity. He hunted
Lang all over the ring, and finally sent him down for
four. Then Lang grew wary, and met Curran’s
rushes with straight lefts, which were hard to en-
dure. But Curran bored in, and while taking a
monumental hiding administered one just as large.
He had a good defence, and he crossed like lightning
and shook Lang badly. While his steam was at full
head Curran did magnificently, and belted the devil
out of Lang in spite of the superior condition of the
Austral an. Lang saved himself considerably by
good footwork, and at every opportunity his left fell
like a falling tenement. For a time Curran man-
aged to cross quickly and cleanly with the right,
and the result was that for a time Lang’s nose wa«
seriously deteriorated, and 10 per cent, had to be
written off the feature for depreciation. Then Lang
managed to split Curran’s cheek, and the 12th, the
most exciting round of the battle, ended amidst a
storm of applause. After that Curran’s attack wilted
and faded out, the seaman acting entirely on
the defensive and only occasionally sending heavy
wallops as he retreated. Lang grew more and more
aggressive, and Curran, about whom there is no
beastly pride, did not hesitate to literally flee before
the fury of the storm. But alwavs he came back,
advanced as if to the attack, and fell into a clinch.
The 14th found Curran still sprinting, and so did
the 15th, but in this he came back and opened an
old wound over Lang’s eye. In the 18th Curran
made a brave attempt to cut loose, but couldn’t. He
was moored to his bingey. The 19th was a round of
clinches and heavy body blows, and so was the 20th.
Then the gong sounded, and Scott placed his hand
on Lang’s head. Curran sat down, had his face
washed, went over and shook hands, apparently
quite unmoved. And yet it was a hard fight, full
of heavy battery. And that was how Lang came
back.
* * * *
Another man who talks of coming back is
none other than the one and only Jim Jeffries. His
last effort was a ghastly- failure, and Johnson was
supposed to have killed his aspiration for a return
ticket. The mention of his intended resurrection
moved some of the scribes to dwell on his general
demeanor prior to that downfall. There was a small
army of newspaper men at his camp writing up his
training, and it seems to have been the great fighter’s
joy in life to insult every one of them during the
course of the day. Johnson was fairly arrogant, but he
is said to have been all charm and modesty compared
to the bull-necked Jeffries. The latter’s resurrection
announcement roused a storm of press disapproval,
in which he was accused of having thrown away
the championship to Johnson for the sake of the
boodle he would get out of it. win, lose or draw. It
is hardly a fair charge, as Johnson was practically
champion before he met Jeffries. Jeffries had no
championship to lose, as he had prev'ously retired.
But Jeffries seems to be only a shade less unpopular
than Johnson himself.
* * * *
Talking of Johnson reminds that Samuel
Fitzpatrick, who brought the negro to Australia,
and practically gave him his start in glory by bring-
ing off the fight with Burns, got no share of the
wealth Johnson garnered as the result of that vic-
tory. He has now retired from the strenuous task
of looking after pugs, and settled down in a quiet,
comfortable cafe on 24th-street, New York. He
remarks that he lifted Johnson from the free-lunch
class, and if the authorities will let the Murky One
get away from Chicago, Johnson is welcome to the
best his free-lunch counter affords. But, somehow,
the authorities seem to hate the thought of letting
Johnson out of their sight, and haye more than once
gone to a lot of trouble to bring him back when he
has strolled away.
* * *
A 1 Palzer, who for a time loomed large as
a White Hope, is now general'y regarded as what the
Yankees call a “bust volcano.” He is, or was lately,
in Paris, weiring to fix up a fight with Johnson as
soon as the Streak could get over his argument
with the Law authorities in Chicago. Palzer belongs
to the numerous class known as “natural fighters,”
a type of scrapper which rapidly degenerates when
hard work is given over. He had several spells of
idleness, and could not be induced to train properly
for what fights he did have in between, with the re-
sult that he is said to have cut a poor figure in the
ring. What fights- these were is impossible to say,
as they were evidently not worth cabling, and the
commentators do not give details. But one thing is
evident—that Palzer has either slumped or got
up the back of the great Amurkan press.
* * * *
After much going up and down the conti-
nent, newspaper puffing, alleged secrecy, and other
frills, E. R. Mayne got some sort of a cricket team
together for his raid on the United States. Twenty-
five engagements have already been fixed up, and the
team is open for more. Which sounds strange along-
side Mr. Mavne’s statement that the team is going
for pleasure only and not for boodle, and has not
even considered how it will whack the profits should
any accrue. Of course, it is an easy thing to avoid
having any profits. Only 12 men are going, and a
reserve of one is a mighty weak entrenchment for
a foreign raid. Trumper did not go. Business
matters compelled him to give up the idea, though
he liked it very much. Altogether the Mayne cric-
ket team is just as much of a mystery as it was at
the beginning.
N.S.W. Cyclists’ Union has definitely de-
cided that Australia shall be represented by a road-
racer in the Olympic Cycle Race at Berlin in 1916.
A fund has been started to collect the £2OO which
it is reckoned will be necessary to do the thing pro-
perly. The next job is to find the man, who must
be an extra special, dyed-in-the-wool champion of
road work. He wll take more finding than the
£2OO, mine tink it. as Billy. Rex would say. To
assist in the job of locating him a 130-mile race is
to be held this winter between Goulburn and Sydney,
over the route of the old-time Dunlop fixture. The
date has (n.v.) been fixed for June 23.
* * * »
T.ast year the Dunlop Co. offered to donate
£25 towards a Tamwort.h-Newcastle road race for
cyclists, but owing to the usual trouble between
the northern clubs of N.S.W. and the, League of
Wheelmen, the idea was dropped. However, the
sky is now calfn, the outlook halcyon, and the idea
has revived, like the grass in the” soft spring rain.
The Dunlop gift of £25 for first prize is understood
to be still on tap, and this season is likely to see
the event pulled off. The distance is 160 miles and
the roads are often figures of speech only; so, be-
sides being the longest of road races it is likely to
be the toughest.

Saturday was a day of rout and butcherv
among the Sydney bowlers in the preliminary rounds
of the Rawson Cun. The result of the riot and
rout-was to leave City, Mosman, Parramatta and St
George to battle in the semi-final next Saturday
afternoon. Another disturbance the same afternoon
was the final of the “B” competition. The Orange
(N.S.W.) rink fell on Mosman ip the morning and
walloped it by 69 to 61, and in the afternoon it got
loose on and all over Ryde by 69 to 64, and so won
the “Blanky” competition ; which was good work on
top of a wearisome railway journey of about 200
miles. But if the trip in the train was wearisome
the stay in Sydney was splendid, for the Svdnev
bowlers gave their conquerors a right royal time.
Lacrosse let itself loose on Saturday with
the loud howl of the Yankee aborigine, and when
the dust cleared away it was discovered that Sydney
had outed the mermen from Manly bv 11 goals to
three. Petersham made a hash of the'haughty gang
from Burwood by nine goals to three. Mosman and
Birchgrove journeyed to Sydney Domain to settle
their difference, but discovered that all available
space was already occupied by young Australia, and
were, perforce, obliged to put away their onion nets
and tell weird tales about girls they used to know.
Altogether, Sydney, once known as the city of parks
and poverty, finds that it has not half enough open
spaces for the rising generation to stretch its muscles
in. _ There’s an opening for some budding State
politician to earn deathless fame bv devising a
method to endow Sydney with about 10 more recrea-
tion grounds within easy reach of the residential
portions of the metropolis of Congestion.

“J.G.” : This is a sample of the fair deal
the Loafing party gives the Labor party. A
week or so back the Confusionist candidate
for Corio (Vic.), one Ivendell, dropped into
a one-horse, one-pub village, called Bever-
idge, which has the distinction of holding the
Merriang Shire Hall. As Kendell had come
along to bless Cook and curse Fisher, the
overjoyed councillors fell over each other to
welcome him as the savior of their cow-
sheds. The Shire Hall was thrown open to
him, and he spouted his spout therein, no
charge being made for the use of the rate-
payers’ premises. About a week afterwards
Ozanne, the Labor member for Corio, ar-
ranged to address the electors in the same hall
that was given to Kendell free of charge.
But when the councillors were asked for the
use of the spider-infested building the coun-
cillors professed not to know Ozanne from
an ink bottle; they said that before the
premises could be handed over to him, three
ratepayers would have to present a petitioh
or something to the council, saying that he
was a respectable person, and was not carry-
ing bombs or other pleasant things in his
coat tails. And after this document had
been sniffed at, Ozanne was required to pay
for the use of the structure. On second
thoughts, I don’t wondef at the Fat party
reckoning that, if the Laborites got the
powers they ask for, they would make it hot
for the other crowd. It apparently doesn’t
itself know any use for power except to
make things unpleasant for the other party.
[J. Grills, Wallan, Victoria.]
