The Bishop of Bath and Wells writes : “The
value of Formamint as a preventive is
appreciated in this house.”
The Archdeacon of Bristol writes : “I habitually
carry Wulfing’s Fonuamint with me as a
preventive of Sore Throat.”
The Earl of Kilmorey writes : “I am glad to give
niv testimony to the soothing and curative
effects of your potent remedy—Formamint,
with which lam so familiar. As is my
constant practice, I keep one of your brown
bottles on my dressing table.”
How to Avoid Infectious Diseases.
Do you realise that most of the dangerous infectious
diseases, such as Diphtheria, Consumption, Influenza, Scarlet
Fever, Measles and the like, are caused by germs which gain
entrance to the body through the mouth and throat ?
The minute living organisms which produce such diseases
grow and develop like the seeds of plants, and the mouth and
throat form an ideal liot-house for cultivating them.
The scientist, of course, can grow them outside the body,
artificially. For example, a test-tube culture of the germs
which cause Consumption is depicted here ; and also a bottle
of Wulfing’s Formamint—the means which Science has found
to destroy such germs in the mouth and throat, and so protect
us against the diseases which they breed.
T*. WULFJNG \S • j
rormamint
THE CERM-KILLING THROAT TABLET
These harmless and palatable tablets, contain the most
powerful destroyer of disease germs known to Science. They
dissolve in the saliva, and the disinfectant is thus carried to
every little crevice, chink, or cranny in the mouth, gums,
tonsils and throat. Thus all germ growths are sought out and
destroyed, their spread is checked, their power to do harm is
entirely broken.
As a preventive of infectious disease, Formamint Tablets
should be used daily by everyone, in health or sickness, and
they are particularly recommended for school children, who
are so exposed to infection, especially at this time of the year.
Beware, however, of harmful imitations ; there is only one
genuine Formamint, viz. Wulfing’s, and all Chemists sell it at
2s. 6d. per bottle. Tli£ manufacturers, A. Wulfing & Co.,
17, Bond Street, Sydney, N.S.W., will gladly send you a Free
Sample and an interesting Booklet, if you write to them,
mentioning this paper, and enclose two penny stamps towards
postage.
‘ O.K. : One more reply to “F.C.” should
bring the Flemington Elat discussion to a finish. In
his comparison between the non-paying flatites and
the paying public’s view of the racing, “F.C.” care-
fully dodges the fact that thousands, not hundreds,
of people on the Flat, which is the inside ring of the
course, can get a close, right-up-to-the-rails view of
many races, particularly jumping races, at points
where the horses’ colors are barely distinguishable
through strong field-glasses to the faraway paying
public. If “F.C.” chooses to place himself in the
back row of a dense crowd, right opposite the win-
ning-post, and then complains that he can see no-
thing of the horses, I would beg him to try and
understand that thousands of people on the Lawn at
Flemington on a Cup Day have just the same cause
for complaint. As to the inconsistency of “F.C.”
and his co-barrackers for the freedom of the Flat—
some of them complaining that they can’t see the
races, whilst others state that it isn’t the races they
are worrying about —I have already said enough.
But seeing that “F.C.” questions the club’s rights,
and the Premier’s right to admit those rights, and
says, “Anyhow, somebody might advantageously pour
a lot of light on this subject,” I will be the pourer
of as much light as there is to pour. In 1871, when
the Melbourne Cup was already beginning to be
talked of as an all-Australian or federalising event,
the V.R.C. became anxious about its tenure of the
Flemington ground, and a V.R C. Bill, which gave
the club a 99 years’ lease of the property for public
racing and racehorse training purposes, was read in
the State Legislative Assembly. Hansard records of
the proceedings show that the only critical comment
on the Bill came from Mr. Vale, who suggested that
some portion of the land should be free to the public.
Nobody said ditto to Mr. Vale, nor did any Minister
reply to him. The House evidently reckoned that
the V.R.C. was doing good advertising work for
A ictoria, and had proved its fitness to be granted
control of the land. The only question raised in
committee was whether the premises might be used
for reviews, and the then secretary (Mr. Bagot)
said it all depended on the state of the weather. A
review on a very wet day would cut the course to
pieces. The V.R.C. Bill was passed in September,
and came into force in November, 1871. The Select
Committee, in its report to the House, made no men-
tion of any suggestion, understanding or promise
of free admission to any part of the property. Some
20 years ago men used to walk from Footscrav to
Newmarket, across the V.R.C. ground, and they
wore a narrow path in the turf. When tackled by the
club officials, they demanded right-of-way, and finally
a friendly law suH was arranged at the club’s ex-
pense. The champions of the right-of-way lost the
suit, as a matter of course. Some years later the
club—which all the time had been leaving the Flat
free, with the shrewd business object of increasing
the popularitv of thq famous Cup, conceded to the
public a walk along the Saltwater River, off the
racecourse, and converted some superfluous high land,
north of the Members’ Drive, into a public park.
Trees were planted and seats placed on it. The
Footscray Council, at about that time, wanted some
of the land beyond the racecourse for a public park,
and the club let the council have a tract of the im-
proved property for a fourth of its market value.
The V.R.C. has been an absolute boss of the whole
property for 42 years. For most of that time it
was good business policy to run a free Flat, even
as an hotelkeeoer runs a free luncheon. Now the
time is more than rioe for demanding of the flatites
the same “bob” admission which they part up cheer-
fully to the V.A.T.C. and other big racing clubs
everywhere, whose land is not a freehold. What is
there more to say ?
“0.K.” : The intense toryism of the pow-
ers that be in cricket is frankly confessed by their
latest reform bill. Public interest in the game as
a gate-money show has been steadily declining for
years past because, life seemed too short for it. On
a wet wicket the batsmen came and went quicklv
enough-—if the bowlers were allowed to get at them.
But, as a rule, wet turf meant a rainy day and a
suspension of the game. Or, if the sky was blue,
the wicket was said to be unplayable, and the game
was suspended to give the ground time to dry. An
unwet wicket meant, in the ordinary way, a dull,
slow game, because the bowlers couldn’t hit the
stumps often enough, or tempt the batsmen to hit
the ball up and give chances to the fieldsmen. There-
fore, the public wearied of cricket matches under
existing conditions. The public wanted taller and
broader stumps, not to mention a somewhat smaller
ball; so that the bowler’s chance of hitting the
stumps whenever he “beat the batsman,” would be
greatly improved. The one thing the public did not
want was an enlargement of the “leg before” rule,
which would necessarily put the batsman more than
ever at the mercy of the umpire, and might result in
a whole side being given out l.b.w, unless the experts
ceased to stop certain balls with the pad and blocked
them with the bat instead. Obviously, the ball that
can be deliberately stopped by a padsman with im-
punity, can be almost ©r quite as easily stopped by
his bat when he cultivates the new habit. It is the
same old ball, and the exact same old wicket is be-
hind him. Given the same ball, or a fractionally
smaller one to play at, with a larger wicket to de-
fend from the attack, and the game would at once
gain in interest for the spectator. The cricket
authorities are opposed to this inevitable reform, be-
cause it would compel the batsmen of the present
day to abandon their cherished cut-and-dried rules
of procedure. A radical change of play would be in-
troduced. The new generation would adapt their
systems or “art” of batting to the new conditions.
Torvism hates radical changes. The very thought of
(say) a four-stumps wicket moves Toryism to scorn,
seeing that our forefathers established the rule of
three. So Toryism, forced by financial considerations
to propose some sort of cricket reform, suggests a
new law of l.b.w. more unsatisfactory to the public
than the existing law. Can it conceivably make the
game more lively for the onlookers? Can it possibly
shorten the length of a game except by increasing the
proportion of batsmen who would be got out in an
objectionable manner ?
N.S.W. Cyclist Union, at its annual meet-
ing the other night discussed the question of sending
an Australian rider to compete in the great road
race at the next Olympic Games. Two great finds
have to be made —first, the man and then the money
with which to send him. The man must be a real
Champion of champions; no ordinary “good” rider
will do. He must be a real, dyed-in-the-wool flier.
As he will take some discovering and developing, it
is proposed to start looking for him right away. As
for the money, it is thought that if a start were
made immediately, it should be possible, by the
year 1916, to have enough in hand to send the man
in a proper manner. The 1916 Olvmniad will take
place at Berlin, where Kaiser Bill eats his sauer-
kraut.
The Titanic must be a very rusty wreck
by now. but the last faint echoes of the in-
quiry that followed the smash still rumble
in the small print of the shipping column.
Last week it was stated thrt British ship-
owners had received, “with considerable
satisfaction.” the statement that the U.S.
Government would accept the new British
Board of Trade rules as to life-saving appli-
ances as equivalent to their own, so far as
ocean-passenger steamers were concerned.
But public interest in the important subject
of life-saving at sea fell down dead long
ago, and few men besides those directly in-
terested will again think seriously about
boats, belts or rafts, until another bad wreck
tests the allegedly increased safeguards. The
only guide the casual traveller has to go by
is the British Board’s proved incompetence,
as instanced by the Titanic horror, and the
way “boat drill” is attended to on most
steamers. Writer recently travelled on an
Australian coastal boat, which, according to
one of the crew, has never put a lifeboat in
the water since he had been in her. Once a
month or so, they have “stations.” Then, at
the whistle of command, the man who makes
the beds and the bloke who peels the pota-
toes and casts the rind into the sea, amble
to the boat deck and range themselves by
their respective life-sieves. Presently the
hawk-eyed captain strides along, picks out
the potato artist at random, and asks him
sharply what he has to do when the bleak
hour comes. The potato man replies respect-
fully that he will be lowered with the tub,
or that he is to assist with the bow tackle,
or that he will commit bigamy, or forge a
cheque, or something. The captain nods
curtly, and moves on to interrogate the bed-
maker or the steward, who blows the bugle
at seasick passengers, and tries to make the
half-dead come to judgment in the saloon.
Two or three such inquiries, and the bos’n’s
whistle pipes all hands to their scuffling
jobs, while the moveless lifeboats bake on
the chocks, and the rust and paint clog the
derricks and w'eather-bleached tackle. The
nervous sea-traveller’s best line is to make
for the newest ship on the run. Its boats
are less likely to be wedded to their lofty
roosts with rust and paint than those of the
oldest ship. The crew is probably just as
ignorant about the management of them as
the crew of the more ancient vessel. But
it seems hardly possible that it can be more
ignorant. No man can know less than half
of nothing.
“J.R.”: Why all this mystery anent the
Scott expedition? A recent cable says that
Commander Evans had been interviewed at
Port Said, and had denied certain rumors
about his namesake, the petty officer:—
There was certainly no friction with the rest of
the southern party, and no reason to believe that
Petty-Officer Evans was insane. Possibly he died of
scurvy, but there was no positive knowledge of it. He
was only carried on the sledge on the day of his
death.
But there should have been some positive
knowledge of what he died of, since one of
the party was a qualified medical man. Plere
is a curious admission in view of the persist-
ent assertion that none of the details of the
final tragedy have been deliberately sup-
pressed :
The members of the expedition have not seen five
of the diaries, which have been handed to the rela-
tives of the deceased.
Why were they not retained and compared
with the leader’s diary to ensure that no
detail had been omitted? It is an invariable
rule that private journals kept by members
of exploring expeditions sent out by the
British Admiralty are to be given up for use
in compiling the records of the expedition.
It is not necessary that every member of the
expedition should have access to every other
member’s diary, but, especially in a case like
this, where the leader loses his life, the pri-
vate journals of all the members should be
available to those who compile the final
records: or. rather, there should be no pri-
vate journals, but each member should keep
a daily record of all he saw and did.

moral ants must have bitten in their time at
the base of the age-defying Pyramids, under
the delusion that they were sins of a pecu-
liarly obstinate sort. Finally, the most
interfering woman in Australasia asserts
(Sydney Sun. 22/4/T3), that "we are
becoming a nation of lust and immorality
because we have too much luxury and olea-
sure.” There will be a brighter outlook for
our salvation “when he have to work 50
times harder, and have no spare time to
attend picture shows, prize fights, or go
surf-bathing.” So, after all, it isn’t cheap
labor that is our ruin ; it is all due to clean-
liness, exercise, fresh air, leisure and pros-
perity. AVhy does the fussy holy person always
urge, by implication, the advantages of dirt,
flabbiness, stuffiness, hard work and poverty?
Also, wouldn’t Mrs. Lee-Oowie be perfectly
angelic, if she had to do 50 times more wash-
ing and ironing, and had less time to attend
to the business of the wide world? Mrs.
Lee-Cowie indicates one of the curious
defects of womenkind in general. A woman
must either have a household and regard it
as her world, or have the world and rpgard
it as her household, to be swent, and l : «sed,
and spanked at her own sweet ’mil. While
she’s alive, she’s going to raise a dust some-
where.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
W.W. (W.A.) : Appears to be a matter
for local disturbance —in the meantime, anyhow....

paper is stale now. In any case, the yelp of the
immigrant from Aberdeen, who failed to get his
hands on to Australian bawbees five minutes after
landing, that he had fallen amongst “damned thieves
and rogues,’’ won’t hurt Australians, and may help
to keep other breathless bandits anchored at Aber-
deen W.A.W.: You’re a dashed plumber.....

M.H.R., does claim that during Labor rule the mar-
riage and birth rates have been unusually high and
the death-rate unusually low ? When Bill Smith,
who has been clerking at 32s. 6d. per week, gets a
job in a new factory and makes 63s. a week, lie
naturally thinks of a Mrs. B. Smith, and there s
something wrong if the birth-rate doesn t presently
respond. And little Smith has a much better chance
of pulling through its teething when Bill gets 63s.
a week than it would have had if William had
chanced it and married on his old 32s. 6d. . . . .

dies for the Present Discontent,’’ is that it is asinine..
Freda O.: Some of the verse is very good, but we
can’t stand item re Ananias. Ananias, so far as we
know, onlv told one lie—nothing else is recorded
against him. He was a wildjy gepetras man who
was giving away money, his ne being to the ettect
that, in his mad extravagance, he was giving away
all his money, while he was really keeping a trine
back to buy boots with. There is hardly a drapery
shop-hand in Sydney who doesn’t do the Ananias act
at least once a day, as a matter of course in order
to save his job. This paper doesn’t actually admire
Ananias, but it regards him as a shamefully ill-used
Jew W r .O.K.: George Reid number sold out....

and the art of turning ghastly incidents into verse is

Art and possess Vivid inspiration, which aids my Task.
Wanderings, hardships and bitter Trials as blighted
my life, but never the less I endeavour to do my
best under' the circumstances: —
A STORMY NIGHT IN THE SLUMS.
The Mongolians closed their window
And listened to the sound
Of the beating rain and whistling winds
Which fiercely raged around
Huddled within their hovels
With their oriental chums
Fan tan was suggested while
The storm raved o’er the slums
Within an alley habitation lay a
Rag picker’s dying child
While the peltering rain descended
And the wind did waileth wild
Piteously mourned the wretched father
With two sympathetic chums
Waiting the arrival of a doctor
While the storm raved o’er the slums.”
. . . . H.T.O.: The poem practically blows up in the
second verse and scatters its fragments over the
countryside... .W.R.B.: Axe-marks all over it. A
rough product... .N.F.: An unsigned, indelible pen-
cil poem about blowflies is about the last contribu-

handle it Wallie'B".: Spelling is the only original
thing about it.... Amos M.: Promising theme, but
poor verse. Also, where are your name and address ?
A.A.: Well, if you hadn’t tied yourself up in
knots by using only one rhyme throughout each of-
the first two verses, you might have managed to say
more; you might also have been more humorous.
Can’t say for certain, because we don’t know how
much you know, or how. humorous you are. Don’t
feel inclined to go any deeper into the poem ; for
one thing, it isn’t'worth it. (P.S. —Nearly forgot
to call your attention to the fact that “whack”
doesn’t rhyme with “fat.”) H.W.D.: Your senti-
ments do you credit, but the verses really are not
worth a d —n W.W.R.: Verses trip along to a
tame conclusion. Conclusions should mostly be as
wild and unexpected as a hungry zoo Jack A.L.:
Too labored —it sweats at every pore Cadonia:
Cheerful enough, but only worth a par at most....
W.B.D.: (1) If you. are genuinely interested, that is
to say, if you are about to indulge in a marriage-
office wife, The Bulletin’s Past Grand Barmaid
may investigate. (2) A reverend of that name and
address is honorably recorded in the Sydney Direc-

tailed snort? Well, the central idea is a literary
antique, and you have bagged it up anyhow in thread-
bare phrases. The construction of the sentences is
poor, the punctuation baffling, and there is at least
one break in the spelling. How did the convict
know the lady’s swelling bosom was also aching?
She hadn’t spoken to him. Then what do you mean
by this: “James Faber gave himself for lost”? You
probably meant to say, “gave himself up for lost.”
It is a battered old phrase, anyhow John S.:
There must be some good points about being dead, or

are dead, and most people have been dead most of
the time. After reading your contribution we urge
you to go and be dead. Be in the fashion. Why
be a back-number when you can be buried for
37s. 6d. ?

The American Ring Pulveriser
is designed for
Crushing and pulverising limestone, lime, coke, ore,
brickbats, sewer pipe, terracotta, glass, quartz, phos-
phate rock, sandstone, gravel, tile crockery, flint,
manganese ore, oyster shells, stove lining, shale and
all other refradlory materials.
Made in Six Sizes.
The various sizes are made with a view of meeting
the requirements of the small, medium and large
consumer.
Crushing and Pulverising to Fines.
Those materials of a refradlory nature can be fed to
the machine in 3in. to 6in. cubes and smaller.
#§r
The several degrees of fines are secured by installing Grate Bars containing interstices of various widths. A measured
degree of fines is secured by running the materials over a given mesh screen, returning the rejections to the Pulveriser. The
coarser the grind the greater the tonnage capacity, while the finer the grind the smaller the tonnage capacity. The more
dry the material is made, the faster and finer the machine will pulverise.
Strength and Durability.
Careful observations, based upon adtual experience, have resulted in adding to and making extremely strong all parts
of the machine. Extended research and grinding tests year in and year out have brought about the use of Manganese Steel,
a metal that will best resist abrasion and withstand the greatest impaCL
We shall be pleased to show you these in operation in Sydney. Send for Catalogue.

Vegetable Creek (N.S.W.) is at a critical
point in its career. So far, it has mostly benefited
the pocket of the man who sits heavily on the pro-
perty and draws a killing royalty. The co. has been
scratching hard to save the weary shareholders from
too frequent calls. The necessary policy has been
to open up as many working faces as possible, when-
ever cash permitted. Latest advice is that the gross-
cut from the third shaft sunk (Allen’s) has struck
the outside edge of a body of wash, going 501 b. per
yard, but it is too early yet to predict what the
new find is worth. However, this much seems to be
clear, that, if a few decent patches are not discov-
ered from this shaft, which is nearest the scene of
the rich yields of the early days, the future of the
lower portions of the bed will look decidedly glum.
The Coppabella (N.S.W.) silver-lead-zinc
mmes have been re-leased by a Melbourne syndicate
which intends to do some prospecting. The lode is
reported to be wide, and assays are glorious, and as
the show is only eight miles from the Murray River
and 30 miles from Germanton, the conditions for
cheap working are unusually good. What is wanted
is a mine. It is always good to have a mine about
the place.
Eleanora (Hillgrove, N.S.W.) has treated
64 tons of ore for a return of 81oz. of gold bullion.
This crushing was obtained from the winze which
has now connected No. 7 with No. 8 level. There
was an average of 6ft. of lode material in this winze
for a depth of 74ft., and the average value is estimated
at loz. per ton. But the official report stated that
only 61 p.c. of the ore was suitable for milling. No.
8 level has been rather disappointing, for the assays
(15dwt. per ton) are below those obtained in sink-
ing the winze, and the ore in No. 8 level doesn’t
appear to be so heavily impregnated with antimony.
No. 9 level has been driven 37ft. north-west, but
assays are rather low (Bdwt.). “The hoisting facili-
ties are barely able to deal with the waste rock that
is produced from development work” sums up the
situation.
“Kobbi”: The Silverspur (Q. ), the co.
whose property camps near the sliprails that let the
State Frights to and from N.S.W., has just con-
cluded a further annual meeting. But the report
would not cheer a tottering shareholder. In the
early part of the year a drought came in and de-
manded free board and lodging, and the visitor
stayed such a time on the premises that smelting
operations had to knock off. Then a lawsuit about
a man “who departed this life,” so to speak, on the
premises, happened along also, and the lawsuit won.
And a co. which is chasing an elusive lode, as the
Silverspur co. is, can’t afford lawsuits. In fact,
Silverspur is not too sure that it can afford any-
thing just at present. The value for matte for the
year runs into only £12,058 ; the previous year it
was £18,250. An English gentleman, according to
the directors’ report, has taken a six months’ option
over the mine, thinking that, with improved
methods, a better job can be made of the extraction
of the metals —gold, copper, silver, lead and zinc.
The directors appeal for a railway to link their show
tip with Inglewood, and reckon that it would make
dividends possible. But at present Denham has not
as much as a toy track to give away.
Charters Towers is rather disappointed
at the Papuan’s crushing from the Moonstone reef.
The last lot (411 tons for £2859) is a slump com-
pared with the previous return of 262 tons for
£2981. The mine, in order to increase its stoping
area, has had to push out its levels to the north-
east, and the reef in that direction isn’t over rich.
The deepest level (No. 4), at 158 ft. to the N.E., has
2ft. of low-grade stone in the face. The driving
of No. 2 N.E. has also been resumed, and the reef
at the end shows an improvement.
New Queen Cross, Charters Towers, owes
its good fortune to the rich vein on the hanging-
wall side of the Moonstone formation, but one good
crushing and the development of one shallow level
don’t ipake a mine, any more than one swallow makes
a dinner. The fact that stone worth £l2 per
ton was left untouched for over 20 years, although
in close proximity to old workings, just illustrates
the luck of the game, and more particularly the
luck of the Queen Cross.
* * * *
“Kobbi” : Central Bananaland’s two chief
coal mines seem to be drifting apart like the “two
little girls in blue.” Blair Athol, which has a long
stretch of railway to trundle its coal over, has just
handed another sixpenny dividend to its owners
(100,000 shares in all), which brings up its return to
the shareholder to a total of ss. 9d. The output
for the quarter which has just been pushed to the
rear was 20,999 tons. The Bluff, which has the ad-
vantage of being nearer the sea, is somewhat handi-
capped in point of quantity (at least), and has just
been tapping its owners on the shoulder for the loan
of “a bob” on the 6000 newly issued shares, which
have now been called up to 6s. out of the £1 due.
This scribe opines that the Bluff made some wild
mistakes in the beginning. For instance, when the
Bluff was in reality the only black horse in the field,
it wasted its feed, so to speak, by attempting to coke
a non-coking coal. After that it hunted about the
neighborhood to gather up sundry coal shows, and
wasted its estate in proving the worth of these
odds and ends. And they were not worth much as
thimrs have turned out. Another show in the coal
hunt, which ought to make itself heard before long,
is the Newcastle Coal Cos., outside of Blair Athol.
It is credited with 40ft. of fuel at 133 ft., and is now
only waiting for the small branch line to be com-
pleted, when the co. will commence to hit the con-
sumer with the usual “best coal in the world.”
Another month ought to hear the thud.
Jack McDermott, for 6i years superin-
tendent of the Sons of Gwalia (W.A.), has been
given the management of the Cam and Motor gold
mine, Rhodesia (Africa). Th's show is one of a
group bossed by the Goldfields Rhodesia Develop-
ment Cos., and is alleged to be practically a virgin
property, with over a million and a half in sight.
F. A. Govett, an ardent admirer of McDermott, is
on the Board, and this probably explains how he
got the job. As an underground man, McDermott
had few equals in Australia, and probably no
superiors. When it came to treatment, however, he
was not brilliant.
* * * *
“Quarry Hill”: The legal manager’s game
is not what it used to be. During the infancy of
the gold industry, and then of the silver industry,
and next of the copper industry, the legal manager
got fine chances to make money, and he generally
took them. Besides getting a good wage from each
co. he managed, he was always on the ground floor,
and sometimes on the basement below that, in good
“floats.” In those days brokers were allowed to pay
him half commission on any scrip orders he sent
them, and during the silver boom such business
mounted up to dizzy figures. But the Stock Ex-
changes, greedy for lucre, stopped all rebate com-
missions, and half the legal manager’s income was
swept away. Then gold mining began to slither
down hill.”and its journev does not seem finished.
Look at the dead goldfields of Victoria—Rutherglen,
Steiglitz, Elaine, Stawell, Pitfield. Clunes, Creswick,
Wood’s Point. Jamieson, Smvthesdale, Scarsdale,
Malmsburv, Maldon and Walhalla. Bad management
and Stock Exchange gambling are not wholly re-
sponsible for this decadence of mining. The writer
believes it is due largely to the departure for Tophet
or Heaven of the first generation of mining men of
grit, imagination—and ignorance. Their sons hold
the dollars and lack the ambition to grab more.
Also, they are short of pluck and not so big in their
ideas. They hate pain and discomfort, and love
motor-cars and girls. And roing down a mine is
dirty work and a little dangerous. So mining lan-
guishes from want of broad-minded investors and
big-minded men. Incidentally, it languishes because
you can’t dig the same lot of gold out twice.

ALTONA!
Melbourne’s Coming . .
Garden Seaside Suburb.
A magnificent town-planning project by which it is proposed to convert
2500 acres of ideal building land, having 2]/i miles of beach frontages,
at ALTONA, near Williamstown, into a Model Seaside Suburb, and
to provide all facilities for Continental Bathing; in short, to create
An Atlantic City for Australia.
AN IDEAL BEACH.
Nowhere around Melbourne is there a beach so well suited to this purpose as
at Altona, where for over two miles there is a natural esplanade and hard, shelving
sand (no mud, stone or seaweed), with good deep water.
■***»s&
I
a
a«SS*
THE ALTONA HOMESTEAD,
above pictured, is situatfed right at the beach, and shows the possibilities of the
soil for the making of ideal gardens. Mr. H. T. Howard, the Sydney surveyor who
designed the garden suburbs of Sydney, has published the statement that he has
never yet had one-tenth of the natural advantages to work upon that Altona
possesses.
<v
\ >
w
:■
x
A WONDERFUL FEATURE OF ALTON A
is that everything is all ready, as though Nature had so designed it, for the planning
of a model suburb. There is an abundant supply of blue metal for road making,
.stone for foundations, clay, sand, cement and lime for brickworks, coal for generat-
ing power, light, etc., and the best soil for gardens. There are several plantations
of fine ornamental trees, including tamarisks, Moreton Bay figs, pines, firs, box, etc.

::

A SPLENDID PIER,
1300 ft. in length, is already at Altona, and the company propose to take advantage .
of it to create a ferry service between St. Kilda, South Melbourne, Port Melbourne,
Williamstown and Altona. Before long an excursion to Altona, for bathing and
fishing, will be considered one of the greatest delights that Melbourne can afford.

THE EASY ACCESSIBILITY
of Altona will be one of its greatest features. As a rule new suburbs are severely
handicapped, in their early years, through want of proper transportation facilities.
Altona already has the advantage of t!wo lines of railway, one the Government line
from Melbourne, via Newport, and the other consisting of three miles of railway
junctioning with the Government line at the Williamstown Racecourse platform.
The also proposes to negotiate for the construction of an electric tram-
way from Prince’s Bridge to Altona, via Port Melbourne and Williamstown.
mm
Wm PPPIP^
THE ALTONA COLLIERIES
are well known, and this great industry alone would justify the creation of a new
suburb at Altona. Vast, however, as are the deposits of brown coal, the exploita-
tion of them will be merely incidental to the greater proposition —that of giving
Melbourne a magnificent model seaside suburb. At the same time these collieries
are a wonderful asset. The Melbourne Age says: “A field like that at Altona Bay
might be found capable of yielding enough power to work the whole of our suburban
railways electrically, or > lighting the city and supplying power to industrial
consumers.”
The PORT PHILLIP ESTATES AND RAILWAY, LTD., is now registered to carryout this magnificent project.
The Company Is fortunate in having secured, as its directing head, Mr. ARTHUR RICKARD, the most successful and
progressive subdivi-sional expert in Australia.

BRISBANE.
IN BANANA LAND
Quite an identity was old Peter Mcßorie,
of Bundaberg, who handed in his checks the
other day. Peter was one of the early
Labor battlers in Southern Queensland, and
accqmpanied William Lane in his Para-
guayan pilgrimage. He was prouder, though,
of the fact that he had fought beside Peter
Lalor at Eureka. Thanks to the cursed
Northern climate, he was cut off in the very
act of sprinting into his 80th year.
„ The rich landlord is the true natriot.
Unlike the wicked workman who strikes
against the Government, he never tries to
take his country down for a bean. There was
a sample case of this in Central Queensland
the other day. Three acres were wanted
for railway purposes, and the owner de-
jnanded £4OO for the lot. The vile Govern-
ipent offered £6O. After argument the
owner said he would stand the loss and take
£2OO. The absurd Government still offered
£6O. Then the matter went to the Land
Court, which took evidence, and said that
£35 was plenty.
At last Rockhampton sees in front of it
a comprehensive water scheme. In the
years gone by it has drawn its juice (more
or less mixed with other stuffs not usually
sold by the gallon) from adjacent lagoons,
which had a habit of drying up when thev
were most wanted. Now Rockhampton
hopes to get its liquid from the Fitzroy
River some 20-odd miles up. The council
paid a hurried visit to Treasurer Barnes
the other day to ask him for a loan of a
trifle; to be exact, £125,000. The gentle-
man promised the necessary cash, where-
upon the deputation went out and bought
itself something out of a bottle. It reckoned
it might be disappointed over the water, so
it decided to make dead-sure of the whisky.
In the fight for places in the Reps, and
the Senate, Queensland Liberals have put
up three ex-Labor men —John Mann for
Herbert; David Hunter, for Oxley (the m&n
with the big voice and the not so big intel-
lect) ; and the ever-ready James Michael
Cross, for Maranoa, who is fighting a for-
lorn hope against Page. Cross has had four
tries to re-enter State politics, and he’ll have
lots of other chances if he’s hankering that
way. The Senate election is a straight-out
fight between _ Labor and Liberal, and if
misrepresentation of the Government’s legis-
lation counts for anything, Sayers, Chata-
way and St. Ledger will make a memorable
splash. So, when you come to think of it,
did Jonah when he went overboard. Pretty
nearly half the Mediterranean Sea flew up
when that Conservative nrophet fell in.
[Duncan J. Cameron, Thorn-street, Toowoomba.]
Archibald Meston, director of the Queens-
land Government Bureau in Sydney, got a
body-hold on a Townsville doctor named Nes-
had talked at great length in the
fe. M. Herald against N.Q. as a home for
white men. Meston lived in Queensland for
38 years, seven years of it in the North,
mostly m Cape York Peninsula, where the
wet season is certainly damp. Nesbit talked
wildly ot tropical diseases, and Meston asks
what these are, in view of the fact that
Queensland (next to Maoriland) has the
lowest death rate in the world. Cooktown
has a better climate, says Meston. than
Sydney or Melbourne ; and Frank Jardine, oL
Albany 1 ass, has lived 50 years in the
North, and is “still equal to throwing the
average .man over the fence.” The navvies
who built the Cairns railway did better
work, according to contractor John Robb,
than that done on the Victorian lines,
ihere have been fever epidemics on the
up of new country in Queensland,
and that fact applies to all new countries in
the world. Having finally stated with a
loud whoop of defiance that “Queensland
is a white man s country, and will stay so,”
Meston concludes with the belief that Nesbit
has been abolished.

Up North, the bookmaker is in for a cold
time, by the look of things. The other day,
at Townsville, a mob of him and allied
tradesmen were mustered before the Police
Magistrate and fined in varying amounts
from £7 to £lOO per Nose. The threat of
Rhadamanthus as to the altitude of the fine
next time Brian O’Galilee appears in the
worshipful presence was fairly stimulating.
Seeing there is a margin of £5OO to work on,
the Pencillers of Jericho have a chance to
get up arrears of thinking.
“Kobbi” : Something of a typhoid wave has
struck Western Bananaland just lately.
Winton, out towards the sunset, is having a
very bad time of it, with no less than 22
patients in the local hospital, which is a big
record for so small a township. The doctor
has advised “the padlocking of all rain-water
tanks, and the use of lime water for drinking
purposes.” The local bore water has some
considerable heat as it issues from the flow,
and no doubt the germs therein, being boiled
on the immense stove of Providence, are dead
and helpless. At Barcaldine, the other day,
typhoid got into holts with Roxby, one of
the popular Bush Brotherhood in those parts,
and unfortunately typhoid got the verdict.
As a consequence, the fighting Hulton Sams,
who anticipated packing his portmanteau for
England, has decided to unpack for another
year at least, and continue to hustle the
Gospel over the great empty wilderness.
“Good-natured—With Means.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Gentleman, no encumbrance, wishes to
meet healthy gentlewoman, 30 to 40. Good-natured—
with means.— Courier advt.
She need not be millionaire-rich,
She need not be goddess or witch,
She may not have hair like the raven.
Or features like Juno the Queen’s;
She may not be loaded with beauty,
Her voice may not strike you as fluty.
Provided you’re sure when you’ve got her
secure,
She’s soft and good-natured—with Means.
The man who has battled for long,
And buffeted hard in the throng,
Ts apt to care less for high colors,
And tone down the yellows and greens;
No longer he pines for a fairy,
In Polly or Milly or Mary,
Contented were he if his partner could be
J.ust kind and good-natured—with Means.
How potent a tamer is Time—
He'cools aspirations sublime,
The wheat from the straw how he threshes,
And tirelessly tests what he gleans ;
He says to us: “Brothers, he aisy,
The moon’s only meant for the crazy—
Yes —Prissy was pretty, and Winnie was
witty,
But Jane is good-natured—with Means.
And men who get wed to a goddess
May find in the bounds of her bodice
A temper that smacks of old Hades,
And not of Celestial Scenes;
And often I guess they’d surrender
Divinity fair and untender
For just a plain girl with a snub and a curl
Who could be good-natured—with Means.
For loveliness is but a flower
That passes and dies in an hour,
But one thing remaineth to comfort.
And that is—the Magic of Beans !
So, come along freckled-faced Hilda,
Or kindly but stodgy Matilda,
And lend us your hand for a little gold
band,
The meed of—Good-Nature and Means.
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, Mat 3, 1913.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
His Ex. and one gentleman fag are mean-
dering about somewhere near Camooweal.
where the local products are largely composed
of dust-storms, Afghans and camels. Lady
Mac. and the V T ision are perching peaceably
at home, but next week they will put in a
few days preening their feathers on the more
or less blue waters of the bay the name
whereof is Moreton.
Last Wednesday Blanche Philp swapped
her name for that of Henderson, with the
aid of two very pretty bridesmaids, a parson,
a bridegroom and a few other odd accessories.
The wedding dress was white satin, very
dainty, and so plainly cut as to refresh eyes
wearied by much trimming. The ’maids
(another Miss Philp, and a sister of the
groom) wore white, with girdles and hats of
pale blue, and showing cunning touches of
tangerine. Only relatives were at the break-
fast, which was eaten at the Philp residence
at Toowong. The Orsova afterwards con-
veyed the honeymooners south.
Benjamin returned to Brisbane on
Monday, leaving son Arthur working hard at
the London College of Music. She has
Ck ? b ? x , full of garments that are
pected to stand between the sun and the
local birds ot Paradise. The home at New
I arm was made into an almost new estab-
lishment by hubby while its mistress was
a ? d now, when she rises at dawn to
get the breakfast, she finds the porridge pot
in a quite unaccustomed place, and the soup
plates no longer hang on the same nails as
formerly. This comes of leaving a man
about the house with waste time on his
hands.

Professor Michie read a paner on “Hieh
land Superstitions” at the Women’s Pink
on Thursday. The club room £ abouV as
commodious as a hen-coop, and when an
audience has finally been extricated, after a
Stirling lecture, it is sometimes necessary to
give artificial respiration to those who were
m the centre of the pack. This has nothing
to do with Highland superstitions, but it
deserves much more ventilation.
Mrs. Downes, head-teacher of the Kan
garoo Point State Kindergarten, has had
several presentations handed in to cheer
her on her way to England. Weepful
parents pressed a necklet upon her, a de-
pressed staff used an inkstand, and the East
Moreton Teachers’ Association crowded in
with a brooch and a dressing-bag.
Two unfortunate children of five or six
who have been joined together from birth’
are being exhibited in this town. What
about the S.P.C.A. people, or don’t they
worry about anything unless it has four
legs?
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
The St. George Society, at its annual din-
ner at Ipswich (Q.), was roused to immense
enthusiasm by the reading of a telegram
from the parent society in the Cold Land, to
the effect that “England is watching!” The
idea that Britain stays awake to watch a
gorge in a second-rate Queensland town
delights Ipswich immensely.
The much-boasted benefits of the old Tory
method of settling people on the land, by
buying up private estates at a fancy figure,
and then re-selling to settlers at boom prices,
are coming home to roost. Jimbour settlers,
burdened to death by purchase engagements,
are going to deputationise the Minister for
easier terms. If they do this in the midst
of the present magnincent seasons, what
would they have done after a drought period?
“Kobbi”: The “Never Never” is a fast
diminishing quantity. It was the motor-car
that first started to wind in the outer edges.
Then the telephone happened along, and it
has roped together a mighty junk of Banana-
land’s back country. The latest move is to
link the ear of Winton to the voice of
Middleton, a brief stretch of 120 miles, or
half-way to Boulia. And Boulia is so near
the Edge that one holds his breath when he
looks out the back window. The drop is
awful.
The _St. George Society in Brisbane got
something of a shock the other day. At its
annual dinner it called upon Archbishop
Donaldson to propose the toast of “The
Army and Navy”—meaning, of course, the
Army and Navy of “Old England.” But
the Archbishop is a good Australian, and he
did the business in fine style by bidding
Australia increase its own regiments and
hustle along its own navy ; and St. George
gasoed as though it had met the dragon
again, as his Sensible Gaiters hurried along
to wish hearty success to both branches of
his own country’s Defence service.
North Queensland had a drink and opened
another meat-packing factory i few days ago
—at Biboohra, on the railway line that
wriggles out from Cairns to Chillagoe. Or,
rather, it turned the key afresh. For 10 or
12 years the late W. J. Munro owned the
premises and kept them carefully locked up,
because he reckoned they interfered with
his trade as a wholesale and retail dealer in
stock, dead and alive; but on his death ex-
politician C. A. Jenkinson buzzed round,
formed a co. to buy and run the works, and
northern cow-breeders begin to see a better
time ahead.

|y| ELBOURNE’S NEW CONCERT HALL.
THE AUDITORIUM,
Now nearing completion, has been erected at a cost
of over £40,000, and will be the first Concert Hall
proper in Australia.
Seating accommodation—24oo.
Comfortable tip-up chairs in Stalls.
Handsome Lounge Seats in Balcony.
Comfortable Gallery seating throughout.
The Auditorium is equipped with heating and cooling
apparatus, which cost over £3OOO, by which the
atmosphere in winter and summer will be kept to an
average temperature of 65 degrees.
Ample provision has been made for spacious en-
trances, exits, crush rooms, etc., all of which
will be heated in winter.
The Auditorium is suitable for Concerts of all
descriptions—Lectures, Meetings, Shows, Bazaars—
and other classes of amusements.
For vacant dates apply to
J. & N. TAIT, The Auditorium, Collins Street.

Those who are troubled with falling hair, dandruff,
baldness, itching scalp, etc., know full well the
humiliation that they cause. There is no need, how-
ever, of such conditions. These troubles can be
quickly overcome by my True Hair Grower.
I am not an advertising quack, but am a business
man. My hair began to grow thin about the time
I reached the age of thirty. When I had reached the
age of thirty-five the top of my head was quite bald.
I had considerable pride, and sought for something
to cause renewed growth. I invested in every pre-
paration that I could see or hear of, and here let me
say that not only did I try all the English so-called
restorers', but those of Continental Europe. I also
visited specialists. My experimenting cost me many
sovereigns and considerable time —yet the hairs came
not. I had given up the idea of ever growing hair
again. One afternoon, while enjoying a brief busi-
ness respite by an Alpine trip, I fell in with an old
collegiate chemist, whose hair was particularly plen-
tiful. I jokingly remarked that I wished he could
spare some of his hair for my benefit. This lead him
to tell me that he understood the human hair as well
as a good dentist understands teeth. He wrote out a
formula, and suggested that I have a quantity of the
compound prepared. I did this a few days later. 1
was astonished to see the hair coming forth after
several applications, and within six weeks my head
was covered by a firm, yet soft, black growth of hair
Since then the hair on my head has been as luxuriant
as when in my school days. Having arranged for
the legal right to do so, I have for some time been
privately selling this marvellous hair-growing com-
pound. I hold over one thousand letters from people
who have actually grown new hair through the
medium of my true hair grower. I do not pretend
to say it is the only hair grower in the world—l am
not interested in decrying others—but I do assert
that it is the most reliable, and in the end by far the
cheapest.

The Inky Way.
“Deadhead”: The dignity of the press is well
maintained by the suburban Archimedean lever, as
witness the following, from the Brunswick and
Coburg (Vic.) Star of April 18:
“Occasionally murmurs of dissatisfaction are heard
amongst members of local organisations and pro-
moters of local functions that their affairs are
ignored by the press; and the Fourth Estate unjustly
comes under the lash of criticism. There is another
side to the case, where those who get up these enter-
tainments omit to send the usual complimentary
press tickets. As far as we are concerned, no re-
porter for this paper , is going to explain his position
to a probably sceptical doorkeeper, and take the
chance of being thought mean. If a newspaper is
expected to devote space, time and money reporting
and publishing reports, it is about time amateur
public entrepreneurs learned the regular and cus-
tomary procedure. If, on the other hand, omissions
spring from the supposition that the press would go
and pay at the door, such entrepreneurs merely de-
lude themselves, as the failure to send press tickets
affords the press the best excuse in the world for
getting out of the expense of reporting the event.”
After which the “amateur public entrepreneur”
crawled into his burrow and died, and no one shed
a tear over his remains.
London Times came out on St. Patrick’s Day with
a 32-page Irish supplement. It was quite a departure
from the traditional violently anti-Irish tone and
attitude of the Times. But the old journalistic
steam-roller has got a new editor named Robertson,
and he evidently means to break away from the evil
traditions of the past. The change was emphasised
at the beginning of the first article, “Ireland and the
Empire,” in the Patrick’s Day supplement. There
a handsome apology was actually made for the old-
time animosity of the publication towards the Irish,
and by way of reparation a vigorous protest in verse
from one of the Young Ireland poets of 1848, against
the insults and calumnies of the Times of that period,
was printed in italics. It needed some moral courage
to do that. But assuredly an apology was called for,
considering that the Times, in one of its sober-
minded old moments, addressed O’Connell, the Lib-
erator, as
“Scum spewed out of Irish bog,
Ruffian, murderer and hog.”
Australia got a section of the “Ireland and Empire”
article in the shape of some matter drawn from J.
F. Hogan’s “History -of the Irish in Australia.”
Richard Bourke was described as the greatest of
Irish viceroys, and the inscription on his Sydney
statue was quoted. The Irish “political exiles,” or
transported rebels, were said to have developed into
“good colonists.” Irish literature, Irish drama,
Irish education, Irish manufactures, Irish folk lore,
Irish humor, and a lot of other Hibernian subjects
were treated.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin, —The newly-elected Sydney Uni-
versity Senate might well consider whether the big
academy should not give a little less prominence to
the teaching of dead Latin and the corpse of Greek,
and make a start with a chair of Living Asiatic
Languages. The future trade of this continent with
China and Japan will necessarily be very extensive,
but, more important is the fact that the Eastern
Asiatic will probably be Australia’s most delicate
diplomatic problem for all time. It is absolutely
necessary that our statesmen should keep a vigilant
eye on the development of the saffron proposition,
and how they are going to do this successfully if
they can’t personally analyse the printed utterances
of the Brown Cove this writer fails to see. Censored
though it may be, the Japanese press of to-day is by
no means an unreliable indication of the trend of
Japanese diplomacy. Neither is its share in the
development of Japan’s policy a negligible one. As
time goes on it is probable that the Jap press will
become enfranchised, and will be as powerful as, say,
the pan-German organs of to-day. At the present
day any educated Mongolian can (and many of them
probably do) read the unpatriotic blather of our
own Australia-hating ' newspapers, and realise the
immense help to be gained therefrom to the infant
nation’s undoing. But, to us, Japan is a closed
book; we can just as readily keep abreast of Japan’s
thought as we can of Tibetan. When we can under-
stand and study our neighbors our inertness will
probably vanish with a unanimity that may even
include Australia’s notoriously unpatriotic press.—•
Yours, etc., Vergniaud.
HYMN TO INK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Child of the worm, the oak tree, and the fire,
Naiad of dreams,
Whose black unhairpinned tresses are my lyre,
Making a crooked music of desire
In many streams
Of silent sound .
Flowing my much-corrected paper through,
Until the round
Of this big earth is eloquently drowned
In floods of you.
The worm that was thy father writhes along,
The 6low, base verbiage of the written line,
Eating the green and fluttered heart of song
As days eat mine.
The tree that was thy mother sways and thrills,
Drunk with clear sunshine, in the thing I write,
As when the soft winds to the purple hills
Murmur at night.
For me the flame that gave thee purpose glows,
Fanned by -the light touch of the errant thought—
When God goes fishing thus some star he knows
Burns to be caught.
The mill that made thy blackness into dust
Turns on for me through countless nights and
days—
The poet needs must grind his inky crust
Or eat his bays.
Thou art the pen-kissed bride of striving thought,
Meek as some holy, silence-pickled nun,
Subtle as light, or light’s best rainbow caught
In spring’s green branches when young dreams
are spun
Out of a happy fleece of showers and sun,
Facile to follow fancy, or to deck
Romance with that black blossom, hardly won —
The name that gives its lustre to the cheque.
The white gods, floating free
Down skies of Thessaly,
Live only now in thee;
The pageant of the kings
A spent, far radiance flings
’Mid old imaginings
Thou hast inshrined ;
From records of thy keeping
The bent, grey world is reaping
A harvest like the wind
Of ink-unfettered mind.
But thou, my pen and I,
Till all thy wells are dry
Must still conspire
To work Promethean-wise
Arch-treason to the skies
And steal their fire.
And if we hold and tame
With thy black bands that flame,
Shall Jones and Smith admire our dangerous
plunder ?
Or shall we get abuse
For plagiarising Zeus?
Who knows? I often wonder.
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy about it; we describe it fully, and explain
just how it acts. You use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same wav you use this sound magni-
fier to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
able. No one can tell you are wearing them. They
rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off them—the
strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What a
relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural drum.
Being of the softest rubber, it cannot hurt the ear.
It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort.
The division that runs
across the mouth is to al-
low the removal and inser-
tion of the Ear-Drum, and
to brace it.
The shape of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has been chosen
after years of experience as
the best adapt-
ed to carry the
faintest sound.
Of course, if
you have lost
the h e a r i ng-
nerve com-
pletely, or were
born deaf, we
cannot pretend _ _
to cure you. Only a miracle could
do that, in spite of some of the
absurd claims made to-day. But
of you can hear the faintest sound,
do not despair. You will never
regret trying the Wilson Ear-
Drums. We have letters to show
you from deaf people in Australia
and New Zealand, who have been

Leslie Burrell, a Christchurch Boy, cured by
Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery

2N-" %
LESLIE
BURRELL,
(from a photo).
“My little boy, four years of age,
was suffering from a very bad cold on
the chest, accompanied by a severe
croupy and chronic cough which would
always leave him exhausted,” writes
Mr. T. Burrell, Victoria Street,
Christchurch, N.Z. “During the at-
tack he would go black in the face,
causing us great worry and alarm.
We tried everything that was adver-
tised and recommended, but it was of
no use. Hearing about how Dr.
Sheldon’s New Discovery had cured a
similar case, I made inquiries and
purchased a bottle for a trial, with
the result that the coughing and
pains in his chest were immediately
relieved, and his health and appetite
were completely restored, and he is
to-day perfectly cured and in the
very best of health, for which I have
Dr. Sheldon’s New Discovery alone to
thank. I hope these few words of
gratitude may cause others in the
same state as my child to give it the
trial it honestly deserves.”

The Last Reserve.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
King Ferdinand and bis Ministers called out not
an army, but a nation. It was a clean sweep of
the nation’s manhood. In the last reserve were boys
of 16 and men past 60 years of age. No one was
spared from the roll-call. Professors and painters,
journalists, merchants, shopkeepers, shop-assistants,
schoolmasters and schoolboys, every kind and condi-
tion of Bulgarian, were summoned in that general
“mop-up” of the national strength. It was cruel,
hut it was also magnificent. For the call was an-
swered with a tremendous enthusiasm, and there
were no evasions, no deserters.
—Philip Gibbs, in the British Review (London).
SAY, what is the thing that wins in war
—is it merely iron and steel?
’Tis the passionate heart at the nation’s
core when the forth-riglit bugles peal 1
’Tis the spirit that leaps in the shop-man’s
breast, that stirs in the school-man’s
soul,
When the yardstick’s dropped and the lines
are dressed, as the drums of the nation
roll!
Professors and painters?— soldiers all! —
editors, smiths and clerks —
Ready and armed, are the short and tall,
when the Maxim splutters and barks!
For this is the Thing that counts in war—
’tis the leap of the nation’s nerve,
When the greybeards march by the hundred
score in the ranks of the Last Reserve !
When cannon have shattered the nation’s
best, and the trenches smoke with
blood,
Then the land must stand to the final test —
or the nation’s name is Mud!
When the shrapnel’s scythe from the dawn
has swung, and the human windrows
lie,
Then the land must answer with old and
young, or its soul must shrivel and die!
For this is the law that the nations all must
break at the cost of death —
Yea, the class must cease and the pen must
fall when the warning bugle saith !
Since the Thing that matters the most in
war, when the shell-flogged squadrons
swerve,
Is the valiant heart in the students’ corps
and the strength of the Last Reserve !
And what will Australia’s answer be, and
what will Australians do,
When the strong man sinks on a failing knee
and the colors are beaten too?
Say, how will the editors answer then, and
how will the shop-men stand?
Will the journalist drop the scissors and
pen for the rifle ready at hand?
Will the merchant dress in the self-same
line, with his dozen of invoice-clerks?
For the schoolboy’s ready at danger’s sign—
but what of these Ancient Narks?
Are the parsons ready, who cant and groan—
are their curates prepared to serve? _
Will each sidesman fight—will he hold his
own with the lads in the Last Reserve?
For this is the Question, after all, that
strikes to the heart of things—-
Will the nation answer the bugle’s call with
a spirit that throbs and swings?
Are the grey professors prepared for war,
as well as the boys at school;
Are the writers ready?—Their written lore
must shrivel when Maxims rule!
For the land that moves on an ordered plan
must call these writers in—
It must arm the poet and artisan, and
the priest and the parson’s kin !
It must base its strength on the Final Line
-—each arc of our manhood’s curve,
The young and the old —they must put their
spine in the strength of the Last Re-
serve !
For the Thing that wins in the time of war
goes down to the roots of faith—
’Tis the passionate heart at the nation’s core
that leaps when the bugle saith!
What spirit shall stir in the shop-man’s
breast, what strength in the schoolboy’s
soul ?
We shall need a church with a martial zest
when the drums of the nation roll!
Our bishops and curates? — -soldiers all! —-
cheering the lads and clerks—-
Ready to march when the bugles call and
the Maxim splutters and barks!
Is the Church prepared?—for its aid we need,
and the leap of the nation's nerve
Ts as strong as Australia's strongest creed —
for the Church is the Last Reserve!
VICTORIA
GRANT HERYEY.
The Professor.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I AM late, and there is quite a rush for
seats to witness the “Dedication” as
advertised. Indeed, our versatile
professor, clairvoyant healer, psycometrist,
thought reader and what not, is already
adding another facet to his gem-like collec-
tion of talents by acting as priest and nurse
to the weakly, hiccoughing infant who has
the star part for the evening. After wind-
ing up a lengthy vague address on
“Thoughts” and “The Child,” he dedicates
the babe, which, clasped against his roly-
poly form, he seems, till now, to have for-
gotten, to the cause of Spiritualistic Science.
“Beatrice Besant Brown” —he rolls the
alliteration delicately upon Ins tongue—-‘1
dedicate you to the Best in Life—to the
study of the Occult—to the advanced Spint-
u-lism of the present age.” Beatrice Besant
Brown, alarmed at this strenuous prospect,
wails publicly. He tries his mesmeric stare
upon her as a silencer. This stare is a. terri-
fying thing to meet at long range. Its imme-
diate result upon the babe at close quarters
is something resembling convulsions. Here
the mother would intervene, but she is
01 “Be calm, madam,” he says. )( “Your child
is now within psychic control.”
Certainly the infant raises her voice no
more in sorrow. Indifferent alike to several
“bright controls” which the High Priest sees
hovering near, or to the touching with
bolic flowers” which helps to pad out the
ceremony, she sleeps. , „ • *
“I prog-nos-ticate,” says the High Priest,
“that the Spirit of Intelligence will bless
this babe.” He glances ceilingwards
“Intelligence,” obedient to command, is flut-
tering about there. “Yes, a bright light is
dawning over my head, he affirms. -the
be-yutiful Spirit of Intelligence is float-ing
down—down.” His eye follows the descent
and rests at length upon the piano-stool.
‘‘lntelligence, bearing beside her bright light,
her symbol, which, as those of you who
study the occult under my guidance, are
aware, is a scroll. She breathes into this
child a shower of Good Thoughts, which,
lying in her sub-conscious mind, will one
day send forth a harvest of Good Deeds. In-
telligence prognosticates for her a life of
Good —Good —Good.”
His voice, risen almost to a scream, is now
melodramatically dropped a few octaves as
with mesmeric glare he says: “The Aura of
this child shows benevolent rays, bloo and
voylet.”
Soon after he begins to read our “Auras.”
“You can’t take me in,” he remarks, “no
matter how you impose on the man in the
street. Outlining each one of you is the
Aura —the color of which proclaims you
either white-teaded boy or the bad girl of
the family.”
At this unexpected gleam of comedy we
break into laughter. The leader must be a
humane seer. He will not divulge too much
and damage our reputations. Quite the con-
trary. For, by the time he is through with
us we mostly have “swelled head.” In his
delineations we figure as heroic characters.
Even our dubious points are endearing—-
"generous to a fault,” “rashly daring,” “no
one’s enemy but our own,” and the like!
Most of us, too, are “fraught with clairvoy-
ance,” only needing the tutelage of our flat-
terer to develop into first-class psychics and
qualify to “demonstrate,” “function,” and
“go in for reading of the hair.” The only
wonder is that such fine characters as we
should find so little appreciation in our own
homes.
After a silver offering has tinkled into
little glass plates and been borne away by
students of the Occult, the Professor is ob-
served to be in semi-trance. Wedged into
an armchair and muttering stagily, his mag-
netic eye is dulled, yet observant.
“What’s wrong?” I inquire of my next
chair neighbor. “What’s he muttering
about? Is it indigestion or acute dandruff?”
With a pitying glance for my crass ignor-
ance she replies, “He’s en rapport with his
Guide. He’ll be himself again presently.
Then he’ll demonstrate. You here for a
message ?”
“In quest of Truth,” I answer. “Perhaps
a grain of it may lurk even among the
cranks”
“He’s no crank” —indicating the Profes-
sor. “He’s genu-wine. _ Gets marvellous re-
sults. Mater-ialised his Guide once —saw
him do it —a black gentleman he is. Ma-
’ommedan.” I make groans of astonishment
and raise my eyebrows till they disappear in
my hair.
“Yes, indeed,” she resumes. “And won’er-
ful cures by suggestion. Cured my daughter
of depleted nerves when all the doctors
failed.” Again I gnash my teeth and strike
an attitude of amazement. “Spent a for-
tune on doctors for ’er, I ’ad.”
“Apropos of cures,” I whisper, “I am suf-
fering from a depleted purse. Would he try
the suggestion cure on that?” She rebukes
me, base materialist that I am. “Hush
h’sh,” she warns. “You mustn’t joke here.
The spirits don’t like it. I believe there was
a joke made here one night, an’ all the spirits
up and left.”
“What! Touchy as all that? The next
world must be even more unpleasant than I
thought. Some chestnut, perhaps”
An angry hissing from the seats of the
elect silences me.
“He’s starting,” whispers my friend. He
was. He was on his legs, his glittering iri-
descent eye seeming to revolve like a Cather-
ine wheel. He looks at some critics near the
door.
“I am con-scious, friends, of an un-sympa-
tlietic. influence. Some material-istic soul
here is put-ting obstacles in the way of
spirit com-mun-ication. Let us all put good
thoughts in-to the e-vil soul.”
We all do, but they don’t act. For one of
the cavillers, baser than his fellows, and
having no sense of fitness, suddenly sees a
joke that was told him yesterday, and re-
lates it to his evil neighbor, who explodes
into a muffled guffaw.
“Friend, as you are an ass, you had better
make a donkey-slide of the staircase. This
is not a circus,” says the Professor. And
the ass, gathering up his hat and his bray,
goes forth in disgrace. Instantly we feel a
purer, rarer quality in the air, and the Pro-
fessor sees in the aisle a bright spirit.
“A beautiful spirit form, from whom em-
an-ates beams of b-100 and amber. Passed
out recently, she tells me”
Here he interrupts himself to adjust his
cough. “Wo-hoo? Wo-hoo?” He sounds
like an old and faithful hound I once had
that suffered from asthma. “Wo-hoo! Wo-
hoo !” he barks. “She has forgotten the
name she bore while on the earth plane. I
will de-scribe her more fully. She is bright—
—all bright. Fair blue eyes, fair hair” —
“Why does he not say ‘blonde,’ and be done
with it?” I whisper tartly. “How is my
depleted purse to be restored to solvency if
he wastes words like this?”
“Wo-hoo.” (Oh, that he would, by sug
gestion or eucalyptus, cure his cough.) “In
fact, I might describe her as a blonde,” so at
last we come to the point. “A radiant
spirit, this, slightly built, illumined with
soft rays, bearing the light of Wisdom. She
wants _ communi-ca-shun with someone in
the third row. Does anyone claim her?”
The entire row claims her. Yet can she
belong to all? If she is daughter to the
blue-bloused woman at the end, is she also
niece to the red-nosed man by the wall? It
is all very puzzling. Why has she not
brought her card with her? There is some
scuffling and perplexity among the claim-
ants, but the Seer, equal to these emergencies,
gives an explanation which quite contra-
dicts his former remarks about the visitant.
“She tells me, friends, that she is the
Spirit of Universal Sisterhood. That is
how we all recognise and reach out to her.”
We accept the lie tamely, and are intro-
duced to a “stoutish. darkish, baldish” spirit
who blocks the aisle. Nobody recognises
him even when a “jocular manner” is added
as an identifiable detail. Perhaps the
reason is that, by a remarkable oversight,
the Professor has forgotten to equip him
with a “bright light.” How can we claim
a dark spirit—it would look so odd? The
Professor’s is the blame, yet he irritably
throws slurs on our intelligence.
“Is there anyone here who understands
what I’m talking about —who recognises
this stoutish spirit? Wo-hoo! Wo-hoo!”
Stay! A thought arrests me. There was

COUGH
a gas-rate collector I once knew, with a bad
habit of jocularity. 1 often wished him
dead on account of it. Perhaps this shade
is his.
rut to the chair, so to speak, this very
person it turns out to be. My collector was
tall and hairy and bony, but I take the
shade, with all discrepancies; to my bosom,
and it shows its gratitude by “passing its
hands lovingly over my face” and “breaking
over me a bright beam of light.” Evidently
he js still in the gas business, as witness the
switching on of his hidden illuminant.
But now the Professor wishes all the
Shades “good-night,” and starts curing us
by suggestion. “Anyone needing a curing
current hold up the hand,” we are com-
manded. Most of us do. Among such a
crop of hands even a healer may blunder.
That is why an old man, greatly daring,
disputes a diagnosis. “Twasn’t my hand
you read, sir; I haven’t got a floating kid-
ney. My trouble is chronic poomoanyer.”
“That is what I implied,” shrieks the
Healer. He ignores the mistaken hand’s
accusations. “You had pneumonia. Now
you have it no more; I transmute it for
you.” “Transmute” is a good word. The
caviller goes down before it baffled. Mean-
while, the Healer rushes things. He stares
mesmerically, raises his plump white hands
to a clapping position, and points to a hand,
saying in a loud, threatening voice: “I
affirm that the pain in your back and legs
wall now leave you.” He claps thrice. “One,
two, three ! Gone ! Next!”
He takes on “Next,” saying, as he again
prepares to clap: “I affirm that the dull,
don’t-care, full-up feeling you’ve had ever
since Cup Day will now leave you.” Clap-
clap-clap ! The Healer carries all before
him, curing us now in batches. His “One.
two, three, next,” is hurrying us to the end
of the programme and the last trams. Does
he diagnose correctly? No man knowetli.
We seem to be trodden under foot in the
rush of the hoofs of the prophet.
“It’s not you that’s sick,” he tells us
later, “not the real you, but your old bod-
dies. Concentrate, cast out the e-vil thought
of pain and you are instant-ly well, well;
well.”
It is on buoyant legs, therefore, and carry-
ing our old “bod-dies” at a rejuvenated tilt,
that we at last leave a depleted and wrung-
out Healer to a consideration of the takings,
while we sprint like the very devil for the
last tram.
WEEROONA.
On the Midnight Slow Express.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I RECENTLY stated in The Bulletin
that as a result of much scientific re-
search I had discovered that the easi-
est way for the beer-dazzled Sydney subur-
banite who lives near the railway to reach
his home safely, and within a reasonable
time, was to wait for the midnight train,
a weary caravan upon which they carry
drunks and discharge them at the proper
place of delivery. Since then I have re-
searched some more, and I want to add that
it is no use seeking safety on the drunks’
train unless you really are drunk. The
sober citizen seems to be regarded as a tres-
passer on the midnight vehicle, and if he
should happen, by the sheer weariness of the
long stoppages on the journey, to drop to
sleep, he may sleep until the train gets to
Parramatta or the last chapter of the Book
of Revelation, but no railway servant will
lift a hand or a foot to assist him, any more
than they would on a common train which
started at 5 p.m.
I, of course, am the sober citizen, and it
seems to me that I have done more unneces-
sary travelling within the last six months
than is good for any man. Wearied out
with business —in a bottle or otherwise —I
may happen to doze, and if I do, I am bound
to wake up at Parramatta, or Clyde, or
Granville, or Penrith, or some other old
Methuselah of a place where there is no
train back till morning, and the pubs are
all shut, and it is cold and dismal, and the
station officials are, sometimes, hard-hearted.
On one occasion I woke up at Penrith, and
a ticket collector looked at me severely, and
spoke about breaches of regulations, and col-
lected Is. 7d. excess fare just so that I
wouldn’t have the pleasure of experiencing
several hours’ unnecessary travelling, and
missing my dinner and an appointment for
the theatre with a lady who might be
annoyed, for nothing. When I did get a
train back, another collector looked at me
severely and collected another Is. 7d. I am
fond of travelling, but travelling beyond Bur-
wood, when I am anxious to get home
within, at all events, two or three hours of
the time I arranged to be there, is not a
pleasant thing to be charged for.
The first time I woke up at Granville,
somewhere about the hour of nil a.in., I
decided to walk home, about eight miles. I
did it, too, after meeting a lady and gentle-
man who wanted to know if it was 10 o’clock
yet, and a wandering spirit outside Rook-
wood cemetery, which proved to be a mile-
stone or something else equally hard, and a
young policeman with very old suspicions.
Auburn or Clyde are convenient places, I
have found, at which to wake up. There
are goods trains leaving the adjacent Clyde
yards at various times during the night, and
reaching my suburb at various times during
next month. I once walked home, also,
from Flemington, thinking it was fairly handy
to my suburb, and after losing myself in the
bush I met a bull which was wearing my hat
when I left it. •
But the most persistent travelling I have
experienced on the suburban railways hap-
pened early this year. I had caught my train
for home shortly after six, and when I woke
up at Granville, I found another train wait-
ing to bring me back again. I must have
mislaid my station again on the way back,
however, for I surprised myself on the Syd-
ney station engaged in a heated argument
with an individual who, for some reason or
another, wanted to tell me that he was
worth £5OOO, and whom, for some reason or
another, I desired to inform that he didn’t
own a cent. When I woke up at Lindfield, I
found I had mislaid my crayfish, so I went
into the Sydney train to look for it, and
woke up at Milson’s Point. I crossed the
ferry, secured another crayfish, had a few
drinks to sustain my failing strength, and
determined upon a fresh start. This time I
woke up at Parramatta, and discovered that
the train I was on was the first train back
in the morning. It was an “all-stops,” and
would undoubtedly have got me home if I
hadn’t wakened up at the Sydney station
again. I reckoned that there was a con-
spiracy amongst the trains to prevent me
getting home after that, so I didn’t try again.
I had spent about 12 hours—let alone two
crayfish—in an endeavor to travel seven
miles or so, and the amount of coin the rail-
way officials who wouldn’t deliver me to the
proper address charged me for the pleasure
of travelling to places I didn’t want to see
would have kept me in picture shows for a
year.
HAMER.
A Tale of Wet Endeavor.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was the middle of the night
When first we bumped upon the
rock,
The big ship shivered with affright,
The loud seas rose our hones to mock,
And everybody then on deck
Made some remarks about a wreck.
The thing they said was justified
By observations shortly made;
A hole was in the vessel's side
Through which a spouting whale might
wade,
And smaller fishes very soon
Were waltzing in the first saloon.
The girl was beautiful to see,
Her hair like funnel smoke outblown,
Her lips like some red shell that three
Tall waves with foam have shoreward
thrown ;
Her cabin luggage all was gone
And she had very little on.
The situation was not nice,
For we were thirty leagues from land,
all the life-boats in a trice
Were smashed as soon as they were
manned.
Viewing the circumstances round
It seemed we must be shortly drowned.
It seemed a drowning I must share
At midnight in the angry wave,
With that sweet dimpled maid whose hair
Seemed worth a large attempt to save;
But the stern fact was written clear
That we were lost and death was near.
I reallv did not know her name,
We had not then been introduced,
But in that time of wrath and blame
The social bonds were somewhat loosed ;
We felt we could dispense with form
Amid the tumult and the storm.
I took her softly in my arms.
The. wild sea round us seethed and hissed.
The night was full of fierce alarms
And none observed us where we kissed :
We knew we must go under soon,
Yet paused a moment there to spoon.
The situation was absurd,
As everyone must freely own.
We had not said a single word.
Yet now I live for her alone;
The waves our hot love tried to splash.
And all the decks were soon awash.
The ship heeled over with a lurch,
And we were in the angry sea—
The prayers they always say in church
Were small avail to her and me—
And yet she swam about a mile,
And let me kiss her for a while.
Our plight was really rather bad ;
A hen-coop might have saved us then,
But there was nothing to be had
Except a glimpse of dying men.
And over us the seas a-roll,
Seemed washing out her gentle soul.
I said. Dear love, we needs must part.
The angry ocean claims its own,
You really have the nicest heart
Of any girl that I have known.”
And as upon the wave she rose,
I kissed her softly on the nose.
She said, “This parting gives me pain,
But as there is no sort of hope,
Just trv to reach me once again
Before my senses all elope,
For drowning is a kind of bliss
If soothed and softened by a kiss.”
I kissed her on the mouth and brow,
And then a large wave loud and cold
Elimbed up above us like a cow,
And, moo-ing wildly, o’er us rolled
And as its wet weight pushed me down
I knew the time had come to drown.
■Vnd this is where the tale grows sad.
1 drowned as I expected to,
Because no air was to be had
And there was nothing else to do:
But that sweet maid so lately kissed
Amid the wet I somehow missed.
Here, lying on the cold, dank weed,
Mow dreams my fancies set astir,
And all the world of life and deed
T \. e . e ? s , t 0 its limit filled with her;
I think she rode that cow-wave tall
And did not really drown at all.
I dream that sunlight gilds the sea.
And up and down the waves she goes.
'at s °me bold merman swimming free
May pinch her beauty on the toes;
1 see her float like smiling sin
With two sweet dimples on her chin.
In daylong frolic in the wet
She holds a dolphin by the tail,
And when the merry sun has set
Mm sleeps beneath the moonlight pale
Dr mid the laughing star-beams fair
Bets small green fishes comb her hair.
And I am dead as Jonah’s whale.
_Aml she’s alive and bright and well,
Which makes the very saddest tale
ihat any ocean thing can tell;
For I, by death, was taken down’
Because my lady would not drown.
She would not drown, she will not come
Her red lips I shall taste no more- ’
1 erhaps she yields them now to some
Dry .man who brought her to the shore.
But at the bottom of the sea
The small, blue crabs crawl over me.
N.S.W.
PAT O'MAORI.
